The Four Doctrines on the Composition of Bodies
Enumerates the four views on body composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four major philosophical views on the composition of bodies and their key differences.

Core Idea
Four views, one body: are its parts finite, infinite, or none at all?

This passage presents a concise survey of the four possible positions on how a body is composed. After explaining the first view (finite indivisible parts) in the previous section, the text now lists the remaining three, attributed to different schools and thinkers. Understanding these views is essential for grasping later arguments about causality and divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. As you read, note that the text lists four distinct views, each introduced with 'As for the...'. Identify the core claim of each view.
The first view is briefly restated; it was explained in the previous lesson.
2
Note the proponents of each view
Pay attention to which thinker or school is associated with each view. For example, the second view is attributed to al-Nazzam and some ancient philosophers.
The fourth view is uniquely attributed to al-Shahrastani.
3
Compare the third and fourth views
Both views describe a 'simple body' that is one in itself and in sensation. The difference lies in the nature of its divisibility: the third view says divisions are infinite (potential), while the fourth says they are finite.
This distinction is crucial for theological debates about the nature of creation.
4
Reflect on why these distinctions matter
Ask yourself: why would a theologian or philosopher care about whether a body is composed of finite or infinite parts? How might these views relate to arguments for the existence of God or the nature of causality?
Later lessons will build on these concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا هو شرح مذهب القائلين بكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء متناهية ، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة البتة . وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الجسم مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل ، فهذا هو مذهب النظام ، من المعتزلة ، وهو أيضاً : منسوب إلى جمع من قدماء الفلاسفة . وأما الوجه الثالث : وهو أن يقال : الجسم البسيط : واحد في نفسه ، كما أنه واحد عند الحس، إلا أنه مع كونه واحداً ، قابل لانقسامات لا نهاية لها . وهذا رأي جمهور الفلاسفة . وأما الوجه الرابع : وهو أن يقال : الجسم البسيط : شيء واحد في نفسه ، كما أنه واحد عند الحس، إلا أنه قابل لانقسامات متناهية . وهذا القول لم يقل به أحد إلا محمد الشهرستاني في الكتاب الذي سماه بـ المناهج والبيانات.
This is the explanation of the doctrine of those who say that the body is composed of finite parts, each one of which does not admit of division at all. As for the second view: it is to say that the body is composed of actually infinite parts. This is the doctrine of al-Nazzam, of the Mu'tazila, and it is also attributed to a group of the ancient philosophers. As for the third view: it is to say that the simple body is one in itself, just as it is one in sensation, but despite being one, it is receptive to divisions with no end. This is the opinion of the majority of philosophers. As for the fourth view: it is to say that the simple body is one thing in itself, just as it is one in sensation, but it is receptive to finite divisions. No one has held this view except Muhammad al-Shahrastani, in the book he called al-Manahij wa al-Bayanat.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four possible views on the composition of bodies. It first restates the first view: the body is composed of finite indivisible parts (the atomist view). Then it presents the second view, attributed to al-Nazzam of the Mu'tazila and some ancient philosophers: the body is composed of actually infinite parts (i.e., infinite in actuality). The third view, held by the majority of philosophers (falasifa), is that the simple body is one in itself and in sensation, but is receptive to infinitely many potential divisions. The fourth view, unique to Muhammad al-Shahrastani, is that the simple body is one and receptive to finite divisions only. Note that the first view is the one explained in the previous passage; here it is summarized as the first of four possibilities.

Key Terms
جسم
أجزاء متناهية
أجزاء غير متناهية
جسم بسيط
انقسامات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the third view (infinite potential divisions) is the same as the second (actual infinite parts).
Correction: They differ: the second asserts actual infinite parts, while the third asserts a simple unity with potential infinite divisibility.

Checkpoint
Which view is uniquely attributed to Muhammad al-Shahrastani?
A) The body is composed of finite indivisible parts.
B) The body is composed of actually infinite parts.
C) The simple body is receptive to infinite divisions.
D) The simple body is receptive to finite divisions.

Key Takeaway
The four doctrines cover a spectrum from atomism to infinite divisibility, with al-Shahrastani's finite divisibility being unique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage presents four views on body composition without definitively endorsing one. When you encounter multiple valid perspectives on a theological or philosophical issue, do you feel anxious for a single answer, or can you tolerate ambiguity? What does that reveal about your trust in God's wisdom and your intellectual humility?
?
Al-Shahrastani held a unique view that no one else shared. Reflect on a time you held a minority opinion or conviction. Did you hold it with courage or fear? How does this example challenge you to seek truth even if it isolates you?
?
Scholars debated the nature of physical reality without empirical evidence. How often do you form strong opinions about matters where you lack complete knowledge? Are you willing to admit uncertainty and remain open to correction?

The Philosophers' Three Causes of Actual Division in a Body
Razi reports and analyzes the three causes of body division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causes of actual division in a body according to the philosophers, and how all three ultimately reduce to the difference of accidents.

Core Idea
Division becomes actual only when some accident distinguishes parts.

This passage from Imam al-Razi reports the philosophers' agreement that a simple body is potentially divisible, but actual division requires a specific cause. Razi enumerates three such causes—cutting, difference of accidents, and estimation—then traces them back to a single principle: the difference of accidents. Understanding this classification is key to grasping the philosophical theory of composition and division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: an opening agreement, a list of three causes, and a closing investigation.
Pay attention to the terms for potentiality (قوة) and actuality (فعل).
2
Identify the three causes
Find and list the three causes of actual division: cutting, difference of accidents (real or relational), and estimation with indication.
Consider how each cause introduces a distinction between parts.
3
Follow the unifying argument
Trace Razi's investigation: he shows that in each cause, the underlying reason is that the two parts have different accidents (spatial location, attribute, or mental indication).
The conclusion is that multiplicity always stems from difference of accidents.
4
Reflect on the philosophical implication
Think about why this matters: if a body had no accidental difference, it would remain one undivided entity.
This connects to later discussions on the nature of matter and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الفلاسفة اتفقوا على أن الجسم البسيط في نفسه شيء واحد ، كما أنه عند الحس شيء واحد . واتفقوا على أنه مع ذلك قابل للانقسامات . واتفقوا على أن ذلك الانقسام لا يخرج من القوة إلى الفعل إلا لأحد أمور ثلاثة : الأول : القطع والتفكيك . والثاني : اختلاف عرضين . إما عرضين حقيقيين . كما في الأبلق وإما عرضين إضافيين، كاختلاف محاذاتين أو مماستين . والثالث : الوهم والإشارة . وذلك هو أن يشير الإنسان إلى أحد طرفي الجسم ، دون الثاني فلسبب حصول الامتياز في هذه الإشارة يتميز أحد طرفي ذلك الجسم عن الثاني امتيازا بالفعل ثم ههنا بحث . وهو أنه يشبه أن يقال : السبب المقتضى لوقوع الكثرة بالفعل . على قول الفلاسفة هو اختصاص كل واحد من قسمي الجسم بعرض ، لا يوجد في القسم الآخر منه . فأما عند التقطيع والتفكيك ، السبب في حصول الكثرة ، حصول كل واحد من هذين القسمين ، في حيز غير الحيز ، الذي حصل فيه الآخر. وأما عند اختلاف الأعراض والصفات ، فالأمر فيه ظاهر . وأما عند التقسيم بالوهم ، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك . لأن الإشارة إنما اقتضت وقوع الامتياز في المشار إليه ، لأجل أن أحد جانبي الجسم : عرض له وصف كونه مشاراً إليه . فهذه الإشارة الخاصة ، والجانب الثاني ، لم تتعلق به هذه الإشارة ، بل إشارة أخرى. وكون الشيء المشار إليه : من الأعراض الإضافية . فثبت بهذا : أن على مذهبهم ، الموجب لحصول الكثرة في الجسم : اختلاف الأعراض .
When you have understood this, we say: The philosophers agreed that the simple body is in itself one thing, just as it is one thing in perception. And they agreed that despite that it is susceptible to divisions. And they agreed that this division does not emerge from potentiality to actuality except by one of three things: First: cutting and fragmentation. Second: difference of two accidents – either real accidents, as in a piebald [thing], or relational accidents, like difference of two oppositions or contacts. Third: estimation and indication. That is that a person points to one side of the body, not the other, and due to the occurrence of distinction in this indication, one side of that body becomes distinct from the second actual distinction. Then here is an investigation. That is: it resembles saying: the cause necessitating the occurrence of multiplicity in actuality, according to the philosophers, is the specification of each of the two parts of the body by an accident not found in the other part of it. As for in cutting and fragmentation, the cause of multiplicity is the occurrence of each of these two parts in a location other than the location in which the other occurred. As for in difference of accidents and attributes, the matter is apparent. As for in division by estimation, the matter is also so, because the indication only demands the occurrence of distinction in the indicated thing because one side of the body is characterized by the attribute of being indicated. So this specific indication [applies to one side], and the second side is not attached to this indication, but to another indication. And that the indicated thing is among relational accidents. So it is established by this: that according to their school, the cause necessitating multiplicity in the body is the difference of accidents.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the philosophers' position on the division of bodies. It begins by stating that philosophers agree a simple body is one in itself and in perception, yet divisible potentially. The division only becomes actual (from potentiality to actuality) by three causes. The first is physical cutting or fragmentation. The second is difference of accidents: either real accidents like colors (e.g., a piebald animal) or relational accidents like different orientations or contacts. The third is estimation and mental pointing: when a person mentally points to one side of the body, a distinction arises. Then a further investigation examines the underlying cause: the cause of multiplicity is that each part has an accident not present in the other. In cutting, it's location difference; in accidents, it's obvious; in estimation, it's the relational accident of being indicated. Thus the conclusion is that, on the philosophers' view, multiplicity is caused by difference in accidents.

عرض حقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a simple body divides by itself or that division is inherent in its nature.
Correction: The passage clarifies that division requires an external or accidental cause; a body remains one as long as no accidental distinction arises.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the common cause underlying all three modes of actual division?
A) The body's desire to divide.
B) The difference of accidents between parts.
C) The power of imagination.
D) The presence of void.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers hold that multiplicity in a body always arises from the difference of accidents, not from the body's essence alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally divide people into categories (rich/poor, educated/ignorant), are you aware that these are accidental distinctions, not essential ones? How can you train yourself to look beyond such accidents to the essence of a person?
?
This lesson shows that even a simple thought can create real distinctions. Think of a recent judgment you made about someone based on a single observation. Will you now reconsider that judgment, knowing it might be based on a mere accident?
?
The philosophers say division only becomes actual through accidents. In your own life, do you allow accidents—like status, appearance, or location—to create unnecessary divisions between you and others? Commit to one concrete action today that bridges such a division.

The Three Causes of Actual Division in Bodies
Enumerates the three causes that actualize potential division in a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causes that actualize potential division in a body and how they differ.

Core Idea
Potential division becomes actual only through an external cause: cutting, attribute difference, or mental estimation.

Philosophers agree that every body is potentially divisible, but actual division requires a trigger. This passage enumerates three distinct causes—physical, attributive, and mental—that actualize this potential. Understanding these causes helps clarify how we perceive and distinguish parts of a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three enumerated causes.
The passage is compact; each cause is introduced with a clear signal.
2
Identify each cause
List the three causes: cutting, difference of attributes (real or relative), and estimation/indication.
Note the subdivision of the second cause into real and relative attributes.
3
Reflect on the 'potential to actual' framework
Consider why the passage emphasizes that division does not become actual without one of these causes. This highlights a key philosophical principle.
4
Apply the causes to examples
Think of a piebald animal (real attributes) or pointing to one side of a table (estimation) as examples.
These examples help solidify the abstract concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قابل للانقسامات. واتفقوا على أن ذلك الانقسام لا يخرج من القوة إلى الفعل إلا لأحد أمور ثلاثة: الأول: القطع والتفكيك. والثاني: اختلاف عرضين. إما عرضين حقيقيين كما في الأبلق، وإما عرضين إضافيين كاختلاف محاذاتين أو مماستين. والثالث: الوهم والإشارة. وذلك هو أن يشير الإنسان إلى أحد طرفي الجسم دون الثاني، فلسبب حصول الامتياز في هذه الإشارة يتميز أحد طرفي ذلك الجسم عن الثاني امتيازا بالفعل.
It is divisible. And they agreed that that division does not go from potentiality to actuality except by one of three matters: First: cutting and separation. Second: difference of two attributes, either real attributes as in the piebald (black and white), or relative attributes like difference of two facing points or two points of contact. Third: estimation and indication. That is that a person points to one of the two ends of the body and not the second, and due to the occurrence of distinction in this indication, one end of that body becomes actually distinguished from the second.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage classifies the causes that actualize the potential division of a body. It begins by stating that every body is divisible, but the transition from potential to actual division requires one of three factors. The first is physical cutting and separation. The second is the difference of attributes: either real attributes inherent in the body itself, such as black and white in a piebald animal, or relative attributes like two different facing directions or points of contact. The third is mental estimation and pointing: when a person points to one side of a body and not the other, the mind distinguishes that side, producing an actual distinction. The author emphasizes that the distinction occurs in actuality due to the indication.

Key Terms
القطع والتفكيك
اختلاف عرضين
الوهم والإشارة
القوة والفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That potential division alone suffices for actual division; an external cause is not required.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that division does not go from potentiality to actuality except by one of the three causes, showing that an external trigger is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three causes that actualize division?
A) Cutting and separation
B) Difference of attributes
C) Time and motion
D) Estimation and indication

Key Takeaway
Actual division in a body requires one of three triggers: physical cutting, attribute difference, or mental estimation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring conflict in your life where you have mentally divided yourself or others into opposing sides. Which of these three causes—physical separation, attribute differences, or your own mental estimation—is at the root of that division? Are you willing to see that your own pointing and labeling might be the actual cause?
?
You constantly make distinctions in your mind: 'my past vs. my present,' 'my strength vs. my weakness.' According to this lesson, such mental divisions (وهم وإشارة) are real causes of actual separation. How does knowing that your thoughts create actual distinctions change the responsibility you feel for the divisions you hold?
?
The second cause of division is difference of attributes, whether real or relative. Consider a relationship where you focus on differences of opinion or personality. Are those differences 'real attributes' inherent in you both, or are they 'relative attributes' that depend on your perspective? What would it take to remove the division by changing how you relate rather than by cutting?

Discussion: The True Cause of Multiplicity in Bodies
Argues that multiplicity always results from difference of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author unifies the three apparent causes of multiplicity in a body under the single principle of difference of attributes.

Core Idea
All multiplicity in bodies stems from difference of attributes—even cutting and estimation.

The author examines three ways multiplicity arises in a body: cutting, differing attributes, and mental division. He shows that in every case, the real cause is a difference in attributes—either physical or relational. This reveals the underlying logical coherence of the philosophers' position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author presents a thesis and then supports it with three cases.
The passage uses a 'thesis–cases–conclusion' structure.
2
Trace the three cases of multiplicity
Identify the three cases: cutting, difference of attributes, and estimation. For each, note how the author explains that the cause is ultimately a difference in attributes—real or relative.
Cutting leads to different locations (attribute of place); estimation leads to different indications (relative attribute).
3
Understand the unifying conclusion
The conclusion is that in all cases, the cause of multiplicity is difference of attributes. Ask yourself: Why does this matter? It shows that the philosophers' account is internally consistent.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا بحث. وهو أنه يشبه أن يقال: السبب المقتضى لوقوع الكثرة بالفعل على قول الفلاسفة هو اختصاص كل واحد من قسمي الجسم بعرض لا يوجد في القسم الآخر منه. فأما عند التقطيع والتفكيك، السبب في حصول الكثرة حصول كل واحد من هذين القسمين في حيز غير الحيز الذي حصل فيه الآخر. وأما عند اختلاف الأعراض والصفات، فالأمر فيه ظاهر. وأما عند التقسيم بالوهم، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك. لأن الإشارة إنما اقتضت وقوع الامتياز في المشار إليه، لأجل أن أحد جانبي الجسم عرض له وصف كونه مشاراً إليه. فهذه الإشارة الخاصة، والجانب الثاني لم تتعلق به هذه الإشارة بل إشارة أخرى. وكون الشيء المشار إليه من الأعراض الإضافية. فثبت بهذا: أن على مذهبهم، الموجب لحصول الكثرة في الجسم: اختلاف الأعراض.
Then here is a discussion. It is that it may be said: The cause necessitating the occurrence of multiplicity in act, according to the philosophers, is that each of the two parts of the body is distinguished by an attribute not found in the other part. As for cutting and separation, the cause of the occurrence of multiplicity is that each of these two parts occurs in a region different from the region in which the other occurs. As for difference of attributes and qualities, the matter is apparent. As for division by estimation, the matter is also likewise. Because the indication only necessitated the occurrence of distinction in the indicated thing, due to the fact that one side of the body acquired the description of being indicated. So this particular indication, and the second side is not attached to this indication but to another indication. And the fact that the indicated thing is among relative attributes. So it is established by this: that according to their doctrine, the cause necessitating the occurrence of multiplicity in the body is difference of attributes.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer or rather a reasoning that reduces all three causes to one underlying principle: difference of attributes. It begins by stating the thesis: the real cause of multiplicity in a body, per the philosophers, is that each part of the body has a distinguishing attribute not present in the other part. Then it examines each of the three causes. For cutting, the two parts end up in different spatial regions, which is effectively a difference of location—a relative attribute. For difference of attributes (the second cause), it is obvious. For estimation, pointing to one side gives it the attribute of 'indicated', while the other side lacks that attribute; being indicated is a relative attribute. Therefore, the conclusion is that in all three cases, the cause of multiplicity is ultimately difference of attributes (either real or relative). The author thus shows the underlying unity of the causes.

Key Terms
الكثرة
اختصاص بعرض
الأعراض الإضافية
الإشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the three causes of multiplicity (cutting, attributes, estimation) are independent and irreducible.
Correction: The author shows that all three reduce to a single principle: difference of attributes—either real or relative.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude is the common cause of multiplicity in all three cases?
A) Cutting
B) Difference of attributes and qualities
C) Difference of attributes (real or relative)
D) Spatial separation

Key Takeaway
The cause of multiplicity in a body is always a difference in attributes, whether real or relative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every multiplicity in the universe—every difference between two things—ultimately reduces to a difference in attributes given by Allah, how does that reshape your understanding of His absolute oneness and sovereignty over every distinction?
?
When you mentally divide a situation into 'this part' and 'that part', do you recognize that even your mental pointing is a gift from Allah? How can this awareness lead you to greater gratitude and humility?
?
In your daily life, do you often attribute differences between people, circumstances, or objects to independent causes (like luck, effort, or nature) instead of seeing them as manifestations of Allah's creation of attributes? What would change if you consistently traced every multiplicity back to the Creator of attributes?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Divisions by Estimation Are Infinite
States that divisions by estimation are infinite with a brief proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why philosophers agree that mental divisions of a body are infinite and how the proof relies on the inability to reach a final limit of smallness without distinguishing sides.

Core Idea
No matter how small a body is, you can always imagine smaller.

After establishing the three causes of division, the author turns to the first: divisions by estimation and supposition (mental division). Philosophers universally agree that such divisions are infinite. This short passage provides a concise proof: any smallness limit can be surpassed by distinguishing sides. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's claim and proof.
Notice the compact style—the proof is just one sentence.
2
Identify the claim and the consensus
The author states that philosophers (الفلاسفة) agreed that divisions by estimation and supposition are infinite. This is a universal consensus among them.
This contrasts with other types of division still to be discussed.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof: a body never reaches a limit of smallness except that one side becomes distinguished from the other. This means at any point, we can mentally separate sides, allowing further division without bound.
The key is that distinguishing sides is a mental act, not actual cutting.
4
Examine the common misunderstanding
This infinite divisibility is potential (in the mind), not actual. The body is not composed of infinitely many parts in reality; rather, the process of mental division can continue indefinitely.
This distinction is crucial for later arguments about atomism.
5
Reflect on the significance
This shows that mental divisions do not entail an actual infinite in the physical world. It prepares for discussing causality and the nature of bodies.
Think about how this relates to the broader discussion of the three causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنرجع إلى هذه الأسباب الثلاثة فنقول: أما الانقسامات الحاصلة بحسب الوهم ويحسب الفرض، فالفلاسفة اتفقوا على أنها غير متناهية. وذلك لأن الجسم لا ينتهي في الصغر إلى حد، إلا ويتميز أحد جانبيه عن الآخر.
If you have known this, we return to these three causes and say: As for the divisions occurring according to estimation and according to supposition, the philosophers agreed that they are infinite. That is because the body does not reach a limit in smallness except that one of its sides becomes distinguished from the other.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a classification and a consensus. After the previous discussion, the author returns to the three causes of division and examines each. The first type is division by estimation and supposition (mental division). The author states that all philosophers agree that such divisions are infinite. The proof is brief: no matter how small a body becomes, one can always mentally distinguish one side from the other, meaning further division is possible. This implies that the process can go on without end.

Key Terms
الوهم والفرض
غير متناهية
الانقسامات الوهمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infinite divisibility means the body is actually composed of infinitely many parts.
Correction: The divisions are only potential and mental; the body itself is finite and not composed of an actual infinite number of parts.

Checkpoint
Why do philosophers agree that divisions by estimation are infinite?
A) Because the body can always be cut into smaller pieces
B) Because the body never becomes so small that one cannot distinguish its sides
C) Because the body is composed of atoms
D) Because the body is infinitely large

Key Takeaway
Philosophers agree that mental divisions of a body are infinite because no matter how small, you can always distinguish one side from another.

What step will you take this week to humble yourself before that reality?

How does this truth change the way you look at a simple object like a leaf or a grain of sand? What specific action will you take to increase your awe of the Creator when you see such things?

Aristotle's Proof for Infinite Divisions by Cutting
Aristotle and his followers' proof that divisions by cutting are infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Aristotelian argument that physical bodies can be divided by cutting infinitely.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility by cutting is proven by the compound/simple distinction.

In this passage, al-Shirazi presents the first of two philosophical camps on the nature of physical divisions. Aristotle and his followers argue that actual cutting can never reach an end. Their reasoning relies on analyzing each part after division: whether it is compound or simple. Let us see how they establish their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for Aristotle's view. Note the structure: claim, then proof.
2
Understand the proof
The proof divides parts into compound or simple. Understand why both cases lead to further divisibility.
For simple parts, they are similar and separation is possible.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The conclusion is that there is no end to the possibility of division by cutting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثاني: وهو الانقسامات الحاصلة بسبب القطع والافتراق فالفلاسفة قد اختلفوا في أنها متناهية أو غير متناهية. أما أرسطاطاليس وأصحابه المتقدمون والمتأخرون كأبي نصر الفارابي وأبي علي بن سينا فقد اتفقوا على أن قبول هذا النوع من القسمة حاصل إلى غير النهاية. قالوا وتقريره: إن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء إما أن يكون مركباً أو بسيطاً. فإن كان مركباً فهو قابل للانحلال والتفرق. وإن كان بسيطاً كانت الأجزاء المفترضة فيه بحسب الوهم متشابهة، فكما صح على الجزءين أن يتباينا مباينة رافعة للاتصال الحقيقي. وإذا كان كذلك وجب القطع بأنه لا نهاية لقبول الانقسامات الحاصلة بسبب التفكك والتفرق. وقول قوم عظيم من قدماء الفلاسفة: إن الانقسامات الحاصلة بالوهم والفرض وإن كانت غير متناهية إلا أن الانقسامات الحاصلة بسبب التفرق والتباعد متناهية. فهذه الأجسام المحسوسة مؤلفة ومركبة من أجزاء أصلية قابلة للقسمة الوهمية وغير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية. فهذه الأجسام المحسوسة لما كانت قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية، كان إحداث القسمة فيها عبارة عن تبعيد المتجاورين. وأما كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء فإن أحد نصفيه متصل في النصف الآخر منه اتصالاً حقيقياً، فلا جرم يمتنع ورود القسمة عليه. وهذا قول ديمقراطيس وقوم آخرون.
As for the second type: it is the divisions occurring due to cutting and separation; the philosophers have differed whether they are finite or infinite. As for Aristotle and his followers, both earlier and later, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali Ibn Sina, they agreed that the acceptance of this type of division occurs ad infinitum. They said, and its proof: each of those parts is either compound or simple. If it is compound, it is divisible and disintegrates. If it is simple, the parts supposed in it by estimation are similar, and just as it is correct for the two parts to be separated by a separation that removes true contiguity, and if that is so, it must be concluded that there is no end to the acceptance of divisions occurring due to dismemberment and separation. And the saying of a great group of ancient philosophers: that the divisions occurring by estimation and supposition, even if they are infinite, yet the divisions occurring due to separation and distancing are finite. So these sensible bodies are composed and compounded of primary parts that are divisible by estimation and not divisible by actual separation. So these sensible bodies, since they are divisible by actual separation, causing division in them is nothing but distancing the adjacent parts. As for each of those parts, one of its halves is contiguous with the other half with true contiguity, so inevitably division cannot occur on it. And this is the saying of Democritus and other people.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of the second type of division (by cutting) and the disagreement about it. It first states that philosophers differ on whether divisions by cutting are finite or infinite. Then it presents two camps. The first camp is Aristotle and his followers (including Farabi and Ibn Sina); they hold that such divisions are infinite. Their proof is: each part of a body after division is either compound or simple. If compound, it can be further divided. If simple (i.e., without parts), the parts that we imagine in it are similar; but since we can conceive of two parts separating, true contiguity is lost, meaning division is possible. Therefore, there is no end to the possibility of division by cutting. The second camp is Democritus and others; they hold that divisions by estimation are infinite, but actual physical divisions by separation are finite. Their argument: sensible bodies are composed of primary parts that can be mentally divided but cannot actually be separated. When we cause actual division, we are only separating neighbors, not splitting the primary parts themselves. Each primary part has its halves truly contiguous and cannot be divided. So actual division stops at the primary parts.

Key Terms
متناهية وغير متناهية
مركب وبسيط
الانقسام الانفكاكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Aristotle believed bodies can actually be divided infinitely in reality.
Correction: Aristotle held that division by cutting is potentially infinite, meaning you can always cut again, not that there are infinite parts actually present.

Checkpoint
According to Aristotle's proof, what is the nature of a simple part?
A) It cannot be divided at all
B) It has no parts and its imagined parts are similar
C) It is composed of smaller parts
D) It is indivisible by estimation

Key Takeaway
According to Aristotle, the possibility of division by cutting continues without end because every part, whether compound or simple, can be further divided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this proof, do you feel a sense of intellectual security in human reasoning, or does it remind you of the limits of logic without divine guidance? Where in your life do you trust your own reasoning over Allah's revelation?
?
The philosophers argue that even simple parts can be divided by separation. In your spiritual life, do you ever consider small acts of obedience or sin as 'simple' and therefore insignificant? How does this lesson challenge you to recognize the potential impact of every action?
?
If matter can be divided infinitely, what does that say about the Creator who brought it into existence? Does this increase your awe of His power, or does it tempt you to try to comprehend Him through human categories? Pause and ask Allah to grant you humility in your quest for knowledge.

Democritus' View: Divisions by Cutting are Finite
Democritus and his followers' argument that divisions by cutting are finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Democritus' argument that actual physical divisions are finite and stop at primary parts.

Core Idea
Actual division stops at primary parts which are indivisible by cutting.

In contrast to Aristotle, Democritus and his followers argue that while we can imagine infinite divisions, actual cutting eventually reaches primary parts that cannot be further separated. Their reasoning involves a distinction between mental divisibility and physical divisibility. Let us see how they defend this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for Democritus' view. Notice the distinction between divisions by estimation and by actual separation.
2
Identify the argument
Democritus argues that sensible bodies are composed of primary parts. These parts can be mentally divided but cannot actually be separated.
The key concept is 'true contiguity' between halves of a primary part.
3
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, the number of actual divisions by cutting is finite, limited to the number of primary parts.

Source Text

As for the second type: it is the divisions occurring due to cutting and separation; the philosophers have differed whether they are finite or infinite. As for Aristotle and his followers, both earlier and later, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali Ibn Sina, they agreed that the acceptance of this type of division occurs ad infinitum. They said, and its proof: each of those parts is either compound or simple. If it is compound, it is divisible and disintegrates. If it is simple, the parts supposed in it by estimation are similar, and just as it is correct for the two parts to be separated by a separation that removes true contiguity, and if that is so, it must be concluded that there is no end to the acceptance of divisions occurring due to dismemberment and separation. As for each of those parts, one of its halves is contiguous with the other half with true contiguity, so inevitably division cannot occur on it. And this is the saying of Democritus and other people.

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of the second type of division (by cutting) and the disagreement about it. It first states that philosophers differ on whether divisions by cutting are finite or infinite. Then it presents two camps. The first camp is Aristotle and his followers (including Farabi and Ibn Sina); they hold that such divisions are infinite. Aristotle's Dilemma for Infinite Division
Aristotle and his followers prove that separative division is infinite via a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's dilemma argument for infinite separative division and how it applies to both composite and simple parts.

Core Idea
Every part, composite or simple, can be separated further without end.

In this passage, the author presents the proof of Aristotle and his followers (al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā) for the claim that separative division continues without limit. The argument is a classic dilemma: whichever way we analyze a part, we end up with the possibility of further division. Let's unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases of the dilemma
Notice that the proof begins by dividing every part into two kinds: composite or simple. Each case leads to a different reason for divisibility.
This classification is exhaustive—every part must be one or the other.
2
Understand the composite case
If the part is composite (made of smaller parts), it can be dissolved (انحلال) and dispersed (تفرق). This obviously allows further division.
This case is straightforward: composite parts are already divisible into their constituents.
3
Understand the simple case
If the part is simple, the parts imagined within it (in estimation) are similar. It is then possible for any two such imagined parts to separate completely, breaking the real connection (اتصال حقيقي). This also leads to infinite division.
The key is that even simple parts are not absolutely indivisible—they can separate if they are distinct in estimation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أرسطاطاليس، وأصحابه المتقدمون والمتأخرون كأبي نصر الفارابي وأبي علي بن سينا فقد اتفقوا على أن قبول هذا النوع من القسمة حاصل إلى غير النهاية. قالوا: وتقريره: إن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء إما أن يكون مركباً أو بسيطاً. فإن كان مركباً، فهو قابل للانحلال والتفرق. وإن كان بسيطاً، كانت الأجزاء المفترضة فيه بحسب الوهم متشابهة، فكما صح على الجزءين أن يتباينا مباينة رافعة للاتصال الحقيقي. وإذا كان كذلك، وجب القطع بأنه لا نهاية لقبول الانقسامات الحاصلة بسبب التفكك والتفرق.
As for Aristotle and his earlier and later followers, such as Abū Naṣr al-Fārābī and Abū ʿAlī ibn Sīnā, they agreed that the acceptance of this type of division occurs ad infinitum. They said: and its exposition: every one of those parts is either composite or simple. If it is composite, it is susceptible to dissolution and dispersion. And if it is simple, the parts supposed in it according to estimation are similar; so just as it is valid for the two parts to be separate, a separation that removes real connection. And if that is so, it is necessary to judge that there is no end to the acceptance of the divisions occurring due to disassembly and dispersion.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a proof. It first states the position: Aristotle and his school (including al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā) agree that separative division can continue without end. Then it gives their reasoning (taqrīr). The proof is a dilemma: any part of a body is either composite or simple. If composite, it can be dissolved further. If simple, then the parts imaginatively distinguished within it (by estimation) are similar; hence it is possible for any two such parts to separate entirely, breaking the real connection. Since this applies to any simple part, division can proceed indefinitely. The conclusion (wa idhā kāna dhālika, wajaba al-qaṭʿ...) is that separative division has no final limit. This argument relies on the idea that even simple parts are not absolutely indivisible—they can separate if they are distinct in estimation.

Key Terms
مركب
بسيط
انحلال
تفرق
اتصال حقيقي
غير النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Aristotle denies the existence of atomic parts entirely.
Correction: Aristotle does not deny atomic parts in all contexts; he only denies that they are an absolute ultimate limit in the context of separative division. The argument shows that even simple parts can separate, so they are not absolutely indivisible.

Checkpoint
In Aristotle's proof, if a part is simple, what property leads to infinite divisibility?
A) It has no parts
B) The parts supposed in it are similar and can separate
C) It is made of atoms
D) It cannot be divided further

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's argument shows that any part, whether composite or simple, can be further divided by separation, leading to an infinite process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that every part can be divided further without end. In your own character, you may think some trait is 'simple' and cannot change. But even the smallest habit can be separated and refined. Which flaw in yourself have you considered permanent or indivisible? Will you commit today to disassemble it by taking one concrete step of dissolution?
?
Aristotle's proof relies on estimation (wahm) to show that even simple parts can break connection. In your life, what belief or assumption do you hold as 'one solid piece' that actually needs to be broken apart by honest estimation? Will you let this lesson force you to examine that belief today?
?
If there is no end to division, then nothing in creation is truly simple. This humbles the intellect. What aspect of your knowledge or faith have you considered 'simple' and complete? Are you open to the possibility that it can be further refined? What step will you take today to deepen your understanding?

Atomist Theory: Finite Separative Division
Democritus' view on finite separative division and indivisible atoms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomist position that only estimative division is infinite, while actual separative division is finite, and that atoms are truly indivisible.

Core Idea
Atoms are real, indivisible units; only our minds can divide them further.

Ancient atomists like Democritus proposed that while we can imagine dividing matter endlessly, real physical division stops at some point. This lesson explores their reasoning: bodies are composed of original parts that are connected so strongly that they cannot be separated. This sets the stage for understanding the nature of atoms and why they must be simple and indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on atomist division
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author distinguishes between two types of division: by estimation (infinite) and by separation (finite).
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'جزء أصلي' (original part), 'قابلة للقسمة الوهمية' (divisible by estimation), and 'غير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية' (not divisible by separation). Understand what each means.
3
Understand the argument for indivisibility
The text says each part's halves are truly connected, so real division cannot happen. Reflect on why this leads to the conclusion that atoms are indivisible in reality.
4
Connect to Democritus
Note that this is attributed to Democritus and others. This sets the historical context.
Democritus is a key figure in atomism.
5
Address a common misunderstanding
Some think atomists believed all division is finite. The passage clarifies that only separative division is finite; conceptual division is infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول قوم عظيم من قدماء الفلاسفة: إن الانقسامات الحاصلة بالوهم والفرض، وإن كانت غير متناهية؛ إلا أن الانقسامات الحاصلة بسبب التفرق والتباعد متناهية. فهذه الأجسام المحسوسة مؤلفة ومركبة من أجزاء أصلية قابلة للقسمة الوهمية، وغير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية. فهذه الأجسام المحسوسة لما كانت قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية، كان إحداث القسمة فيها عبارة عن تبعيد المتجاورين. وأما كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء، فإن أحد نصفيه متصل بالنصف الآخر منه، اتصالاً حقيقياً. فلا جرم يمتنع ورود القسمة عليه. وهذا قول ديمقراطيس، وقوم آخرون. وهؤلاء اختلفوا في أشكال تلك الأجزاء. فالأكثرون قالوا: إنها كرات. وذلك لأن كل واحد منها، يجب أن يكون بسيطاً. إذ لو كان مركباً، لقبل الانحلال. وقد فرضنا أنه غير قابل للانحلال. هذا خلف. وإن كان بسيطاً، وجب أن يكون شكله: الكرة. لما ثبت أن شكل البسيط، يجب أن يكون هو الكرة. ثم إنهم لما عرفوا أن الكرات المتماسة، لا بد وأن تبقى فيما بينها فرج خالية، لا جرم التزموا القول بالخلاء. وقال الباقون: إنه لا يجب فيها أن تكون كرات. لأن القول بالخلاء ممتنع. ثم إنهم اختلفوا. فمنهم من قال: إنه يجب أن تكون أشكالها المكعبات. لأن الشكل الذي يملأ الفرج، ولا يبقى معه شيء من الخلاء في الأجسام: ليس إلا المكعبات. ومنهم من قال: إنها مثلثات. لأن هذا الشكل أول المضلعات. ومنهم من قال: إنها مربعات. ومنهم من قال: إنها على خمسة أنواع من الأشكال. فالأول: ما يحيط به أربع مثلثات متساوية الأضلاع. وهذا الشكل هو الشكل الناري. وهذه الأجزاء إذا تألفت واجتمعت حصل منها النار. والسبب فيه: أن خاصية النار التفريق وذلك إنما يحصل إذا كان جوهر النار قوياً على النفوذ في بواطن المتصلات. والجسم متى كان موصوفاً بالشكل المذكور، كان قوياً على النفوذ في المتصلات، وعلى الغوص فيها بسبب زواياه الحادة النافذة. والنوع الثاني من الأشكال: المكعب. وهو الذي يحيط به ست مربعات متساوية الأضلاع، وهذه الأجزاء هي التي إذا تألفت حصل من تألفها الأرض، وإنما قلنا ذلك، لأن الجسم الموصوف بالشكل المكعب، يعسر
And the statement of a great group of ancient philosophers: that the divisions occurring by estimation and supposition, even if they are infinite, yet the divisions occurring due to dispersion and distance are finite. Thus these sensible bodies are composed and compounded of original parts that are divisible by estimation and not divisible by separation. So since these sensible bodies are divisible by separation, the causing of division in them is an act of distancing adjacent parts. But as for each one of those parts, one of its halves is connected to the other half with a real connection. Hence necessarily division does not occur upon it. And this is the statement of Democritus and other people. And these differed regarding the shapes of those parts. The majority said: they are spheres. And that is because each one of them must be simple. For if it were composite, it would accept dissolution. But we have assumed it is not subject to dissolution. This is a contradiction. And if it is simple, its shape must be the sphere. Because it is established that the shape of the simple must be the sphere. Then when they realized that spheres in contact must leave empty gaps between them, they necessarily committed to the statement of vacuum. And the rest said: it is not necessary for them to be spheres, because the statement of vacuum is impossible. Then they differed. Among them were those who said: their shapes must be cubes, because the shape that fills gaps and leaves nothing of vacuum in bodies is only the cube. And among them were those who said: they are triangles, because this shape is the first of polygons. And among them were those who said: they are squares. And among them were those who said: they are of five types of shapes. The first: what is surrounded by four equilateral triangles. And this shape is the fiery shape. And these parts, when they are composed and come together, fire results from them. And the reason for that is that the property of fire is to separate, and that only occurs if the substance of fire is strong in penetrating the interiors of continuous bodies. And when a body is described by the mentioned shape, it is strong in penetrating continuous bodies and delving into them due to its sharp penetrating angles. And the second type of shapes: the cube. And it is what is surrounded by six equal squares. And these parts are those which, when they are composed, earth results from their composition. And we only say that because a body described by the cubic shape is difficult
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents the atomist view. It begins by stating the position of a great group of ancient philosophers (i.e., Democritus and others): while estimative divisions are infinite, actual separative divisions are finite. Sensible bodies are composed of original parts (atoms) that are divisible only in estimation, not in reality. The reason: when we try to actually separate, we are moving adjacent parts apart; but each atom has its halves truly connected, so division does not happen to it. After stating this theory, the passage turns to the shapes of these atoms. The majority say atoms must be spheres: each atom is simple, and the shape of a simple body is the sphere. However, spheres in contact leave empty gaps (vacuum). So they affirm vacuum. The remaining philosophers deny vacuum and therefore reject spherical atoms. They propose alternative shapes that fill space without vacuum: cubes (since they fill gaps), triangles (the simplest polygon), squares, or five types of shapes. The passage then describes the first of those five: the tetrahedron (four equilateral triangles) which is the shape of fire. Fire's sharp angles allow it to penetrate bodies, explaining its property of separating. The second type is the cube, the shape of earth. Earth's cubic shape makes it stable and difficult to move. The text cuts off at 'yʿsur' (difficult).

قابلة للقسمة الوهمية
غير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية
ديمقراطيس
انقسام انفكاكي

Correction: The text explicitly says estimative divisions are infinite. Only separative divisions are finite.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are too small to be cut
B) Because each half is truly connected to the other half
C) Because they are made of spheres that cannot be separated

Key Takeaway
Atoms are indivisible in reality because their halves are inseparably connected, though the mind can imagine dividing them endlessly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How often do you let your imagination run into infinite worries or possibilities, forgetting that Allah has set real limits? What is one area of your life where you need to accept this boundary and trust His decree?
?
The atom's halves are so connected that no force can separate them. Reflect on your own connection to the truth of tawheed. Is it a real, inseparable connection, or just a conceptual belief that falls apart when tested? What practical step will you take today to strengthen that bond?
?
Democritus and others recognized that real division is finite. Yet you live in a world that promotes infinite choices, endless consumption, and constant change. Where in your daily routine do you ignore the finiteness that Allah placed in creation? How will you align your actions with this reality starting now?

Debates on Atom Shapes: Spheres, Cubes, and More
Debates on atom shapes and their explanatory power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why atomists disagreed on the shapes of atoms, the arguments for spheres and cubes, and how shapes explain properties of substances like fire and earth.

Core Idea
Atom shapes determine the properties of matter: sharp angles cause fire, cubes make earth stable.

After establishing that atoms are indivisible, ancient atomists debated their shapes. The majority argued for spheres due to simplicity, but spheres imply empty space (vacuum). Others rejected vacuum and proposed shapes that fill space without gaps, like cubes. This lesson explores these arguments and how specific shapes (tetrahedron for fire, cube for earth) explain observable phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on shapes
Read the Arabic and translation from 'وهؤلاء اختلفوا' to the end. Notice the different positions: spheres, cubes, triangles, squares, and the five types.
2
Understand the argument for spheres
The majority say atoms must be spheres because each atom is simple, and the shape of the simple must be the sphere. But then they had to accept vacuum. Ponder why spheres leave gaps.
3
Examine the alternatives
Those who deny vacuum propose cubes (fill gaps), triangles (first polygon), squares, or five specific shapes. Note the reasoning for each.
4
Focus on the five types
The passage describes two of the five: the tetrahedron (fire) and cube (earth). Understand why fire's sharp angles let it penetrate, and earth's cubic shape makes it stable.
5
Connect shape to property
The atomists explained macroscopic properties by microscopic shapes. Fire separates because its atoms have sharp angles; earth is stable because cubes are hard to move.

وقول قوم عظيم من قدماء الفلاسفة: إن الانقسامات الحاصلة بالوهم والفرض، وإن كانت غير متناهية؛ إلا أن الانقسامات الحاصلة بسبب التفرق والتباعد متناهية. فهذه الأجسام المحسوسة مؤلفة ومركبة من أجزاء أصلية قابلة للقسمة الوهمية، وغير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية. فهذه الأجسام المحسوسة لما كانت قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية، كان إحداث القسمة فيها عبارة عن تبعيد المتجاورين. وأما كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء، فإن أحد نصفيه متصل بالنصف الآخر منه، اتصالاً حقيقياً. فلا جرم يمتنع ورود القسمة عليه. وهذا قول ديمقراطيس، وقوم آخرون. وهؤلاء اختلفوا في أشكال تلك الأجزاء. فالأكثرون قالوا: إنها كرات. وذلك لأن كل واحد منها، يجب أن يكون بسيطاً. إذ لو كان مركباً، لقبل الانحلال. وقد فرضنا أنه غير قابل للانحلال. هذا خلف. وإن كان بسيطاً، وجب أن يكون شكله: الكرة. لما ثبت أن شكل البسيط، يجب أن يكون هو الكرة. ثم إنهم لما عرفوا أن الكرات المتماسة، لا بد وأن تبقى فيما بينها فرج خالية، لا جرم التزموا القول بالخلاء. وقال الباقون: إنه لا يجب فيها أن تكون كرات. لأن القول بالخلاء ممتنع. ثم إنهم اختلفوا. فمنهم من قال: إنه يجب أن تكون أشكالها المكعبات. لأن الشكل الذي يملأ الفرج، ولا يبقى معه شيء من الخلاء في الأجسام: ليس إلا المكعبات. ومنهم من قال: إنها مثلثات. لأن هذا الشكل أول المضلعات. ومنهم من قال: إنها مربعات. ومنهم من قال: إنها على خمسة أنواع من الأشكال. فالأول: ما يحيط به أربع مثلثات متساوية الأضلاع. وهذا الشكل هو الشكل الناري. وهذه الأجزاء إذا تألفت واجتمعت حصل منها النار. والسبب فيه: أن خاصية النار التفريق وذلك إنما يحصل إذا كان جوهر النار قوياً على النفوذ في بواطن المتصلات. والجسم متى كان موصوفاً بالشكل المذكور، كان قوياً على النفوذ في المتصلات، وعلى الغوص فيها بسبب زواياه الحادة النافذة. والنوع الثاني من الأشكال: المكعب. وهو الذي يحيط به ست مربعات متساوية الأضلاع، وهذه الأجزاء هي التي إذا تألفت حصل من تألفها الأرض، وإنما قلنا ذلك، لأن الجسم الموصوف بالشكل المكعب، يعسر

Disagreement on the Shapes of Atomic Particles
The author presents the disagreement about the shapes of atoms and the arguments for spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments for different atomic shapes and how the problem of void shapes the debate.

Core Idea
Atomic shapes are deduced from simplicity, but the void problem splits opinions.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic atomism: what shape are indivisible particles? The majority argues for spheres based on simplicity, but then must accept void—a stance others reject, leading to alternative shapes. As you read, trace how each position logically unfolds from its premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main groups
Read the passage and note that the author first presents the majority view (spheres) then the opposition (cubes, triangles, squares). Understand their starting assumptions.
The majority starts from the premise that atoms are simple; the opposition starts from the impossibility of void.
2
Analyze the majority's argument
Follow the reasoning: atoms must be simple (not composite), therefore spherical. Then realize that spheres in contact leave empty gaps, forcing acceptance of void.
This shows a chain of necessary consequences.
3
Examine the alternative shapes
See why some reject spheres: void is impossible. Then see the different proposals—cubes fill space, triangles are simplest polygon, squares are another option.
Each alternative tries to avoid void while maintaining indivisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهؤلاء اختلفوا في أشكال تلك الأجزاء. فالأكثرون قالوا : إنها كرات . وذلك لأن كل واحد منها ، يجب أن يكون بسيطاً . إذ لو كان مركباً ، لقبل الانحلال . وقد فرضنا أنه غير قابل للانحلال . هذا خلف . وإن كان بسيطاً ، وجب أن يكون شكله : الكرة . لما ثبت أن شكل البسيط ، يجب أن يكون هو الكرة . ثم إنهم لما عرفوا أن الكرات المتماسة ، لا بد وأن تبقى فيما بينها فرج خالية ، لا جرم التزموا القول بالخلاء . وقال الباقون : [ إنه ] لا يجب فيها أن تكون كرات . لأن القول بالخلاء ممتنع . ثم إنهم اختلفوا . فمنهم من قال : إنه يجب أن تكون أشكالها المكعبات . لأن الشكل الذي يملأ الفرج ، ولا يبقى معه شيء من الخلاء في الأجسام : ليس إلا المكعبات . ومنهم من قال : إنها مثلثات . لأن هذا الشكل أول المضلعات . [ ومنهم من قال : إنها مربعات ] .
And these [scholars] differed concerning the shapes of those parts. The majority said: they are spheres. That is because every one of them must be simple. For if it were composite, it would accept decomposition. And we have supposed that it is not subject to decomposition. This is a contradiction. And if it is simple, its shape must be the sphere, because it has been established that the shape of the simple must be the sphere. Then when they knew that contiguous spheres necessarily leave empty gaps between them, they unreservedly adopted the claim of void. And the rest said: it is not necessary for them to be spheres, because the claim of void is impossible. Then they differed. Among them were those who said: their shapes must be cubes, because the shape that fills gaps and leaves no void in bodies is only the cube. And among them were those who said: they are triangles, because this shape is the first of the polygons. And among them were those who said: they are squares.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument about the shapes of indivisible particles (atoms). The author first reports the majority view that atoms are spheres. The reasoning is that each atom must be simple (not composite), because if it were composite it could be decomposed, contradicting the assumption that it is indivisible. For a simple body, the natural shape is a sphere, as established by geometric principles. However, the majority then realized that if spheres are placed in contact, empty spaces (void) remain between them, so they were forced to accept the existence of void. A second group rejects this, denying that atoms must be spheres because void is impossible. Within this group, further disagreement arises: some say atoms are cubes, because cubes can fill space without leaving void. Others say they are triangles, since the triangle is the simplest polygon. Another opinion suggests squares. The passage thus lays out multiple positions on atomic shapes, emphasizing the problem of void as a key issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for atomic shapes is based on empirical observation or physical experiment.
Correction: The reasoning is a priori: it deduces shape from the logical nature of simplicity and the geometric necessity, not from empirical evidence.

Checkpoint

B) Because contiguous spheres necessarily leave empty gaps between them.
C) Because the void is a necessary condition for motion.
D) Because the sphere is the simplest shape.

Key Takeaway
The debate over atomic shapes highlights the tension between logical necessity and empirical consequence in classical kalam.

Division by Accidents: Relational and Real
Author classifies and discusses division by accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between division due to relational accidents and real accidents, and the positions on infinite divisibility for each.

Core Idea
Relational accidents divide infinitely; real accidents are debated between finite and infinite.

We now turn to a subtle type of division: division arising from variations in accidents. The author distinguishes two categories of accidents, each with a different ruling regarding divisibility ad infinitum. This distinction is crucial for understanding physical composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the two main parts: relational accidents (first sentence) and real accidents (second part).
2
Identify the two types of accidents
The passage distinguishes 'relational accidents' (أعراض إضافية) and 'real accidents' (أعراض حقيقية). Understand what each refers to.
Relational accidents involve relative position; real accidents are intrinsic qualities like water's nature.
3
Analyze the argument for relational accidents
The author argues that relational division is infinite because any part of a body always has something adjacent on both sides. See why this forces infinite differentiation.
This is a clear causal argument.
4
Examine the two views on real accidents
Some say water (example) reaches a limit where further division changes its nature; others say it continues infinitely. Note the Peripatetic view.
The debate hinges on whether essential properties survive division.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters for natural philosophy. It shows that not all division is alike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثالث: وهو القسمة الحاصلة بسبب اختلاف الأعراض. فنقول: أما الأعراض الإضافية. فهذا النوع من القسمة ثابت [ فيها ] إلى غير النهاية. لأنه إلى أي حد انتهى الجسم ، فإنه لا بد أن يماس او يحاذي بأحد جانبيه شيئاً ، وبالجانب الثاني شيئاً آخر. وأما اختلاف الأعراض الحقيقية. فهل يمر إلى غير النهاية ؟ فمنهم من قال : الماء ينتهي في الصغر إلى حيث لو وردت القسمة عليه بعد ذلك ، لما بقي ماء ، بل انقلب إلى طبيعة الجسم المستولى عليه . وهذا قول كثير من المشائين ، ومنهم من قال : بل هذا النوع من القسمة باقي أيضاً إلى غير النهاية . فهذا هو الكلام في تفاصيل المذاهب في هذه المسألة . وبالله التوفيق
As for the third type: it is the division that occurs due to variation of accidents. We say: as for relational accidents, this type of division is established [in them] ad infinitum. Because to whatever limit the body reaches, it must necessarily touch or be adjacent on one side to something, and on the second side to something else. As for variation of real accidents, does it proceed ad infinitum? Among them are those who said: water reaches [a limit] in smallness such that if division were applied to it after that, water would not remain, but it would transform into the nature of the dominant body. This is the view of many of the Peripatetics. And among them are those who said: rather, this type of division also remains ad infinitum. This is the discussion concerning the details of the schools on this question. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage examines a third type of division of bodies: division by variation of accidents (a'rad). The author distinguishes two cases. First, relational accidents (such as position or adjacency) allow division to continue infinitely, because any part of a body, no matter how small, will always have something adjacent to it on one side and something else on the other, so relations can always be differentiated. Second, real accidents (such as the substance of water) may or may not be divisible indefinitely. The author reports two positions: some, following many Peripatetics, argue that water (as an example) reaches a limit where further division would change its nature into that of another element (e.g., air or earth). Others maintain that even real accidents allow infinite division. The passage ends by noting that this covers the details of the various schools on this issue, and invokes divine assistance.

Key Terms
أعراض إضافية
أعراض حقيقية
قسمة
إلى غير النهاية
المشائين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divisions of accidents are the same in their possibility of infinite division.
Correction: The passage distinguishes relational accidents (infinite) from real accidents (debated), showing that the type of accident matters.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does division due to relational accidents proceed infinitely?
A) Because bodies are infinitely large.
B) Because any part will always have something adjacent on both sides.
C) Because real accidents prevent a limit.
D) Because the majority opinion holds it.

Key Takeaway
Division by accidents is not uniform: relational accidents divide infinitely, while real accidents have competing views on limits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The author ends with 'And with God is success.' In what area of your life are you currently trying to rely on your own intellectual conclusions rather than submitting to Allah's ultimate knowledge and decree? What would it look like to truly say 'And with God is success' in that matter?

Three Types of Shapes: Triangular, Icosahedron, Dodecahedron
Author enumerates three types of shapes and their relation to elements and Euclid's book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three geometric shapes described by the author and their associations with elements, properties, and Euclid's classification.

Core Idea
The five regular solids (Platonic solids) correspond to physical elements and celestial forms.

This lesson examines the author's enumeration of the third, fourth, and fifth regular solids. The author describes how each shape, from eight triangular bases to twenty triangular bases to twelve pentagonal bases, corresponds to air, diamond, and the celestial sphere. The lesson also notes that these are the five shapes with which Euclid concluded his book.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses 'ب' (with) instead of 'by' in some places.
2
Identify the three shapes
Find the descriptions of the third, fourth, and fifth shapes. Note the number of bases and their type (triangular or pentagonal).
3
Associate each shape with its element or property
The author links the third shape (8 triangular bases) to air, the fourth (20 triangular) to diamond, and the fifth (12 pentagonal) to the celestial. Why might these links be made?
The reason given for the third shape is its suitability for quick motion and rolling.
4
Consider the Euclid reference
The author says these five shapes are those with which Euclid ended his book. Why would Euclid end with these? What does this tell us about their importance?

Source Text

The reason for it we said: this shape is appointed for quick motion and rolling. And air is likewise. The fourth type: that which is surrounded by twenty triangular bases. And this shape is the diamond. The fifth type: that which is surrounded by twelve pentagonal bases. And this is the celestial shape. And these five shapes are the five with which Euclid concluded his book.

Commentary
This passage enumerates three types of geometric shapes (the third, fourth, and fifth of the five regular solids), explaining their properties and correspondences. The third shape has eight equilateral triangular bases; when these parts come together, they form air. The author notes that this shape is suited for quick motion and rolling, and air behaves similarly. The fourth shape has twenty triangular bases; this is the diamond shape. The fifth shape has twelve pentagonal bases; this is the celestial shape. The author concludes that these five shapes are the ones with which Euclid ended his book, indicating their fundamental importance in geometry and natural philosophy.

Division by Accidents: Infinite Divisibility of Accidental and Real Accidents
Author discusses division by accidents and the two views on infinite divisibility of real accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between accidental and real accidents and the two positions on the infinite divisibility of real accidents.

Core Idea
Not all accidents are infinitely divisible; real accidents may have a limit.

In this passage, the author turns to the third type of division—division due to differences of accidents. He distinguishes between accidental (relative) accidents, which can be divided infinitely, and real accidents, where philosophers disagree. This discussion is important because it touches on the nature of physical change and the limits of material analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's terminology and flow register before interpretation.
Note the terms 'أعراض إضافية' and 'أعراض حقيقية'.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where the author distinguishes accidental accidents from real accidents. Notice the different treatment: for accidental, divisibility is infinite; for real, it is debated.
3
Understand the reasoning for accidental accidents
The author gives a reason: no matter how small, a body always has sides adjacent to others. Ponder why this forces infinite divisibility.
This is a geometric rather than physical argument.
4
Examine the two views on real accidents
First view (many Peripatetics): water reaches a limit where further division changes it to another nature. Second view: division continues infinitely. Compare the two.
The first view implies a qualitative change at the limit.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this relates to broader debates about atomism, change, and the nature of bodies. The author does not choose sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا هو الكلام في القسمة الحاصلة بسبب التفريق والقطع . وأما النوع الثالث : وهو القسمة الحاصلة ، بسبب اختلاف الأعراض . فنقول : أما الأعراض الإضافية . فهذا النوع من القسمة ثابت فيها إلى غير النهاية. لأنه إلى أي حد انتهى الجسم فإنه لا بد أن يماس او يحاذي بأحد جانبيه شيئاً ، وبالجانب الثاني شيئاً آخر. وأما اختلاف الأعراض الحقيقية . فهل يمر إلى غير النهاية ؟ فمنهم من قال : الماء ينتهي في الصغر إلى حيث لو وردت القسمة عليه بعد ذلك ، لما يقي ماء ، بل انقلب إلى طبيعة الجسم المستولى عليه . وهذا قول كثير من المشائين ، ومنهم من قال : بل هذا النوع من القسمة باقي أيضاً إلى غير النهاية . فهذا هو الكلام في تفاصيل المذاهب في هذه المسألة . وبالله التوفيق
This is the discussion of the division that occurs due to separation and cutting. As for the third type: it is the division that occurs due to difference of accidents. We say: as for accidental accidents, this type of division is established in them to infinity. Because to whatever limit the body reaches, it must touch or be adjacent on one side to something, and on the second side to something else. As for the difference of real accidents, does it proceed to infinity? Among them is one who said: water ends in smallness to the point that if division were applied to it after that, no water would remain, rather it would change into the nature of the dominating body. And this is the saying of many of the Peripatetics. And among them is one who said: rather this type of division also remains to infinity. This is the discussion of the details of the schools on this issue. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage discusses the third type of division, namely division due to differences of accidents. The author distinguishes two kinds of accidents: accidental (relative) accidents and real accidents. For accidental accidents, division proceeds to infinity because no matter how small a body is, it will always have sides touching or adjacent to something else. For real accidents, there is a dispute. Some (including many Peripatetics) hold that bodies like water have a limit: when divided to a certain smallness, the water ceases to be water and transforms into the nature of the dominating body (i.e., the element that overcomes it). Others maintain that this kind of division also goes on infinitely. Thus the author presents the two positions without endorsing either.

أعراض حقيقية
غير النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is about the infinite divisibility of atoms or substances.
Correction: The passage is about the division of accidents, not substances. The debate concerns whether qualitative change occurs at the limit of division of real accidents like water.

Checkpoint

B) It becomes air

Key Takeaway
The division of real accidents is disputed: some hold it is finite, leading to transformation into a different nature, while others maintain it is infinite.

First Proof: Motion Exists as a Real Entity
First proof establishing motion as real via contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that motion is a real existing entity, using a contradiction argument that a moving body cannot be non-moving.

Core Idea
Motion must be real; otherwise a moving body would be non-moving.

In this passage, the author begins the first proof that motion is composed of successive indivisible positions. He starts by demonstrating that motion is a real existent thing. He uses a simple but powerful argument: if motion were not real, a body after moving would be in the same state as before (non-moving), which contradicts the observed fact that it is moving. This sets the stage for further analysis of motion's structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the author's style: he states the aim, then gives the proof.
The passage is incomplete; it ends mid-sentence.
2
Identify the contradiction argument
The author says: if nothing new occurred in the body during motion, then after motion it would be as before (non-moving). But that would mean the moving thing is non-moving – a contradiction.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since that contradiction is impossible, motion must be an existing entity that occurs in the body.
The proof does not yet show indivisibility; that comes later.
4
Reflect on the significance
This establishes motion as a real attribute, not just a relation or illusion. It is the foundation for the next steps in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النوع الأول: وهو ذكر الدليل على أن الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة، في أحياز متلاصقة، بحيث يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة . فنقول: الذي يدل على صحة ما قلنا وجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : الجسم لم يكن متحركاً ، ثم صار متحركاً . فالحركة أمر موجود في نفسها . إذ لو لم يحدث في الجسم حال كونه متحركاً، أمر من الأمور . لزم أن يقال : إنه في نفسه بعد الحركة ، كما كان قبل الحركة . لكنه قبل الحركة غير متحرك ، فهو بعد الحركة غير متحرك . فالمتحرك غير متحرك . هذا خلف . فثبت : أن الحركة أمر موجود . ثم نقول : لا يخلو
As for the first type: it is mentioning the proof that motion consists of successive obtainments in contiguous loci, such that each of them is indivisible. So we say: That which indicates the soundness of what we have said are several proofs. The first proof: that we say: The body was not moving, then it became moving. So motion is an entity existing in itself. For if nothing occurred in the body while it was moving, it would be necessary to say that it is in itself after motion as it was before motion. But before motion it was non-moving, so after motion it is non-moving. Thus the moving thing is non-moving; this is a contradiction. So it is established that motion is an existing entity. Then we say: it is not free from...
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that motion is composed of indivisible positions. The author begins by stating the first type of proofs—those for motion being successive, indivisible obtainments. The proof itself starts by observing a body that was not moving and then becomes moving. The author argues that motion must be a real entity (amr mawjūd fī nafsihā), because if nothing new occurred in the body during motion, then after motion it would be identical to its state before motion, which was non-moving. But that would mean the moving body is non-moving, a contradiction. Therefore, motion is something that actually exists. The argument then leads into a discussion of how this motion must be composed of indivisible parts, but the text cuts off at 'then we say: it is not free from...'. This incomplete argument sets up the next step, presumably that the motion must consist of discrete steps.
