The Indivisible Point and the Atom (Jawhar Fard)
Conclusion linking proofs to the indivisible substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four proofs collectively establish the existence of the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) and how bodies are composed from such indivisible points.

Core Idea
The indivisible point is the building block of all bodies.

Having proven that the point of contact is indivisible, the author now argues that this indivisible point is the atom (jawhar fard). By rolling a sphere, we see that points compose lines, lines compose surfaces, and surfaces compose bodies. Thus, bodies are aggregates of indivisible substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding paragraph
Read the Arabic and translation for the final section. Note the phrase 'فيثبت بالبراهين الأربعة' which gathers the four proofs.
2
Follow the rolling ball argument
Imagine rolling a sphere on a plane. As it rolls, contact shifts from one point to the next with no gap between them.
This shows that points are contiguous without division.
3
Understand composition
From these contiguous points, a line is formed. From lines, a surface; from surfaces, a body.
This is the atomic theory of kalam.
4
Connect to jawhar fard
The indivisible point of contact is exactly the jawhar fard (indivisible substance). When aggregated, they form bodies.
Jawhar fard is the minimal unit of matter.
5
Reflect on the transition
Notice that the author moves from a geometrical proof to a metaphysical claim about the nature of matter.
This is typical of kalam arguments using physics to support theology.

Commentary
This passage presents a set of four geometrical proofs from Euclid and a concluding argument to establish that the point of contact between a sphere and a surface is indivisible, leading to the affirmation of the indivisible substance (jawhar fard), which is the atom in Islamic kalam.

The Epicycle Center Proof: Overlapping Points
Sanusi presents a proof from astronomy for the indivisible atom using epicycle center movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the movement of the epicycle center in an astronomical model demonstrates that points can overlap, supporting the existence of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Points in contact generate circles, proving indivisible atoms.

Sanusi now presents a second, more visual argument for the existence of indivisible atoms. He uses the movement of the epicycle center, a concept from Ptolemaic astronomy, to show that a circle can be generated solely from the contact of points. This argument relies on the idea that a point can only touch another point, leading to a series of overlapping points that form a continuous circle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'When... then...'
2
Understand the astronomical model
Visualize the deferent sphere (al-falak al-hamil) carrying the center of the epicycle. As the sphere moves, the point that is the epicycle center traces a path.
This is a simplified model; key term: مركز التدوير
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: The circle is formed by the point contacting something. What does it contact? Only a point. Therefore the circle is composed of overlapping points.
Ask: Why must the contact be point-to-point?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في تقرير هذا البرهان : إنا بينا : إذا تحرك الفلك الحامل المركز التدوير ، فإنه يرتسم من مركز التدوير دائرة وتلك الدائرة إنما تولدت من مماسة تلك النقطة ، التي هي مركز التدوير . والذي تماسه النقطة : نقطة . فتلك الدائرة إنما تولدت من النقطة المتشافعة
The second face in establishing this proof: We have shown that when the sphere carrying the epicycle center moves, then a circle is traced from the epicycle center. And that circle is generated only from the contact of that point, which is the center of the epicycle. And that which the point contacts is a point. So that circle is generated only from the overlapping point.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the second face of the same proof for the indivisible atom. It uses an astronomical example: the movement of the deferent sphere (al-falak al-hamil) carrying the center of an epicycle (markaz al-tadwir). As the sphere moves, the epicycle center traces a circle. The reasoning is that this circle is generated solely by the point of the epicycle center touching points along the path. Since a point can only contact another point (and not a line or surface), the generated circle must consist of overlapping points (al-nuqat al-mutashafi'a). This overlapping demonstrates that points can be adjacent without division, supporting the existence of the indivisible atom. The argument relies on the principle that contact occurs only between points, and a continuous line or circle formed by such contact implies a succession of points.

مركز التدوير
مماسة
نقطة متشافعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the circle is composed of infinitely divisible points and that point contact is impossible.
Correction: The proof argues that the circle is generated from the contact of a point with another point, and this contact produces a series of overlapping points, supporting the idea of indivisible atoms.

Potential vs Actual Infinite Division
Author refutes actual infinite division using analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why potential infinite divisibility does not entail the actual existence of infinite parts, through analogies from divine power and numbers.

Core Idea
Potential infinity is not actual infinity—endlessness in possibility does not mean endlessness in existence.

This passage tackles a common fallacy: if a body can be divided endlessly in thought, does that mean it actually contains an infinite number of parts? The author says no, and he supports his point with two powerful analogies from theology and mathematics. You will see how clear reasoning prevents a mistaken conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's logical flow.
The passage uses analogies, so look for how the comparisons work.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that a body never reaches a limit of smallness but always can be divided further. However, he immediately argues that this does not require all those divisions to be actual.
This is the core point: potential does not equal actual.
3
Examine the two analogies
First analogy: God's power over infinite things means He can endlessly create, but each created thing is finite. Second analogy: each individual number is possible, but the infinite set of all numbers is not actualizable. Both show that potential infinity does not require actual infinity.
Notice how the author uses theological and mathematical examples to support his reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ينتهي في الصغر إلى حد، إلا وهو بعد ذلك يقبل الانقسام. وهذا لا يقتضي أن تكون كل تلك التقسيمات ممكنة الحصول. كما أن المتكلمين لما قالوا: إنه تعالى قادر على ما لا نهاية له من المقدورات، لم يريدوا به: أنه تعالى يقدر على خلق ما لا نهاية له من المقدورات، بل أرادوا به: أنه تعالى لا ينتهي في تخليق الممكنات إلى حد، إلا ويمكنه الإيجاد والتخليق بعد ذلك. بمعنى: أنه يمكنه الإيجاد بعد الإيجاد والتكوين بعد التكوين. مع أن كل ما يخرج منها إلى الفعل، فهو متناه. فكذا هنا. وأيضاً فكل واحد من مراتب الأعداد، ممكن الحصول. ومجموع المراتب التي لا نهاية لها، ليست ممكنة الحصول. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ههنا كذلك؟
It does not end in smallness to a limit except that after that it accepts division. And this does not require that all those divisions are possible of obtaining. Just as the theologians, when they said: He, exalted, is powerful over what has no end of the things He is able to do, they did not mean by it: that He, exalted, is able to create what has no end of the things He is able to do, but they meant by it: that He, exalted, does not end in the creation of possible things to a limit except that He can bring into existence and create after that. Meaning: that He can bring into existence after bringing into existence and form after formation. While everything that emerges from them into actuality is finite. So likewise here. And also: every single one of the ranks of numbers is possible of obtaining, but the totality of the ranks that have no end is not possible of obtaining. So why is it not permissible that it be likewise here?
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a proof that potential infinite division does not entail actual infinite division. The author first states the nature of continuous divisibility: a body can always be divided further, but that does not make all those divisions actually realizable. He then provides an analogy from theology: when scholars say God is powerful over infinite things, they do not mean He can create an actually infinite number of things simultaneously; rather, His power never reaches a limit where He cannot create more, but each created thing is finite. Similarly, each individual number is possible, but the infinite set of all numbers is not actualizable. The author concludes by asking why the same reasoning should not apply to divisions of a body.

متناه
لا نهاية
مقدورات
مراتب الأعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because a body can be divided endlessly in thought, it must actually contain an infinite number of parts.
Correction: Potential infinity does not entail actual infinity. The author shows through analogies that endless possibility does not require simultaneous actual existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Second Proof: The Unity of Water and the Dilemma of Division
Sanusi presents a second proof using water to argue that division reveals pre-existing parts or creates new substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the example of water to demonstrate that division either separates pre-existing parts or creates new substances, leading to an absurd consequence.

Core Idea
Division either reveals pre-existing parts or annihilates and creates new substances.

Sanusi continues building arguments for the composition of bodies. He now presents a second proof rooted in our common experience of water. A single body of water, when divided, becomes two waters. The question is: were those two waters already present or not? The answer leads to a powerful dilemma that forces us to accept a key metaphysical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: premise, dilemma, first horn, second horn, absurd consequence, and an anticipated objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author presents two possibilities: either the two waters existed before the division, or they did not. Identify each horn and trace the logical consequences.
3
Analyze the absurd consequence
If the second horn is true (the waters did not exist before), then division annihilates the original and creates new ones. The analogy of a mosquito piercing the sea shows the absurdity: a tiny puncture would annihilate the entire sea and create a new one.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Consider the anticipated objection
The author anticipates an objection that although the corporeality is annihilated, the underlying matter (hayula) remains. The passage cuts off but promises two answers. Think about how you would respond.
This objection touches on the concept of prime matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات هذا المطلوب: أن نقول: الماء الواحد في الحس، لو كان في نفسه واحداً وحدة حقيقية. فإذا أوردنا التفريق عليه، حق صار ماءين. فإما أن يقال: إن كل واحد من هذين الماءين، كان حاصلا قبل ذلك التفريق، أو ما كان حاصلا. فإن كان الأول، فهذان الماءان كانا موجودين قبل هذا التفريق. فذلك الماء كان قبل ذلك التفريق مؤلفاً من هذين الجزءين، فكان التقسيم عبارة عن تفريق المتجاورين، لا عن إحداث الإثنينية. وهو المطلوب. وإن كان الثاني، وهو أن هذين الماءين الحاصلين بعد هذا التفريق، ما كانا حاصلين قبل هذا التفريق. فنقول: فعلى هذا القول، وجب القطع بأنه حدث هذان الماءان بعد هذا التقسيم. وبطل ذلك الماء، الذي كان واحداً قبل هذا التقسيم. وعلى هذا القول، فيكون التفريق إعداماً للجسم الأول، وإحداثاً للجسمين، الذين حصلا بعد هذا التقسيم. فعلى هذا القول، إذا طارت البعوضة على البحر المحيط، وشقت برأس إبرتها جزءاً من ماء البحر، لزم أن يقال: إنها أعدمت البحر الأول، وأوجدت بحر آخر. لأنه متى تفرق الاتصال في ذلك الموضع فقد فني أيضاً ما كان متصلاً به، بسبب الافتراق. وهلم جرا إلى آخر البحر. لا يقال: إن تلك الجسمية وإن عدمت إلا أن الهيولى باقية. لأنا نجيب بوجهين:
The second proof in establishing this claim: We say: The single water in sensation, if it were in itself one with a real unity, then when we introduce separation upon it, it truly becomes two waters. Then either it is said: that each one of these two waters was present before that separation, or it was not present. If the first, then these two waters were existing before this separation. So that water before the separation was composed of these two parts, so the division was an expression of separating the contiguous, not of producing duality. And that is what is required. And if the second—that these two waters that result after this separation were not present before this separation—then we say: On this statement, it must be concluded that these two waters came to be after this division, and that water which was one before this division ceased to exist. And on this statement, the separation would be an annihilation of the first body and a creation of the two bodies that come about after this division. On this statement, if a mosquito flew over the surrounding sea and pierced with the tip of its needle a part of the sea water, it would be necessary to say: it annihilated the first sea and brought into existence another sea. Because whenever the connection is severed in that place, that which was connected to it also perishes due to the separation, and so on to the end of the sea. It cannot be said: that the body-ness, even if it is annihilated, the matter (hayula) remains. For we answer with two aspects:
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof to establish the required conclusion (likely that bodies are composed of finite parts or that division creates new individuals). The author uses the example of a single body of water. If it is truly one, then when it divides into two, either the two waters existed before the division or they did not. If they existed, then the original water was composed of them, so division is merely separation of contiguous parts, which is the desired conclusion. If they did not exist, then division annihilates the original water and creates two new ones, leading to the absurd consequence that a mosquito could annihilate an entire sea and create a new one with a tiny puncture. The author then anticipates an objection: that the corporeality is annihilated but the underlying matter (hayula) persists. The answer begins but is cut off at the end of the page, promising two points.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Water remains essentially the same substance throughout division.
Correction: The proof shows that if the two waters were not pre-existing, division destroys the original water and creates new ones, so water does not retain its identity.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if the two waters after division did not exist before, what would be the consequence?
A) The first water remains unchanged
B) The first water is annihilated and two new waters are created
C) The division is only imaginary
D) The water is composed of atoms

Key Takeaway
This proof uses a dilemma to show that division of a body either reveals pre-existing parts or leads to the absurd consequence that a tiny action can annihilate an entire sea, thereby supporting the view that bodies are composed of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even a mosquito's needle can annihilate an entire sea in the absurd scenario, what does that say about your own small sins? Could a single moment of heedlessness destroy the spiritual state you have built? How can you ensure each action preserves rather than destroys your unity with Allah?
?
The proof shows that division either reveals pre-existing parts or creates something new. When you look at your own heart, do you see a single, undivided intention, or is it composed of competing desires? Which parts need to be separated from the whole to purify your faith?
?
Sanusi rejects the objection that underlying matter remains after annihilation. Is there something in your life that you treat as 'matter'—a persistent habit or trait you think remains harmless even when your spiritual body is broken? Will you still cling to that, or submit to the fact that even that must be transformed?

Infinite Creation and the Refutation of Democritus
The author refutes Democritus and states that infinite creation is not impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some philosophers accept the possibility of infinite creation and how the author refutes Democritus's opposing view.

Core Idea
Infinite creation is not impossible; rational souls are already infinite.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: can an infinite number of things be created? The author notes that many philosophers answer yes, pointing to the existence of infinite rational souls. He then turns to Democritus's opposing doctrine, declaring it false and promising a logical proof. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' argument
Read the passage noting why philosophers do not deem infinite creation impossible. Identify the example given (rational souls).
This example suggests actual infinity exists, making infinite creation plausible.
2
Learn the author's refutation of Democritus
The author asserts Democritus's view is false and promises a proof based on divisibility. Notice that the proof is not given here; it's a teaser.
This sets up a later argument about imaginary vs. actual division.
3
Connect to broader kalam goals
Consider how this discussion relates to debates about divine power and the nature of the created world.

خلق ما لا نهاية له ، وخروج المراتب التي لا نهاية لها من الأعداد إلى الوجود . فهذا عند الفلاسفة في الجملة غير ممتنع ، لأجل أن النفوس الناطقة الباقية ، في هذا الوقت أعداد لا نهاية لها. فثبت : أن هذا غير ممتنع في الجملة . وأما مذهب ديمقراطيس ، فباطل أيضاً . لأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن كل ما كان قابلاً للقسمة الوهمية ، وجب أن يكون قابلا للقسمة الانفكاكية وحينئذ يسقط هذا السؤال .
Creation of what has no end, and the emergence of infinite ranks from numbers into existence. So this, according to the philosophers in general, is not impossible, because the remaining rational souls at this time are infinite numbers. So it is established that this is not impossible in general. As for the doctrine of Democritus, it is false as well. For we will establish the proof that everything that is divisible in imagination must be divisible in actual separation, and then this question falls away.

This passage first states that philosophers do not deem it impossible to create an infinite number of things, citing the example of infinite rational souls. Then it declares that Democritus's view is false, promising to prove that anything imaginatively divisible must be actually separable, thus eliminating the objection. The passage functions as an introduction and a refutation of a specific opposing view.

The Water Argument Against Infinite Divisibility (Part 1)
The author presents the second proof using the water example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the water argument that demonstrates that if a body is divisible, its parts must either pre-exist or come into being through annihilation, leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
Division reveals either pre-existing parts or absurd annihilation.

This lesson presents the second proof against infinite divisibility, using a simple water example. The argument forces us to choose between two options, both of which lead to the conclusion that bodies are composed of finite parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of the water example as a concrete case.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two possibilities about the two waters after division: either they pre-existed or they are newly created. Find each one and understand the implications.
The first possibility leads to the conclusion that parts pre-exist; the second leads to absurdity.
3
Understand the absurd consequence
If the waters are newly created, then division annihilates the original and creates a new one. This would mean a mosquito's pinprick could annihilate the entire sea. Ask yourself why this is absurd.
This absurdity shows that the second possibility is false, so the first must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : الماء الواحد في الحس ، لو كان في نفسه واحداً وحدة حقيقية. فإذا أوردنا التفريق عليه ، حق صار ماءين . فإما أن يقال : إن كل واحد من هذين الماءين ، كان حاصلا قبل ذلك التفريق، أو ما كان حاصلا . فإن كان الأول، فهذان الماءان كانا موجودين قبل هذا التفريق . فذلك الماء كان قبل ذلك التفريق مؤلفاً من هذين الجزءين ، فكان التقسيم عبارة عن تفريق المتجاورين ، لا عن إحداث الإثنينية . وهو المطلوب. وإن كان الثاني . وهو أن هذين الماءين الحاصلين بعد هذا التفريق ، ما كانا حاصلين قبل هذا التفريق . فنقول : فعلى هذا القول ، وجب القطع بأنه حدث هذان الماءان بعد هذا التقسيم . وبطل ذلك الماء ، الذي كان واحداً قبل هذا التقسيم . وعلى هذا القول ، فيكون التفريق إعداماً للجسم الأول ، وإحداثاً للجسمين ، اللذين حصلا بعد هذا التقسيم . فعلى هذا القول . إذا طارت البعوضة على البحر المحيط ، وشقت برأس إبرتها جزءاً من ماء البحر ، لزم أن يقال : إنها أعدمت البحر الأول ، وأوجدت بحر آخر . لأنه متى تفرق الاتصال في ذلك الموضع فقد فني أيضاً ما كان متصلاً به، بسبب الافتراق . وهلم جرا إلى آخر البحر . لا يقال : إن تلك الجسمية وإن عدمت إلا أن الهيولى باقية . لأنا نجيب بوجهين : الأول : أن نقول : الهيولى ليست تمام ماهية الجسم ، بل هي أحد أجزاء ماهية الجسم. ولا يكفي في بقاء الشيء ، بقاء جزء من أجزاء ماهيته فثبت : أن الذي ذكرناه لازم . والوجه الثاني في الجواب : إن ماء البحر ، حين كان واحداً . إما أن يقال : إن هيولاه كانت واحدة ، أو ما كانت واحدة . فإن كانت واحدة ، فعند انقسام الجسم. إما أن يقال : بقيت تلك الهيولى واحدة كما كانت ، أو انقسمت وتعددت ومحال أن تبقى واحدة ، وإلا لزم أن يقال : الماء الذي بالمشرق ، والماء الذي بالمغرب : ذات واحدة ، قامت به صفتان ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ولأنه يلزم اجتماع الأمثال في المحال . لأن الأعداد من الجسمية أمور متماثلة في تمام الماهية . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن يقال : لما صار الماء الواحد ماءين : صارت تلك الهيولى الواحدة أيضاً اثنتين . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير . كما أن إيراد التقسيم على الماء الواحد ، يوجب إعدام الماء الأول وحدوث ماءين آخرين ، فكذلك هذا التقسيم ، يوجب إعدام الهيولى الأولى وحدوث فردين آخرين من الهيولي . وحينئذ يكون التقسيم إعداماً للجسم الأول ، بحسب هيولاه وبحسب صورته . فيكون التفريق إعداماً له بالكلية . هذا إذا قلنا : إن الجسم عندما كان واحداً ، كانت هيولاه واحدة . أما إذا قلنا : إنه عندما كان الجسم واحداً ، كانت الهيوليات متعددة ، بحسب عدد الانقسامات الممكنة فيه. فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير تكون الجسمية القائمة بكل واحدة من تلك الهيوليات، مغايرة للجسمية القائمة بالهيولى الأخرى ، لامتناع قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمحال الكثيرة . وعلى هذا التقدير فقد كانت الكثرة موجودة بالفعل ، قبل ورود التقسيم . فوجب كون تلك الأجزاء موجودة بالفعل . فثبت : أن الجسم لو كان قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لكانت الأجزاء التي لا نهاية لها ، حاصلة فيه بالفعل . وذلك هو المطلوب
The second proof for establishing this sought conclusion: we say: the single water in sensation, if in itself it is one with a real unity, and then we introduce division upon it, it becomes two waters. So either it is said that each one of these two waters was present before that division, or they were not present. If the first, then these two waters were existent before this division. So that water was before that division composed of these two parts, so the division was an expression of separating contiguous things, not of creating duality. And that is the sought conclusion. And if the second, that these two waters obtained after this division were not present before this division. So we say: according to this statement, it is necessary to be certain that these two waters occur after this division, and that water which was one before this division is annihilated. And according to this statement, the division is an annihilation of the first body and a creation of the two bodies that obtained after this division. According to this statement, if a mosquito flies over the surrounding sea and pierces with the tip of its needle a part of the sea water, it must be said that it annihilated the first sea and brought into existence another sea. Because whenever continuity is separated in that place, what was connected to it also perishes due to the separation. And so on to the end of the sea. Let it not be said that that corporeality, even if it is annihilated, the hyle remains. For we answer in two ways: The first: we say that hyle is not the entire essence of the body, but rather it is one of the parts of the essence of the body. And the persistence of a part of the parts of its essence is not sufficient for the persistence of the thing. So it is established that what we mentioned is necessary. The second way in the answer: when the sea water was one, either it is said that its hyle was one or it was not one. If it was one, then at the division of the body, either it is said that that hyle remained one as it was, or it divided and became multiple. And it is impossible that it remains one, otherwise it would be said that the water in the east and the water in the west are one essence that possesses two attributes, and it is known that that is false. And because it would entail the conjunction of similars in one locus. Because the individuals of corporeality are similar things in the complete essence. As for the second division, which is that it is said: when the one water becomes two waters, that one hyle also becomes two. We say: according to this supposition, just as introducing division upon the one water necessitates the annihilation of the first water and the occurrence of two other waters, likewise this division necessitates the annihilation of the first hyle and the occurrence of two other individuals of hyle. And then the division is an annihilation of the first body with respect to its hyle and with respect to its form. So the separation is an annihilation of it entirely. This is if we say that when the body was one, its hyle was one. But if we say that when the body was one, the hylés were multiple according to the number of possible divisions in it. We say: according to this supposition, the corporeality subsisting in each one of those hylés is other than the corporeality subsisting in the other hyle, because it is impossible for one attribute to subsist in multiple loci. And according to this supposition, multiplicity already existed in actuality before the occurrence of division. So it is necessary that those parts exist in actuality. So it is established that if the body were subject to divisions without end, then the parts without end would be actually present in it. And that is the sought conclusion.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the conclusion that a body cannot be infinitely divisible in a way that infinite parts actually exist. It uses the example of water: a single body of water, when divided, becomes two waters. Two possibilities are considered: either the two waters pre-existed or they did not. If they pre-existed, division merely separates contiguous parts, confirming the existence of parts. If they did not pre-exist, then division annihilates the original body and creates new ones, leading to absurd consequences (e.g., a mosquito's pinprick annihilating the entire sea). An objection is raised that matter (hyle) persists even if the corporeality is annihilated. The author answers with two arguments. First, hyle is not the entire essence of the body, so its persistence does not preserve the body's identity. Second, a detailed analysis of the hyle's state: whether it remains one or becomes multiple, each option leads to a contradiction or to the conclusion that infinite parts are already actual. The proof ends by affirming that if a body were infinitely divisible, infinite parts would actually exist in it.

Key Terms
قسمة
إعدام
إحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the water argument conclude about the two waters after division?
A) They must be newly created.
B) They must have pre-existed before division.
C) They are identical to the original water.
D) They are made of hyle.

Key Takeaway
Division does not create new parts; it only separates pre-existing ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What area of your life have you treated as 'infinitely divisible'—believing you can keep dividing your attention, time, or commitment without ever reaching a real part that matters? How does this proof expose that lie?
?
Consider a small habit you think is harmless. Could it be that this 'small division' is actually annihilating a greater spiritual state within you, just as a mosquito's bite would annihilate the entire sea? What is that habit, and what will you do about it today?
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes act as if you can create a new beginning without addressing the pre-existing state of your heart? The proof shows that division either reveals what is already there or destroys it. How does this challenge your approach to repentance?

Refuting the Hyle Objection to the Water Argument
The author answers the hyle objection with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that matter (hyle) persists does not salvage infinite divisibility, and how the author refutes it.

Core Idea
Even if matter remains, the body's identity is not preserved.

An opponent might claim that the original matter remains after division, so the body isn't truly annihilated. This lesson examines two responses that show the objection fails, leading again to the conclusion that infinite parts would be actual.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answers
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection that hyle persists, followed by two answers.
The objection is introduced by 'لا يقال'.
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer argues that hyle is not the entire essence of a body, so its persistence does not preserve the body's identity. Division still annihilates the original body.
This answer is concise: hyle is only part of the essence.
3
Understand the second answer
The second answer delves deeper: if the hyle remained one after division, absurd consequences follow (water could be in two places at once). If the hyle becomes multiple, then division still annihilates the original hyle, confirming the argument.
This answer covers both subcases.

. وبطل ذلك الماء ، الذي كان واحداً قبل هذا التقسيم . وعلى هذا القول ، فيكون التفريق إعداماً للجسم الأول ، وإحداثاً للجسمين ، اللذين حصلا بعد هذا التقسيم . فعلى هذا القول . إذا طارت البعوضة على البحر المحيط ، وشقت برأس إبرتها جزءاً من ماء البحر ، لزم أن يقال : إنها أعدمت البحر الأول ، وأوجدت بحر آخر . لأنه متى تفرق الاتصال في ذلك الموضع فقد فني أيضاً ما كان متصلاً به، بسبب الافتراق . وهلم جرا إلى آخر البحر . لا يقال : إن تلك الجسمية وإن عدمت إلا أن الهيولى باقية . لأنا نجيب بوجهين : الأول : أن نقول : الهيولى ليست تمام ماهية الجسم ، بل هي أحد أجزاء ماهية الجسم. ولا يكفي في بقاء الشيء ، بقاء جزء من أجزاء ماهيته فثبت : أن الذي ذكرناه لازم . والوجه الثاني في الجواب : إن ماء البحر ، حين كان واحداً . إما أن يقال : إن هيولاه كانت واحدة ، أو ما كانت واحدة . فإن كانت واحدة ، فعند انقسام الجسم. إما أن يقال : بقيت تلك الهيولى واحدة كما كانت ، أو انقسمت وتعددت ومحال أن تبقى واحدة ، وإلا لزم أن يقال : الماء الذي بالمشرق ، والماء الذي بالمغرب : ذات واحدة ، قامت به صفتان ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ولأنه يلزم اجتماع الأمثال في المحال . لأن الأعداد من الجسمية أمور متماثلة في تمام الماهية . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن يقال : لما صار الماء الواحد ماءين : صارت تلك الهيولى الواحدة أيضاً اثنتين . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير . كما أن إيراد التقسيم على الماء الواحد ، يوجب إعدام الماء الأول وحدوث ماءين آخرين ، فكذلك هذا التقسيم ، يوجب إعدام الهيولى الأولى وحدوث فردين آخرين من الهيولي . وحينئذ يكون التقسيم إعداماً للجسم الأول ، بحسب هيولاه وبحسب صورته . فيكون التفريق إعداماً له بالكلية . هذا إذا قلنا : إن الجسم عندما كان واحداً ، كانت هيولاه واحدة . أما إذا قلنا : إنه عندما كان الجسم واحداً ، كانت الهيوليات متعددة ، بحسب عدد الانقسامات الممكنة فيه. فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير تكون الجسمية القائمة بكل واحدة من تلك الهيوليات، مغايرة للجسمية القائمة بالهيولى الأخرى ، لامتناع قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمحال الكثيرة . وعلى هذا التقدير فقد كانت الكثرة موجودة بالفعل ، قبل ورود التقسيم . فوجب كون تلك الأجزاء موجودة بالفعل . فثبت : أن الجسم لو كان قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لكانت الأجزاء التي لا نهاية لها ، حاصلة فيه بالفعل . وذلك هو المطلوب
The second proof for establishing this sought conclusion: we say: the single water in sensation, if in itself it is one with a real unity, and then we introduce division upon it, it becomes two waters. So either it is said that each one of these two waters was present before that division, or they were not present. If the first, then these two waters were existent before this division. So that water was before that division composed of these two parts, so the division was an expression of separating contiguous things, not of creating duality. And that is the sought conclusion. And if the second, that these two waters obtained after this division were not present before this division. So we say: according to this statement, it is necessary to be certain that these two waters occur after this division, and that water which was one before this division is annihilated. And according to this statement, the division is an annihilation of the first body and a creation of the two bodies that obtained after this division. According to this statement, if a mosquito flies over the surrounding sea and pierces with the tip of its needle a part of the sea water, it must be said that it annihilated the first sea and brought into existence another sea. Because whenever continuity is separated in that place, what was connected to it also perishes due to the separation. And so on to the end of the sea. Let it not be said that that corporeality, even if it is annihilated, the hyle remains. For we answer in two ways: The first: we say that hyle is not the entire essence of the body, but rather it is one of the parts of the essence of the body. And the persistence of a part of the parts of its essence is not sufficient for the persistence of the thing. So it is established that what we mentioned is necessary. The second way in the answer: when the sea water was one, either it is said that its hyle was one or it was not one. If it was one, then at the division of the body, either it is said that that hyle remained one as it was, or it divided and became multiple. And it is impossible that it remains one, otherwise it would be said that the water in the east and the water in the west are one essence that possesses two attributes, and it is known that that is false. And because it would entail the conjunction of similars in one locus. Because the individuals of corporeality are similar things in the complete essence. As for the second division, which is that it is said: when the one water becomes two waters, that one hyle also becomes two. We say: according to this supposition, just as introducing division upon the one water necessitates the annihilation of the first water and the occurrence of two other waters, likewise this division necessitates the annihilation of the first hyle and the occurrence of two other individuals of hyle. And then the division is an annihilation of the first body with respect to its hyle and with respect to its form. So the separation is an annihilation of it entirely. This is if we say that when the body was one, its hyle was one. But if we say that when the body was one, the hylés were multiple according to the number of possible divisions in it. We say: according to this supposition, the corporeality subsisting in each one of those hylés is other than the corporeality subsisting in the other hyle, because it is impossible for one attribute to subsist in multiple loci. And according to this supposition, multiplicity already existed in actuality before the occurrence of division. So it is necessary that those parts exist in actuality. So it is established that if the body were subject to divisions without end, then the parts without end would be actually present in it. And that is the sought conclusion.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the conclusion that a body cannot be infinitely divisible in a way that infinite parts actually exist. It uses the example of water: a single body of water, when divided, becomes two waters. Two possibilities are considered: either the two waters pre-existed or they did not. If they pre-existed, division merely separates contiguous parts, confirming the existence of parts. If they did not pre-exist, then division annihilates the original body and creates new ones, leading to absurd consequences (e.g., a mosquito's pinprick annihilating the entire sea). An objection is raised that matter (hyle) persists even if the corporeality is annihilated. The author answers with two arguments. First, hyle is not the entire essence of the body, so its persistence does not preserve the body's identity. Second, a detailed analysis of the hyle's state: whether it remains one or becomes multiple, each option leads to a contradiction or to the conclusion that infinite parts are already actual. The proof ends by affirming that if a body were infinitely divisible, infinite parts would actually exist in it.

Key Terms
هيولى
ماهية
إعدام
إحداث
اجتماع الأمثال
محال كثيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the persistence of hyle after division saves the body from annihilation.
Correction: The author shows that hyle is only part of the essence, and its persistence does not preserve the body. Moreover, analysis shows that either the hyle is annihilated or already multiple.

Checkpoint
What is the first answer to the hyle objection?
A) Hyle is the entire essence of the body.
B) Hyle is only one part of the essence, so its persistence does not preserve the body.
C) Hyle cannot persist because it is indivisible.
D) Hyle is annihilated with the body.

Key Takeaway
The hyle objection fails because matter persistence does not entail body persistence, and further analysis shows that infinite divisibility would still lead to actual infinite parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that your body's identity is not preserved by the persistence of matter—that even if the underlying stuff remains, the body of a moment ago is gone. Do you live as if your physical form is permanent or as if it is constantly being replaced? How would your daily choices change if you truly felt the weight of this annihilation every moment?
?
The author shows that if matter remained unchanged, the water in the east and west would have to be the same essence. What about your own sense of self? Do you assume that because your body 'feels' continuous, your identity is fixed? Where in your life are you clinging to a false sense of permanence—relationships, status, health—that this argument exposes as illusion?
?
The proof concludes that infinite divisibility leads to actual infinite parts, an absurdity. In your spiritual life, do you try to divide your attention among infinite distractions, assuming you can keep them all without losing yourself? What does this logical impossibility teach you about the need for focus and submission to the One who alone is truly continuous?

Al-Nazzam's Objection: Leaping (Tafra)
Razi reports al-Nazzam's objection that the mover can leap over parts of the distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's objection that motion can occur by leaping (ṭafra) and how he uses an argument from parity of plausibility to defend it.

Core Idea
Al-Nazzam argues that if one implausibility is accepted, another should be.

Al-Nazzam responds to Abu al-Hudhayl's proof by introducing the concept of leaping (ṭafra), where a mover skips over parts of the distance. He acknowledges this is implausible but argues that the atomists already accept a similar implausibility—the crumbling of a millstone from indivisible atoms. This sets up a parity argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: objection, statement of leaping, and the parity argument.
The Arabic has minor OCR issues but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the core argument
Al-Nazzam claims the impossibility only applies if the mover traverses all parts. He introduces leaping to avoid that. Find where he defines leaping.
The definition: 'transition to a distant part without passing through what is between them'.
3
Analyze the parity argument
Note that al-Nazzam admits leaping is 'extremely remote' (baʿīd jiddan) but points out that atomists already accept a remote consequence: the millstone crumbling. Why does he think this makes his argument acceptable?
This is an argument from parity: if you accept one implausibility, you must accept another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن : أبا الهذيل العلاف، لما احتج بهذا الدليل على ( النظام ، أجاب النظام عنه : بأن المحال إنما يلزم لو كان المتحرك : تحرك على جميع أجزا المسافة . وليس الأمر كذلك ، بل إنه تحرك على بعضها ، وطفر على الباقي والمراد من الطفر : انتقال المتحرك إلى الجزء البعيد عنه ، من غير أن يمر بما . قال : ( والقول بالطفر ، وإن كان بعيداً جداً ، إلا أن القول بإثبات الجوهر الفرد ، يلزم عليه تفكك حجر الرحى . فإذا جاز لكم التزام ذلك المستبعد ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً التزام مثل هذا المستبعد ؟ ) .
And know that Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, when he used this proof against al-Nazzam, al-Nazzam replied to him: that the impossibility only follows if the moving body moved over all the parts of the distance. But it is not so; rather it moves over some of them and leaps over the rest. The meaning of leaping (ṭafra) is the mover's transition to a distant part without passing through what is between them. He said: 'The doctrine of leaping, although extremely remote, yet the doctrine of affirming the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) entails the crumbling of a millstone. So if you permit committing to that remote thing, why should you not also permit committing to such a remote thing?'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the objection raised by al-Nazzam against the first proof. Al-Nazzam argues that the impossibility of traversing an infinite distance only applies if the mover must pass through every intermediate point. He introduces the concept of ṭafra (leaping), where the mover skips over some parts and lands on farther ones. He acknowledges that this idea is implausible (baʿīd jiddan), but he counters that the proponents of atomism (such as Abu al-Hudhayl) already accept a similar implausibility: that the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) would cause a millstone to disintegrate (because it would be composed of discrete parts that can separate). Since they accept that counterintuitive consequence, they should also accept the leap. This is a tuq (argument by tuq) that if one implausibility is accepted, another should be allowed. The objection challenges the necessity of continuous motion through all points, proposing that motion can occur in jumps, thus avoiding the need to traverse an infinite number of parts.

Key Terms
طفر
الجوهر الفرد
تفكك حجر الرحى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a full refutation of atomism.
Correction: It is not a refutation; it is an argument from parity of plausibility, claiming that if atomists accept one implausibility they should accept another.

Checkpoint
What concept does al-Nazzam introduce to avoid traversing all parts of the distance?
A) Infinite speed
B) Leaping (ṭafra)
C) Disappearance of parts
D) Simultaneous motion

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's leaping objection challenges the necessity of traversing all points, using a parity argument to defend its plausibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nazzam challenged his opponent to accept a far-fetched idea because he already accepted another equally far-fetched one. Is there a belief you hold that you have never questioned, even though it leads to strange implications? Would you be willing to examine it with the same honesty you expect from others?
?
When you encounter a difficult truth in your faith or life, do you sometimes dismiss it because it seems too strange (like the leap), while holding onto other beliefs that are equally puzzling (like the millstone)? What would it take for you to confront that inconsistency and seek the truth, no matter how remote it appears?

Empirical Evidence Against Leaping
Razi gives empirical proof: ink on a moving finger leaves a continuous line, disproving leaps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses empirical observation to refute the actual occurrence of leaping and the logical structure of his argument.

Core Idea
Continuous motion disproves leaps: ink on a moving finger leaves no gaps.

Razi strengthens his refutation of leaping by appealing to observable reality. He presents a simple experiment: if you dip your finger in ink and draw it across a surface, the resulting continuous line shows that every point was touched. This argument not only refutes the opponent's claim but also illustrates the harmony between rational demonstration and empirical evidence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument from the empirical observation to the conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The passage contains Razi's initial claim (the experiment and conclusion), the opponent's objection (diffusion of ink), and Razi's reply (finite vs infinite parts). Write down each part in your own words.
Notice that the opponent's objection is an attempt to explain away the continuous line.
3
Analyze Razi's counter-argument
Focus on Razi's reply using the finite vs infinite distinction. Why does this effectively refute the opponent's objection? Relate it to the larger discussion about leaping.
This is a key example of combined empirical and rational reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن بتقدير أن يكون الطفر ممكناً في الجملة ، لكنه غير واقع . لأنا إذا لطخنا الإصبع بالمداد ، ثم أمررناه على المسافة من أولها إلى آخرها . فإنه يحصل هناك خط مستقيم أسود . وذلك يقتضي أن المتحرك قد مس جميع أجزاء المسافة . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه حصل السواد في بعض أجزاء المسافة ، بسبب حصول المماسة ، ولم يحصل في الباقي بسبب الطفرة . ثم إن تلك الأجزاء اختلط بعضها بالبعض ، ولا يميز الحس بينها . فلا جرم يرى الكل على لون السواد ؟ . فنقول : إن بتقدير أن يصح القول بالطفرة ، فإن الأجزاء التي صارت ممسوسة متناهية ، والأجزاء التي وقعت الطفرة عليها غير متناهية . فالنوع الأول من الأجزاء قليلة جداً بالنسبة إلى النوع الثاني منها . فإذا كان المتلطخ بالسواد هو النوع الأول فقط ، وجب أن تبقى تلك الأجزاء القليلة في تلك الأجزاء الكثيرة . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا : أن كل الأجزاء صارت ممسوسة عند الحركة . وحينئذ يعود الإلزام .
The second: Even granting that leaping is possible in general, it does not actually occur. Because if we smear the finger with ink, then pass it over the distance from its beginning to its end, a straight black line is produced. This entails that the mover has touched all parts of the distance. If they say: 'Why may it not be said that blackness occurs in some parts of the distance due to contact (mumāssa), and does not occur in the rest due to leaping, then those parts intermingle with each other and the sense cannot distinguish between them, so the whole appears colored black?' We say: Even granting that the doctrine of leaping is valid, the parts that have been touched are finite, and the parts over which leaping occurred are infinite. So the first type of parts are very few relative to the second type. If the part smeared with black is only the first type, then those few parts would have to remain [visible] among those many parts. But since the matter is not so, we know that all parts become touched during motion. Then the objection returns (i.e., the original proof stands).
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is the second answer (al-wajh al-thānī) from Razi against the leaping objection. It provides empirical evidence that leaping does not occur in actual motion. The argument uses a simple experiment: if you dip your finger in ink and draw it across a surface, you get a continuous straight black line. This shows that the finger touched every part of the surface; otherwise, the line would have gaps. The opponent might object that the ink could have spread from the touched parts to the untouched ones by diffusion (ikhtilāṭ), making the whole appear continuous. Razi counters that if the touched parts are finite and the untouched parts are infinite, then the touched parts would be negligible in comparison; they would not be enough to cover the entire line uniformly. Since we observe a uniform continuous line, we conclude that all parts were touched. Therefore, leaping does not occur, and the original proof (that a finite body cannot have infinite parts due to the necessity of traversing all parts) remains valid. This is a powerful combination of empirical observation and rational argumentation, typical of kalām methodology.

Key Terms
مداد
إصبع
خط أسود
مماسة
طفرة
اختلاط
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that leaping is absolutely impossible in all circumstances.
Correction: Razi only argues that leaping does not actually occur in observed motion, not that it is logically impossible. The argument is empirical, not a priori.

Checkpoint
What empirical evidence does Razi use to refute the actual occurrence of leaping?
A) A moving finger with ink leaves a continuous line.
B) A ball thrown in the air leaves a visible trail.
C) Atoms combine to form larger objects.
D) The millstone example from al-Nazzam.

Key Takeaway
Razi's ink experiment demonstrates that actual motion involves continuous contact, refuting the claim that leaping occurs in observed phenomena.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the consistent, predictable patterns in nature—like the continuous line from ink—does it ever remind you that the universe runs by the command of a single, wise Creator? How does this awareness change the way you approach your daily routines? What one habit can you adopt to turn everyday observations into moments of faith-strengthening reflection?
?
Razi uses a simple experiment to dismantle a philosophical objection. Are there intellectual doubts or abstract arguments you hold onto that might be resolved by looking at the clear signs Allah has placed in the world? Will you commit to examining one doubt this week through the lens of sensory evidence and the Qur'an?
?
The finite cannot cover the infinite. Razi's argument exposes the futility of trying to cover infinite gaps with finite means. In your life, where are you trying to satisfy an infinite longing (for meaning, peace, or happiness) with finite things? What step can you take today to reorient your pursuit toward the Infinite—Allah Himself?

First Proof for the Indivisible Atom and Refutation of Ibn Sina's Objection
Al-Matalib presents the first proof for the indivisible atom and counters Ibn Sina's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the indivisible atom based on the absurdity of infinite division and how Ibn Sina's objection is refuted.

Core Idea
Infinite division leads to absurdity, but atomism avoids it.

This lesson presents a foundational argument in classical Islamic atomism. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if bodies were infinitely divisible, a tiny mustard seed could yield enough matter to cover the entire cosmos many times over—an obvious impossibility. He then addresses Ibn Sina's counterargument that the same absurdity might still hold even with atoms. The response shows that the impossibility is only necessary for the infinite divisibility view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the three parts: proof, objection, and response.
2
Identify the reductio structure
Focus on the conditional: 'If body were infinitely divisible, then absurd consequence.' This is the core of the proof.
The absurdity is that a mustard seed could cover the heavens and earth many times over.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's objection
Ibn Sina claims that the same consequence follows from atomism. Ask yourself why he thinks that.
He argues that even with indivisible parts, a seed could theoretically yield many sheets.
4
Analyze the response
The author distinguishes between what is known necessarily (the atomist knows it's impossible) and what is only possible under infinite division. See how the impossibility is pinned on infinite divisibility.
The response emphasizes that the infinite divisibilist must admit the consequence as actually possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن الجسم لو كان قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لجاز أن ينفصل من الخردلة الواحدة ، صفائح يغشى بها أطباق العرش والكرم والسموات والأرضين ، لا مرة واحدة ، بل ألف مرة . ومعلوم أن ذلك في غاية البعد
قال الشيخ الرئيس : ( هذا أيضاً لازم على القائلين بإثبات الجوهر الفرد . لأن مع القول بكون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، لا يبعد أن يحصل في الخردلة صفائح يغشى بها وجه السموات والأرضين . وإذا كان هذا المحذور لازماً على الكل ، فقد زال الاستبعاد ) . والجواب عنه : إن مع القول بإثبات الجوهر الفرد ، نعلم بالضرورة : أن ذلك محال . سلمنا : أنه يبقى احتماله ، إلا أنه احتمال ، لا يعرف ، أنه هل هو ممكن ، أو ممتنع في نفسه ؟ وإن كنا لا نعرف امتناعه - أما على القول بنفي الجوهر الفرد ، فإنا نقطع بأن هذا الذي ألزمناه ، يكون ممكن الوقوع لا مرة واحدة ، بل مراراً لا نهاية فا لأنه لا صفحة توجد من تلك الخردلة ، إلا وهي قابلة لانقسامات غير متناهية . وكل واحد منها ، فإنه يقبل الانقسامات التي لا نهاية لها مرة واحدة فثبت : أن هذا المحال ، إنما يلزم على قول من يقول : إن الجسم يقبل انقسامات لا نهاية لها .
The first proof: If body were divisible infinitely, it would be possible for one mustard seed to yield sheets covering the layers of the Throne, the [divine] generosity, the heavens, and the earths, not once but a thousand times. And it is known that this is extremely far-fetched. The Chief Master (Ibn Sina) said: 'This is also necessary for those who affirm the indivisible atom. Because with the statement that body is composed of indivisible parts, it is not far-fetched that a mustard seed could yield sheets covering the face of the heavens and the earths. And if this objection is necessary for all, then the improbability is removed.' The answer to it: With the affirmation of the indivisible atom, we know necessarily that this is impossible. We concede that its possibility remains, but it is a possibility that is not known whether it is possible or impossible in itself. And even if we do not know its impossibility — as for the denial of the indivisible atom, we are certain that what we have required is possibly actual, not once but infinitely many times, because no sheet from that mustard seed exists except that it is subject to infinite divisions. And each one of them is subject to infinite divisions once. So it is established that this impossibility is only necessary on the statement of one who says that body is subject to infinite divisions.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The proof is a reductio ad absurdum of infinite divisibility: if bodies were infinitely divisible, a single mustard seed could yield enough sheets to cover the heavens and earth many times over—an obviously absurd consequence. Then Ibn Sina objects that the same absurdity also follows from atomism, since even indivisible parts can be arranged into vast sheets. The response distinguishes: the atomist knows necessarily that such a consequence is impossible, while the infinite divisibilist must admit it is possible and actual because each sheet itself admits infinite division. Thus the absurdity only hits the infinite divisibilist.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
انقسام لا نهاية له
محال
خردلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The atomist's view also leads to the absurdity of a mustard seed covering the heavens.
Correction: The atomist knows necessarily that such a covering is impossible because the seed does not contain actual infinite parts; only the infinite divisibility view entails actual infinite divisions.

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the first proof for the indivisible atom?
A) Bodies are composed of indivisible parts because infinite division leads to absurd consequences.
B) The mustard seed is larger than the heavens.
C) Ibn Sina proved that atoms cannot exist.
D) The sheets covering the heavens are made from atoms.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the indivisible atom is proven by the reductio that infinite divisibility leads to absurd consequences, and only the atomist view avoids this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that some truths are known by necessity, even if we cannot fully imagine how they work. Where in your life are you acting on certainty without full comprehension? Are you willing to accept a truth that reason demonstrates, even if it seems strange to your imagination?
?
Ibn Sina's objection sounded plausible but was based on a flawed assumption. How do you handle objections to your faith that seem logical but may rest on hidden premises? Do you take time to dissect arguments, or do you allow doubt to shake your convictions?
?
The atomist knows necessarily that the absurd consequence is impossible. Reflect on a core belief you hold about Allah or the unseen. Why is it necessary for you? Based on what evidence? Do you treat it with the same certainty as the author treats the impossibility of infinite sheets from a mustard seed?

Euclid's Geometric Proof Against Infinite Divisibility
Al-Matalib presents Euclid's second geometric proof against infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's geometric theorem about angles between a tangent and a chord is used to argue that infinite divisibility of magnitude is impossible.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility leads to an impossible race between angles that can never meet.

This second proof from Euclid uses a geometric construction involving circles and angles to show that assuming infinite divisibility forces a contradiction. The proof relies on a known proposition from Euclid's Elements which establishes that certain angles are always larger or smaller than any acute rectilinear angle, leading to an irreconcilable gap when we try to make them meet through infinite division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the proof is structured: first a short statement, then a detailed explanation.
The passage references Euclid's Elements, Book III, Proposition 15. You don't need to know the exact proposition, but understand that it provides the foundation for the angle relationships.
2
Identify the key geometric elements
Focus on the two angles: the external angle (between the perpendicular and the arc) and the internal angle (inside the circle). Note their properties: one is always smaller than any acute rectilinear angle, the other always larger.
These two angles cannot become equal regardless of the size of the circle.
3
Follow the iteration argument
The proof imagines drawing a series of smaller and smaller circles inside the first circle, each tangent at the same point. As the circles shrink, the external angle grows and the internal angle shrinks. If infinite divisibility were possible, this process could continue infinitely.
But the external angle can never become as large as the internal angle because they are bounded by the acute rectilinear angle.
4
Understand the contradiction
The argument shows that even with infinite steps, the external angle can never catch up to the internal angle. This violates the idea that with infinite divisibility you should be able to approach any magnitude. Thus, infinite divisibility leads to an impossibility.
The contradiction is that the series of angles would require the external to eventually equal the internal, but it never can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن القول بقبول القسمة إلى غير النهاية ، يقتضي وجود مقدارين مختلفين في العظم . ثم إن الزائد يتناقض إلى غير النهاية ، والناقص يتزايد إلى غير النهاية . ثم لا يبلغ هذا الناقص مع تزايده أبداً إلى حد ذلك الزائد ، مع تناقصه أبداً . ومعلوم أن ذلك بعيد في العقول
·
بيانه : أنه ثبت في الشكل الخامس عشر ، من المقالة الثالثة من كتاب أقليدس ، أنه إذا أخرج من طرف قطر دائرة ، خط على زاوية قائمة . فإن الزاوية التي يحيط بها ذلك الخط ، مع حدبة الدائرة ، أصغر من كل زاوية حادة مستقيمة الخطين وأن الزاوية الحاصلة من القطر، ومن نصف الدائرة الواقعة في داخل الدائرة ، أعظم من كل زاوية حادة مستقيمة الخطين . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنا إذا علمنا على قطر دائرة ، دائرة أخرى أصغر منها ، مماسة لها ، على النقطة التي هي طرف العمود ، كانت الزاوية التي تحدث من العمود ، ومن حدبة الدائرة الصغرى ، أوسع من الزاوية الأولى ، وصارت الزاوية الواقعة في داخل الدائرة ، أضيق . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : كلما كانت الدائرة أصغر كانت الزاوية الخارجة أوسع ، والداخلة أضيق . فلو كان المقدار قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لأمكن أن يرتسم عند طرف العمود ، دوائر لا نهاية لها ، كل واحدة أصغر من التي قبلها . وحينئذ يلزم منه أن تتزايد الزوايا الخارجة إلى غير النهاية، وأن تتصاغر الزاوية الداخلة إلى غير النهاية . ثم إن تلك الخارجة لا تصير مثل هذه الداخلة . لأن تلك الخارجة كيف كانت أصغر من كل زاوية حادة مستقيمة الخطين. وهذه الداخلة كيف كانت أكبر من كل زاوية حادة مستقيمة الخطين. فثبت : أن المحال الذي ألزمناه : لازم . وأيضاً إن الزاوية الحاصلة من العمود ، ومن حدبة الدائرة : تقبل التزايد إلى غير النهاية . والزاوية الحادة الحاصلة من الخطين المستقيمين تقبل القسمة إلى غير النهاية .
The second proof: The statement of accepting division ad infinitum necessitates the existence of two magnitudes different in size. Then the larger decreases ad infinitum, and the smaller increases ad infinitum. Yet this smaller, with its increase, never reaches the limit of that larger, with its decrease. And it is known that this is far-fetched in intellects.
Its explanation: It is established in the fifteenth proposition of the third book of Euclid's Elements, that if from the endpoint of a diameter of a circle a line is drawn at a right angle, then the angle enclosed by that line and the arc of the circle is smaller than any acute rectilinear angle, and the angle resulting from the diameter and the half-circle lying inside the circle is larger than any acute rectilinear angle. If this is established, we say: If we draw on the diameter of a circle another smaller circle tangent to it at the point which is the foot of the perpendicular, then the angle formed by the perpendicular and the arc of the smaller circle is wider than the first angle, and the angle lying inside the circle becomes narrower. If this is established, we say: Whenever the circle is smaller, the external angle is wider and the internal angle is narrower. If magnitude were subject to infinite divisions, it would be possible for circles infinite in number to be drawn at the foot of the perpendicular, each smaller than the one before. Then it would follow that the external angles increase ad infinitum and the internal angle decreases ad infinitum. Yet that external angle never becomes equal to this internal angle, because that external angle, however it is, is smaller than any acute rectilinear angle, and this internal angle, however it is, is larger than any acute rectilinear angle. So it is established that the impossibility we required is necessary. Also, the angle formed by the perpendicular and the arc of the circle is subject to increase ad infinitum, and the acute angle formed by two straight lines is subject to division ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents a second geometric proof against infinite divisibility. The proof relies on a well-known Euclidian proposition (III.15) about angles between a tangent and a chord. The key idea: if a line is drawn perpendicular to the diameter of a circle, the angle between that line and the arc is smaller than any acute rectilinear angle, while the angle inside the circle (between the diameter and the half-circle) is larger than any acute rectilinear angle. If we then draw a smaller circle tangent to the first at the foot of the perpendicular, the external angle becomes wider and the internal narrower. As circles get smaller, the external angle increases and the internal decreases. If magnitude were infinitely divisible, we could have an infinite set of such circles, leading to an infinite series of external angles increasing and internal angles decreasing. Yet the external angle always remains smaller than any acute rectilinear angle, and the internal always remains larger; thus they can never meet or become equal. This is an impossibility. Furthermore, the external angle can increase infinitely, and the acute rectilinear angle can be divided infinitely—but this does not resolve the impossibility.

زاوية خارجية
زاوية داخلية
دائرة
قطر
انقسام غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof shows that space is discrete and atoms exist.
Correction: The proof only shows that assuming infinite divisibility leads to a geometric contradiction; it does not directly prove physical atomism, though it is used as a supporting argument.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what property do the external and internal angles have with respect to acute rectilinear angles?
A) Both are larger than any acute rectilinear angle.
B) The external angle is smaller and the internal angle is larger than any acute rectilinear angle.
C) Both are smaller than any acute rectilinear angle.
D) The external angle is larger and the internal angle is smaller than any acute rectilinear angle.

Key Takeaway
Euclid's second geometric proof shows that infinite divisibility leads to a contradiction where two angles that should become equal can never do so.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that an infinite process never brings two quantities to equality. In your own life, is there a goal or virtue you have been pursuing endlessly without ever reaching it? What does this geometric truth reveal about the need for a decisive moment or change rather than endless effort?
?
If your intellect can accept that infinite divisibility is impossible because it leads to a contradiction, what does that tell you about the reliability of your reason in matters of faith? Do you trust rational proofs to guide you to truth about the unseen?
?
The external angle and internal angle never meet despite infinite change. Where in your spiritual life do you see two opposing tendencies (e.g., hope and fear, desire and duty) that seem to never converge? How does this proof challenge you to seek a resolution not through endless adjustment but through a decisive act of submission?

The Hand in the Void: Strengthening the Objection
Strengthens the objection by analogizing motion in the void to the impossibility of extending the hand beyond the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hand-in-void argument distinguishes between prevention by an obstacle and prevention by the absence of a condition, and how this distinction strengthens the original objection about motion.

Core Idea
Prevention can be due to a missing condition, not just an obstacle.

The author strengthens his objection by invoking a well-known debate about the void. Those who affirm the void argue that if a person at the world's edge cannot extend a hand, a body must block it. Philosophers respond that the prevention is actually due to the lack of space—a missing condition, not an obstacle. The author applies this same reasoning to the motion of two end parts, showing that their impossibility stems from an absent condition, not a blocking object.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the hand argument, then the philosophical response, then the application.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Distinguish between obstacle and missing condition
In the philosophers' response, they explain that prevention can be due to either an obstacle (مانع) or the absence of a condition (فوات الشرط). Identify which one applies to the hand case.
This distinction is crucial for the overall argument.
3
Apply the reasoning to the original problem
The author applies the same logic to the motion of the two parts. Reflect on how this strengthens the objection—what previously seemed like an obstacle is actually a missing condition.
This shows the power of the analogy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي يقوى هذا السؤال . وجهان : الأول : إن القائلين بإثبات الخلاء خارج العالم ، استدلوا عليه . وقالوا : الواقف على طرف العالم ، هل يمكنه من اليد إلى الخارج أو لا يمكنه ذلك ؟ فإن أمكنه ذلك ، فقد حصلت الأحياز الفارغة خارج العالم . وإن لم يمكنه ذلك ، فقد حصل خارج العالم جسم يمنع من ذلك . وإذا حصل الجسم خارج العالم ، فقد حصلت الأحياز خارج العالم . ثم إن الفلاسفة القائلين بنفي الخلاء خارج العالم ، أجابوا عنه . وقالوا : إنه يمتنع منه مد اليد إلى خارج العالم . إلا أن ذلك الامتناع ليس لأن جسماً حصل خارج العالم ، فمنع من اليد إليه ولكن لأجل أن مد اليد مشروط بحصول الأحياز. فإذا لم توجد الأحياز خارج العالم ، لا جرم امتنع مد اليد إلى خارج العالم. لا لأجل قيام المانع ، بل لأجل فوات الشرط. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا عقلنا هذا الكلام . فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال : إنه يمتنع حركة الجزءين الموضوعين على الطرفين معاً ، لا لقيام المانع ، بل لفوات الشرط . وهو أن حركتها معا : مشروط بانقسام المسافة . فما لم يوحد هذا الشرط ، لا جرم فات الإمكان لفوات الشرط .
What strengthens this objection: two aspects. The first: Indeed, those who affirm the void outside the world used it as a proof. They said: The person standing at the edge of the world – can he extend his hand outward or not? If he can, then empty spaces exist outside the world. If he cannot, then a body outside the world exists that prevents that. And if a body exists outside the world, then spaces exist outside the world. Then the philosophers who deny the void outside the world responded to it. They said: He is prevented from extending his hand outside the world. However, that prevention is not because a body exists outside the world that prevents the hand from reaching it, but because extending the hand is conditioned by the existence of spaces. So when spaces do not exist outside the world, necessarily the extension of the hand outside the world is prevented, not due to the presence of an obstacle, but due to the absence of the condition. If you understand this, we say: Since we have understood this speech, why may it not also be said: The motion of the two parts placed at the ends together is prevented, not due to the presence of an obstacle, but due to the absence of the condition. That is, their motion together is conditioned by the division of the distance. So as long as this condition is not realized, necessarily possibility is lost due to the absence of the condition.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage provides the first of two ways to strengthen the objection. The author invokes a known argument about the void: if someone at the world's edge could extend a hand, then void exists; if not, a body blocks it. Philosophers answer that the impossibility is not due to an obstacle but to the lack of a necessary condition (space). The author applies this reasoning to the motion of the two end parts. Their motion is impossible not because something blocks them but because the condition (division of the intermediate distance) is absent. Thus the objection is strengthened: the impossibility is due to missing condition, not an obstacle.

Correction: The philosophers argue that the impossibility is not due to an obstacle but due to the absence of the necessary condition (space).

B) The hand is too short.
C) The necessary condition (space) is absent.
D) It is physically impossible.

The impossibility of motion can be due to a missing condition, not only an obstacle, which strengthens the objection against the philosophers.

The Void Argument and the Obstacle vs. Condition Distinction
Objection using hand extension to prove void, and philosophers' response with obstacle/condition distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' distinction between an active obstacle (mani') and a missing condition (fawat al-shart) is used to defend the impossibility of motion without spatial division.

Core Idea
Prevention can arise from a missing condition, not just an active obstacle.

This lesson examines a clever argument from proponents of the void and the philosophers' response. The key move is distinguishing between a positive obstacle (مانع) and the absence of a required condition (فوات الشرط). This distinction is then applied to the problem of simultaneous motion of two parts, reinforcing the earlier objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: an objection from the void proponents, then the philosophers' answer, then the author's application.
Pay attention to the terms 'المانع' and 'فوات الشرط'.
2
Analyze the proponents' dilemma
The proponents argue that if a person at the edge of the world can extend their hand, then void exists; if they cannot, then a body exists outside, which also implies void. Understand why both branches lead to void.
3
Understand the philosophers' response
The philosophers deny the proponents' conclusion. They say the hand cannot extend not because of an obstacle but because the condition for extension (existence of spaces) is missing. This is the key conceptual move.
The term 'فوات الشرط' is crucial. It means the absence of a necessary condition.
4
Apply the same reasoning
The author applies this reasoning to the motion of two parts at opposite ends. Their movement together is impossible not due to an obstacle but because the condition (division of distance) is missing. This strengthens the original objection.
See how the same logical structure is used: prevention by missing condition.

الوجه الأول : إن القائلين بإثبات الخلاء خارج العالم، استدلوا عليه . وقالوا : الواقف على طرف العالم، هل يمكنه من اليد إلى الخارج أو لا يمكنه ذلك ؟ فإن أمكنه ذلك ، فقد حصلت الأحياز الفارغة خارج العالم . وإن لم يمكنه ذلك ، فقد حصل خارج العالم جسم يمنع من ذلك . وإذا حصل الجسم خارج العالم ، فقد حصلت الأحياز خارج العالم . ثم إن الفلاسفة القائلين بنفي الخلاء خارج العالم ، أجابوا عنه . وقالوا : إنه يمتنع منه مد اليد إلى خارج العالم . إلا أن ذلك الامتناع ليس لأن جسماً حصل خارج العالم ، فمنع من اليد إليه ولكن لأجل أن مد اليد مشروط بحصول الأحياز. فإذا لم توجد الأحياز خارج العالم ، لا جرم امتنع مد اليد إلى خارج العالم. لا لأجل قيام المانع ، بل لأجل فوات الشرط . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا عقلنا هذا الكلام . فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال : إنه يمتنع حركة الجزءين الموضوعين على الطرفين معاً ، لا لقيام المانع ، بل لفوات الشرط . وهو أن حركتها معا : مشروط بانقسام المسافة . فما لم يوحد هذا الشرط ، لا جرم فات الإمكان لفوات الشرط .
The first aspect: Those who affirm the void outside the world used it as evidence. They said: regarding the one standing at the edge of the world, can he extend his hand outward or not? If he can, then empty spaces outside the world have occurred. And if he cannot, then a body outside the world has occurred that prevents that. And if a body outside the world occurs, then spaces outside the world have occurred. Then the philosophers who deny the void outside the world answered it, saying: He is prevented from extending his hand to outside the world, but that prevention is not because a body has occurred outside the world that prevents the hand from reaching it, but because extending the hand is conditioned upon the existence of spaces. So if spaces do not exist outside the world, inevitably the extension of the hand to outside the world is prevented, not due to the presence of an obstacle, but due to the lack of the condition. Once you understand this, we say: if we comprehend this statement, then why is it not also permissible to say: The movement of the two parts placed at both ends together is prevented, not due to the presence of an obstacle, but due to the lack of the condition, namely that their movement together is conditioned upon the division of the distance. So as long as this condition is not present, inevitably possibility ceases due to the lack of the condition.
