Commentary
This passage presents the first of two aspects that strengthen a previous objection. It begins by recounting an argument used by proponents of the void: If a person at the edge of the world can or cannot extend their hand outward, either case implies the existence of space (void) or a body outside the world, which itself implies space. The philosophers who deny the void respond that the prevention of extending the hand is not due to an obstacle (mani') but due to the absence of a condition (fawat al-shart) — namely, the existence of space. The author then applies this reasoning to his own problem: the impossibility of the two parts moving together is similarly due to the absence of the condition of the division of the distance, not because of an obstacle. This strengthens the original objection by showing that the philosophers' own principle can be turned against their position.

The Stone, the Mountain, and Missing Conditions
An objection using the necessity of a stone's rest when met by a descending mountain, and the extraction of a principle about causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scenario of a pebble and a descending mountain illustrates the principle that even a strong efficient cause may not produce its effect due to a missing condition or an external obstacle, and how this strengthens the objection against the philosophers.

Core Idea
A cause can be present yet its effect blocked by a missing condition.

The author presents a vivid analogy to challenge the philosophers' causal principle. By examining a scenario where a pebble's rest would halt a descending mountain, he extracts a key principle: even a strong efficient cause may fail to produce its effect if a condition is absent. This is then applied to the original debate, forming a powerful objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a hypothetical scenario to make his point.
2
Understand the scenario
The philosophers believe a stone must rest between upward and downward motion. Imagine a huge mountain descending exactly when a pebble reaches its rest point. If the pebble must rest, would it block the mountain? The philosophers admit it would.
The scenario is intentionally exaggerated to test the principle.
3
Grasp the extracted principle
The author draws a general rule: even when the strong efficient cause (the mountain's weight) is present, the effect (its descent) may not occur due to a missing condition or an obstacle (the pebble's rest being necessary).
This principle is key to the objection.
4
See the application
The author applies this to the original problem: the two parts of a continuum may not move, not because of an impediment, but because a condition (like the division of distance) is absent. This makes the objection strong.
This shows the philosophers' own reasoning can be turned against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان تقوية هذا السؤال : إن الفلاسفة اعتقدوا أنه لا بد وأن يحصل بين الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة للحجر : سكون . فإذا قيل لهم : لو قدرنا نزول جبل في غاية العظمة من الهواء ، واتفق أنا رمينا المدرة إلى فوق . فحال انتهاء حركتها الصاعدة . لو وصل ذلك الجبل النازل إلى تلك المدرة . فلو وجب سكون تلك المدرة في الهواء لوجب أن يكون سكونها مانعاً لذلك الجبل العظيم من النزول . ثم إن الفلاسفة التزموا ذلك ، وقالوا : إن ثقل ذلك الجبل ، وإن كان يوجب نزوله، إلا أنه لما وجب بقاء تلك المدرة في الهواء ، وكان بقاؤها في الهواء مانعاً من نزول ذلك الجبل العظيم ، لا جرم قلنا : بأنه يجب بقاء ذلك الجبل في الهواء وسكونه فيه . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : فهذا الكلام يدل على أن مع حصول الموجب القوي ، قد لا يحصل الأثر ، لأجل فوات شرط من الخارج ، أو لقيام مانع من الخارج. وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز في مسألتنا أن يقال : كل واحد من هذين الجزءين ، وإن كان قابلاً للحركة ، وكان العائق عن الحركة زائلاً إلا أنه امتنعت تلك الحركة ، لفوات شرط من شرائط إمكانها . فهذا سؤال قوي
The second aspect in clarifying the strengthening of this question: The philosophers believed that between the upward motion and the downward motion of a stone, a rest must necessarily occur. So if it is said to them: Suppose we imagine a mountain of extreme greatness descending from the air, and it so happens that we throw a pebble upward. At the moment of the end of its upward motion, if that descending mountain reaches that pebble, then if that pebble's rest in the air is necessary, then its rest would have to prevent that great mountain from descending. Then the philosophers committed to that and said: The weight of that mountain, although it necessitates its descent, yet since the pebble's remaining in the air was necessary, and its remaining in the air was a hindrance to the descent of that great mountain, we inevitably say: that mountain must remain in the air and be at rest in it. If this is established, we say: This statement indicates that despite the presence of the strong efficient cause, the effect may not occur, due to the absence of an external condition or the presence of an external obstacle. And if this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible in our issue to say: Each of these two parts, although capable of motion, and although the impediment to motion is removed, yet that motion is prevented due to the absence of a condition from among the conditions of its possibility. This is a strong question.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect strengthening the objection. It recounts a scenario from the philosophers: a stone in upward motion must have a moment of rest before descending. If a huge mountain descends and meets the stone at that moment, the stone's rest would prevent the mountain's descent. The philosophers accept this, saying the mountain must also remain at rest. The author then draws a principle: even a strong cause (al-mujib al-qawi) may not produce its effect due to a missing condition or an obstacle. Applying this to the original problem, he argues that the two parts, though capable of motion and with no impediment, may be prevented from moving due to the absence of a condition (such as the division of the distance). This is presented as a strong objection against the philosophers' position.

المدرسة
الجبل
الموجب القوي
الأثر
فوات الشرط
المانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the stone's rest is a physical necessity, but the author uses it to illustrate the role of conditions in causation.
Correction: The stone's rest is accepted as a given by the philosophers; the point is that even a strong cause can be blocked by a missing condition or obstacle.

Checkpoint
What does the scenario of the pebble and the mountain illustrate according to the author?
A) That a heavy object always overcomes a light one.
B) That a strong cause can be prevented by a missing condition or obstacle.
C) That rest is impossible during motion.
D) That the philosophers reject the concept of causation.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a condition can block even a strong cause, which is used to argue that motion may be prevented without an apparent impediment.

?
The philosophers had to admit that a mountain could be halted by a pebble's rest. What is the smallest obstacle in your life that you have allowed to derail a noble goal? Will you acknowledge it tonight and take one concrete step to remove it?

The Strong Objection: Missing Conditions Prevent Motion
The author presents a strong objection that motion of atoms is prevented by a missing condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a missing condition can prevent an effect even when the cause is present, and how this objection applies to the motion of atoms.

Core Idea
A strong cause does not guarantee its effect if a condition is missing.

The author presents a strong objection based on a physical example. He argues that even when a cause is fully present, the effect may be blocked by an absent condition. This challenges the atomistic assumption that motion occurs whenever nothing hinders it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from example to principle to application.
2
Examine the mountain example
Identify how the author uses the stone-in-air to block the mountain's descent. What does this illustrate about causality?
The stone acts as an obstacle, not just a hindrance.
3
Apply the reasoning to atoms
See how the author generalizes: even with a strong motive and no hindrance, a missing condition can prevent motion. How does this challenge the atomistic position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الجبل ، وإن كان يوجب نزوله، إلا أنه لما وجب بقاء تلك المدرة في الهواء ، وكان بقاؤها في الهواء مانعاً من نزول ذلك الجبل العظيم ، لا جرم قلنا : بأنه يجب بقاء ذلك الجبل في الهواء وسكونه فيه. إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : فهذا الكلام يدل على أن مع حصول الموجب القوي ، قد لا يحصل الأثر ، لأجل فوات شرط من الخارج ، أو لقيام مانع من الخارج. وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز في مسألتنا أن يقال : كل واحد من هذين الجزءين ، وإن كان قابلاً للحركة ، وكان العائق عن الحركة زائلاً إلا أنه امتنعت تلك الحركة ، لفوات شرط من شرائط إمكانها . فهذا سؤال قوي
That mountain, even though it necessitated its descent, yet because the staying of that stone in the air was necessitated, and its staying in the air prevented the descent of that great mountain, unavoidably we said: it is necessary for that mountain to remain in the air and be stationary in it. If this is established, then we say: this speech indicates that with the occurrence of the strong motive, the effect may not occur, due to the absence of an external condition or the existence of an external obstacle. And if this is established, then we say: why should it not be permissible in our issue to say: each of these two parts, even though it is capable of motion and the hindrance to motion is removed, yet that motion is prevented due to the absence of a condition from among the conditions of its possibility. This is a strong objection.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents a strong objection against the atomistic thesis. The author uses an example: a mountain which should descend but cannot because a stone remains in the air, preventing it. He generalizes: even when a cause (motive) is present and active, the effect may not occur because a condition is missing or an obstacle exists. Applying this to the problem of two atoms (parts), he argues that even if each atom is capable of motion and no hindrance is present, motion may still be prevented by the absence of some condition of possibility. This is presented as a strong objection that previous philosophers did not adequately address.

Misconception: If a cause is present and no hindrance exists, the effect must occur.
Correction: Even without a hindrance, a missing condition can prevent the effect.

A strong cause does not guarantee its effect if an essential condition is absent.

First Proof Against the Indivisible Part: Slowness Not from Rests
Sets up the argument against the indivisible part and presents the first proof from celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the comparison between a fast horse and the celestial sphere proves that slowness in motion is not caused by the insertion of rests, thereby undermining the argument for an indivisible part.

Core Idea
If slowness were due to rests, celestial motions would vanish among endless rests—but we see none.

The opponents of the indivisible part begin with a crucial claim: slow motion is not caused by little moments of rest inserted between movements. To prove this, they offer a vivid analogy comparing a galloping horse to the rotating heavens. The argument is designed to show that if rests explained slowness, the universe would be full of invisible pauses, yet we observe only continuous motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Notice how the argument unfolds: a claim, a station, then the first proof.
The author uses a very logical structure—pay attention to 'إذا ثبت ذلك' (if that is established).
2
Identify the central claim
The first sentence states that slow motion is not due to rests. The second says if that is true, the indivisible part is false. Grasp this two‑step logic.
3
Understand the first station
The author calls the first claim 'the first station' (المقام الأول). It is the foundation of the whole argument. See how the horse–sphere comparison proves it.
4
Analyze the horse–sphere comparison
Work through the numbers: horse travels 20 parasangs, sphere does ¼ rotation in the same time. If slowness = rests, then the ratio of rests to motions in the horse equals the ratio of sphere's speed to horse's speed. That ratio is enormous, so the horse would have billions of rests—enough to hide the sphere's motion. But we see the sphere moving without any perceived rests.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if rests existed, we wouldn't see the sphere moving, but we do, so rests do not cause slowness.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof ends by stating that we sense no rests at all, only successive motions. Therefore, slowness is not due to rests, and the idea of an indivisible part is further weakened.
This is the first of several proofs the author will present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول : احتج القائلون بنفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ . فقالوا : ثبت بالدليل أن الحركة البطيئة ليس بطؤها لأجل تخلل السكنات فيها . وإذا ثبت ذلك، وجب أن يكون القول بإثبات الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ باطلاً . أما تقرير المقام الأول. وهو أن بطء الحركات لا يمكن أن يكون لأجل تخلل السكنات . فيدل عليه وجوه . الأول : إنا إذا فرضنا فرساً شديد العدو، بحيث يسير من البكرة إلى الظهر : عشرين فرسخا . فنقول : والفلك الأعظم قد دار في مثل هذه المدة : ربع مداره. فلو كان البطء عبارة عن تخلل السكنات، لكان مقدار زيادة سكنات هذا الفرس على حركاته، مساوياً المقدار زيادة حركات الفلك الأعظم، على حركات هذا الفرس. لكن من المعلوم : أن زيادة حركة الفلك الأعظم. على حركة الفرس أكثر من ألف ألف مرة . فيلزم : أن تكون زيادة سكنات هذا الفرس على حركاته كذلك . ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لما ظهرت هذه الحركات الفلكية، في أثناء هذه السكنات (۱) الكثيرة . فوجب أن لا تظهر تلك الحركات أصلاً في الحس. وحيث كان هذا التالي كاذباً بل كان الحق هو ضده، وهو أنا لا نحس البتة بشيء من السكنات، وإنما نحس بالحركات المتوالية المتعاقبة . علمنا : أن التفاوت الحاصل بين سرعة حركة الفلك، وسرعة حركة الفرس، ليس لأجل تخلل السكنات . وذلك يفيد القطع بأن حصول البطء في الحركات، لا يمكن أن يكون لأجل تخلل السكنات
If you have understood this, we say: Those who affirm the negation of the indivisible part argued. They said: It is established by proof that slow motion is not due to the insertion of rests in it. And if that is established, then the statement affirming the indivisible part must be false. As for the exposition of the first station—which is that the slowness of motions cannot be due to the insertion of rests—proofs indicate it. The first: If we suppose a horse of extreme speed, such that it travels from morning to noon twenty parasangs. We say: The greatest celestial sphere has rotated in a similar period a quarter of its orbit. So if slowness were an expression of the insertion of rests, then the amount of increase of the rests of this horse over its motions would equal the amount of increase of the motions of the greatest celestial sphere over the motions of this horse. But it is known that the increase of the motion of the greatest celestial sphere over the motion of the horse is more than a thousand thousand times. So it would be necessary that the increase of the rests of this horse over its motions be likewise. And if it were so, these celestial motions would not appear amidst these many rests. So it would be necessary that those motions not appear at all in sensation. And since this consequent is false—rather the truth is contrary to it, namely that we do not sense any rests at all, but rather we sense successive consecutive motions—we know that the disparity occurring between the speed of the motion of the celestial sphere and the speed of the motion of the horse is not due to the insertion of rests. And that yields certainty that the occurrence of slowness in motions cannot be due to the insertion of rests.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines the main argument against the indivisible part and then presents the first of several proofs. It begins by stating the conclusion of the opponents: slow motion is not due to the insertion of rests, and if that is true, then the existence of the indivisible part is false. Then it introduces 'the first station' and the first proof. The proof compares a fast horse and the celestial sphere. If slowness were caused by rests, then the number of rests relative to motions should be proportional to the slowness. But the celestial sphere moves vastly faster than the horse; if the horse's slowness were due to rests, the horse would have an enormous number of rests, so many that the celestial motions would be hidden among them. But we observe no rests at all, only continuous motion. Therefore, slowness is not due to rests.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
تخلل السكنات (insertion of rests)
الحركة البطيئة (slow motion)
الفلك الأعظم (greatest celestial sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against the indivisible part is a general attack on atomism.
Correction: It specifically targets the concept of an indivisible spatial part (juz' la yatajazzā), not atomism as a whole.

Checkpoint

A) A horse and a celestial sphere
B) A stone and a feather
C) The speed of sound and light
D) The motion of the sun and moon

Key Takeaway
The slowness of motion is not caused by interspersed rests, because if it were, the extremely fast motion of the celestial sphere would be imperceptible due to the massive number of rests in slower objects.

Second Proof: Weight and Speed of Fall
Presents the second proof based on weight and speed of fall.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observation that heavier bodies fall faster provides a second argument that slowness of motion is not caused by inserted moments of rest.

Core Idea
Speed variation can come from weight, not just rests.

This lesson presents the second proof for the claim that slowness in motion is not due to inserted rests. The argument uses a simple physical observation: heavier objects fall faster. By considering a body already moving without rests that then becomes heavier, we see that its speed increases. This increase cannot be due to rests, so speed differences must have another cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: a known fact, a hypothetical condition, and a conclusion.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof starts with the observation that heavier bodies descend faster. This is taken as known. Mark this premise.
This is an empirical fact used as a starting point.
3
Follow the hypothetical reasoning
The argument asks you to suppose a body already moving without any rests (i.e., perfectly continuous motion). Then imagine it becomes heavier. What must happen? Its motion must become faster.
This shows that an increase in weight alone causes an increase in speed.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the speed increased without any insertion of rests, the difference in speed cannot be due to rests. Therefore, slowness is not caused by rests.
This directly supports the overall thesis being proved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في إثبات هذا المطلوب : وهو أنا نعلم أن الجسم كلما كان اشيد ثقلاً، كان أسرع نزولاً . فإذا فرضنا : أن الجسم قد بلغ في الثقل إلى حيث تكون حركته خالصة عن السكونات ، ثم فرضنا بأنه بعد ذلك ، صار أثقل مما كان ، وجب أن تصير حركته أسرع مما كانت قبل ذلك . فههنا حصل التفاوت بين هاتين الحركتين في السرعة والبطء ، لا بسبب تخلل السكنات وهو المطلوب .
The second proof for establishing this required point: that we know that whenever a body is heavier, it descends faster. So if we suppose that the body has reached in weight to where its motion is free from rests, then suppose that after that it becomes heavier than it was, its motion must become faster than it was before. Here the disparity between these two motions in speed and slowness occurs, not due to the insertion of rests—and that is the required point.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the second proof. It uses the observation that heavier bodies fall faster. If a body is already moving without any rests, and then it becomes heavier, its motion becomes faster. The increase in speed is caused by the increase in weight, not by inserting rests. Therefore, the difference between slow and fast motion is not due to rests.

Correction: The hypothetical scenario is a thought experiment to isolate the effect of weight. Even if such a body does not exist in reality, the logical point stands: if a change in weight alone can change speed, then speed variation does not require rests.

Weight Cannot Cause Both Motion and Rest
Presents the third proof that weight cannot cause both motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that a constant cause like weight cannot produce both motion and rest without a change in the cause.

Core Idea
A constant cause cannot produce opposite effects.

Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a third proof against the theory that slow motion is due to intervening rests. The argument is simple: weight is a constant cause of descent, so it cannot cause motion in one part and rest in another without a difference in the cause. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: premise, impossibility, conclusion.
The proof is very concise.
2
Identify the key premise
What is the constant cause stated at the beginning? How does the argument use its constancy?
3
Understand the impossibility
Why can weight not cause motion in one part and rest in another? What would be required?
The argument says there would need to be a difference between the parts.
4
Draw the conclusion
What does this imply about slow motion? How does it refute the idea of rests?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث : إن الثقل يوجب النزول . فإذا كان الثقل موجباً للنزول ، وأنه باق في جميع الأوقات، امتنع أن يوجب الحركة في أجزاء المسافة . ثم إنه بعينه يوجب السكون في جزء آخر ، من غير تفاوت بين الجزءين البتة . فثبت : أن القول بسكونه باطل . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أن التفاوت بين الحركة البطيئة والسريعة ، لا يجوز أن يكون لأجل تخلل السكنات
The third proof: that weight necessitates descent. Since weight necessitates descent, and it persists at all times, it is impossible that it cause motion in a part of the distance, then the very same [weight] cause rest in another part, with no difference whatsoever between the two parts. So it is established that the statement that it is at rest is false. And when this is established, it becomes clear that the disparity between slow and fast motion cannot be due to the insertion of rests.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is the third proof. It argues that weight is a constant cause of descent. If weight were to cause motion in one part of the distance and rest in another, there would have to be some difference in the weight between those parts. But weight is uniform. Therefore, the body cannot be at rest in some parts while moving in others. Hence, slowness is not due to rests.

Key Terms
ثقل (weight)
نزول (descent)
سكون (rest)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A constant cause like weight can cause motion in one part and rest in another without any change in the cause.
Correction: The argument shows that if weight is constant, it cannot produce opposite effects (motion and rest) in different parts; a difference in the cause would be required.

Checkpoint
The third proof argues that if weight causes motion, it cannot also cause rest without what?
A) A difference between the two parts
B) Insertion of rests
C) The presence of a mover
D) The absence of impediments

Key Takeaway
A constant cause cannot produce opposite effects; thus slow motion is not due to intervening rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you claim to have a constant faith in Allah's power. But when things don't go your way, do your actions reflect trust or anxiety? If a constant cause (faith) should produce consistent effects (peace and reliance), why do you sometimes waver? What would it take for your actions to match your belief?
?
Consider a habit you are trying to change. You say you want to be consistent in prayer or patience, yet you often fail. According to this proof, if your intention (cause) is truly constant, it cannot produce opposite effects (sometimes prayer, sometimes neglect). Is your intention truly constant, or is there a hidden inconsistency in your resolve? Confront that inconsistency now.

The Millstone Proof: Motion Without Rests
Presents the fourth proof based on rotating millstones

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the millstone argument demonstrates that slowness of motion is not due to inserted rests.

Core Idea
Speed differences in a rotating millstone show that slowness does not require rests.

Sanusi presents a geometric argument using a rotating millstone to challenge the idea that slow motion is caused by rests. By analyzing points along a radius, he shows that slower motion can occur without any stopping. This proof is a key example of how kalam uses physical analogies to establish logical principles.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the millstone
Imagine a rotating millstone with a center and circumference. Consider a line from the center to the edge with many points on it; each point traces a circle when the millstone rotates.
The closer a point is to the center, the smaller its circle.
2
Understand the impossible options
Consider two possibilities: either all circles move the same angular distance (impossible because that would make their circumferences equal) or the outer circle moves while the inner stays still (impossible because it would break the millstone).
3
Accept the true option
The only coherent possibility is that the inner circle moves slower than the outer. This proves that slowness does not require rests, as the inner circle never stops.
This is the required conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إنا إذا أخرجنا من مركز الرحى، إلى محيطه خطاً واحداً . فإنه يجب أن يفرض في ذلك الخط نقط كثيرة . فإذا استدارت الزحى ، ارتسم من كل واحدة من تلك النقط : دائرة . وكل نقطة كانت أقرب إلى المركز . فإن الدائرة المرتسمة منها أصغر ، وكل نقطة كانت أقرب إلى المحيط ، كانت الدائرة المرتسمة منها أعظم . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إذا استدارت الرحى . فقد استدارت الدائرة ، التي هي طرفي الرحى ، واستدارت أيضاً الدائرة القريبة من القطب . فإما أن يقال : كلما تحركت الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً ، فقد تحركت الدائرة الصغيرة جزءاً وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن يكون مدار الدائرة [ الصغيرة مساوياً لمدار الدائرة العظيمة . وهو محال . وإما أن يقال : إن الدائرة (۱) ] العظيمة تتحرك جزءاً مع أن الدائرة الصغيرة لا تتحرك البتة . وهذا محال أيضاً . لأنه يوجب وقوع التفكك بين أجزاء الرحى . وهو محال . وإما أن يقال : كلما تحركت الدائرة العظيمة ، فإن الدائرة الصغيرة تتحرك حركة أبطء من حركة الدائرة العظيمة . وهذا هو الحق . وإنه يوجب القطع بأن التفاوت بين البطيء والسريع ، ليس لأجل تخلل السكنات . وهو المطلوب .
The fourth proof: that if we draw from the center of the millstone to its circumference a single line, then many points must be supposed on that line. When the millstone rotates, a circle is traced from each of those points. The closer a point is to the center, the smaller the circle traced from it; and the closer a point is to the circumference, the larger the circle traced from it. This being established, we say: When the millstone rotates, the circle that is the edge of the millstone rotates, and also the circle near the center rotates. Then either it is said: Whenever the large circle moves a part, the small circle moves a part—but that is impossible, because it would necessitate that the orbit of the small circle be equal to the orbit of the large circle, which is impossible. Or it is said: The large circle moves a part while the small circle does not move at all—but this is also impossible, because it would entail disintegration among the parts of the millstone, which is impossible. Or it is said: Whenever the large circle moves, the small circle moves with a motion slower than the motion of the large circle. This is the truth. And it entails the certainty that the disparity between slow and fast is not due to the insertion of rests. And that is the required point.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof, using a rotating millstone. Points on a radius trace circles of different sizes depending on their distance from the center. When the millstone rotates, either all circles move the same angular distance (impossible, because their circumferences differ), or the outer circle moves while the inner is still (impossible, as it would break the millstone), or both move but the inner slower (the truth). Since the inner moves slower without rests, slowness is not due to rests.

Key Terms
الرحى
مركز
محيط
دائرة
قطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The millstone argument depends on the physical properties of a real millstone.
Correction: The argument is a geometric thought experiment about rotating circles; it does not rely on the millstone being a physical object.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what is the consequence if the inner circle of the millstone were to stay still while the outer circle moves?

B) The motion would be faster
C) The circles would have equal circumferences
D) The slow motion would be due to rests

Key Takeaway
The millstone argument proves that slower motion can occur without any inserted rests, showing that rest is not the cause of slowness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof eliminates two impossible options before accepting the truth. Are there beliefs you hold about God or His creation that you have not critically examined? What 'impossible options' are you still entertaining that prevent you from accepting the clear truth that this lesson demands? Will you take the step to eliminate them now?
?
The slowness of the inner circle is not due to rests but to its intrinsic nature. In your own life, do you attribute your shortcomings to 'rests' (laziness, distractions) when they might actually be a slower but still continuous movement? What would change if you saw your efforts as continuous, even if slow, rather than broken by failures?

Slowness Without Rests: The Argument from Celestial Rotation
A brief argument using celestial rotation to show slowness without rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rotation of the celestial sphere demonstrates that slowness can occur without intercalated moments of rest.

Core Idea
Slowness does not require pauses; the slower motion of the polar circle proves it.

This passage presents a concise argument from celestial mechanics. By observing the rotation of the celestial sphere, we see that different parts move at different speeds. The slower motion of the circle near the pole shows that slowness arises without any inserted moments of rest. This argument lays the groundwork for understanding how continuous motion can vary in speed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical connector 'يلزم' which indicates a necessary implication.
2
Identify the two components
Identify the two parts of the celestial sphere mentioned: the region (al-minṭaqa) and the circle near the pole. Understand their relative positions.
3
Understand the logical implication
The passage states that when the region moves, the polar circle moves slower. This is presented as a necessary consequence. Ask yourself why this must be true.
This step connects to the broader debate about slowness and rests.
4
Relate to the doctrinal point
The argument is used to show that slowness can occur without any intercalated moments of rest. Reflect on why this matters in kalam reasoning.
The phrase 'ذلك هو المطلوب' signals that this is the desired conclusion.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider how this simple observation challenges the idea that slowness requires pauses. This is a foundational point for subsequent proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استدارة الفلك . ويلزم أنه كلما تحركت المنطقة ، فإن الدائرة القريبة من القطب ، قد تحركت بحركة أبطا منها وذلك هو المطلوب
The rotation of the celestial sphere. And it follows that whenever the movable belt moves, the circle near the pole moves with a motion slower than it. That is what was sought.

Commentary
This passage presents a short argument from celestial mechanics. It states that when the region (al-minṭaqa) of the celestial sphere rotates, the circle near the pole moves with a slower motion. This demonstrates that slowness occurs without any intercalated moments of rest (sakanāt). The argument is a prelude to the formal proofs that follow.

The Compass Argument: Slowness Without Rests
Geometric proof using a compass to refute the necessity of intercalated rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a geometric compass analogy demonstrates that slowness can occur without intermittent rests, challenging an atomistic assumption.

Core Idea
Slowness can arise from geometry, not from hidden rests.

This lesson presents a powerful geometric counterexample from classical Islamic kalam. Using a simple compass, the author shows that proportional motion can produce slowness without any moments of rest. This argument challenges the Ash'ari occasionalist view that all slowness in motion must be explained by intercalated atoms of rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the compact style; the proof is condensed into a few lines.
2
Understand the compass setup
Identify the three legs: one at center, one on a 50° orbit, one on a 100° orbit.
The numbers are chosen to give a clear 2:1 ratio.
3
Follow the motion
When the outer leg moves 2°, the middle leg must move 1° due to the geometry.
This proportional motion is key.
4
Connect to the point
The middle leg's slowness is purely geometric; no rests are introduced.
The author explicitly says slowness occurs 'not due to intercalation of rests.'
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this refutes the necessity of rests in explaining slowness, reinforcing the author's broader argument.
Compare with earlier examples of the millstone and celestial sphere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : وهو أنا إذا فرضنا فرجاراً ، له شعب ثلاثة . فوضعنا شعبة منه على مركز الدائرة ، والشعبة الثانية على دائرة مدارها خمسون جزءاً ، والشعبة الثالثة على دائرة مدارها مائة جزء . فعندما تتحرك الشعبة الثالثة المحيطة جزءين ، وجب أن تتحرك الشعبة المتوسطة جزءاً واحداً . على قياس ما ذكرنا في الرحى وفي الفلك . وذلك يوجب حصول البطء . لا بسبب تخلل السكنات
The sixth aspect: That if we suppose a compass having three legs. We place one leg on the center of the circle, the second leg on a circle whose orbit is fifty degrees, and the third leg on a circle whose orbit is one hundred degrees. When the third leg encompassing two degrees moves, the middle leg must move one degree, analogous to what we mentioned concerning the millstone and the celestial sphere. That necessitates the occurrence of slowness, not due to the intercalation of rests.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof (al-wajh al-sādis) against the theory that slowness is due to intercalated rests (takhallul al-sakanāt). The author sets up a geometric thought experiment: a compass with three legs, one fixed at the center, the second on a circle of 50° arc, the third on a 100° arc. When the third leg moves two degrees, the middle leg moves only one degree. This ratio produces a slower motion for the middle leg, yet there is no moment of rest; the slowness is a direct result of the geometry. This is analogous to the earlier examples of the millstone and the celestial sphere. The conclusion is that slowness arises from the relationship of distances covered in the same time, not from intermittent rests.

Key Terms
فرجار
شعب
دائرة
مدار
بطء
تخلل السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The compass is used to measure something, like an instrument.
Correction: The compass is purely an analogy for proportional motion, not a measuring device.

Checkpoint

A) The middle leg also moves two degrees
B) The middle leg moves one degree
C) The middle leg remains stationary
D) The compass breaks

Key Takeaway
The compass argument shows that slowness can result from proportional motion without any intercalated rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The source argues that slowness can occur without any intercalated rests, purely from proportional motion. The Shadow Argument Against Atomistic Rests
A trilemma proof using shadow lengths to refute atomistic rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple shadow observation refutes the atomistic claim that slowness is caused by intercalated rests.

Core Idea
Shadow length proves motion is continuous, not interrupted by rests.

This lesson presents a clever astronomical proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah. By considering the shadow of a stick, the author eliminates all possible explanations except one, which demonstrates that speed variation cannot be due to inserted moments of rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: three possibilities are presented and evaluated.
The author uses a trilemma format.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage lists three possible relationships between the sun's altitude and shadow length: one-to-one decrease, no change, or decrease by less than one degree.
These are the only logical options.
3
Understand the refutations
The first two possibilities are rejected because they lead to absurdities: shadow equaling the celestial sphere or sun rising without effect.
These are a priori impossibilities.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that shadow decreases by less than one degree per degree of sun rise. This shows continuous variation, hence no rests.
The conclusion directly refutes atomistic motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : إنا إذا غرزنا خشبة في الأرض . فإذا طلعت الشمس وقع لها ظل على الأرض ، ثم كلما ازداد ارتفاع الشمس ، انتقص طول الظل . فإما أن يقال : كلما ارتفعت الشمس جزءاً ، انتقص من الظل جزء . وهو محال . لأنه يلزم أن يكون طول الظل ، مساوياً لمدار الفلك . وهو محال . وإما أن يقال : قد ترتفع الشمس جزءاً ، مع أن الظل يبقى بحاله ، ولا ينتقص منه شيء . وذلك محال . وإما أن يقال : كلما ارتفعت الشمس جزءاً ، انتقص من الظل أقل من جزء . وذلك يوجب القطع بأن التفاوت بين السريع والبطيء : لا يكون بسبب تخلل السكنات
The seventh aspect: If we plant a stick in the ground. When the sun rises, it casts a shadow on the ground. Then whenever the sun's altitude increases, the length of the shadow decreases. So either it is said: whenever the sun rises by one degree, the shadow decreases by one degree. That is impossible, because it would necessitate that the length of the shadow equals the orbit of the celestial sphere, which is impossible. Or it is said: the sun may rise by one degree while the shadow remains as it is, and nothing decreases from it. That is impossible. Or it is said: whenever the sun rises by one degree, the shadow decreases by less than one degree. That necessitates the certainty that the difference between fast and slow is not due to the intercalation of rests.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (al-wajh al-sābiʿ) using a simple observation of a stick's shadow. The author enumerates three logical possibilities for the relationship between the sun's altitude and shadow length: (1) one-to-one decrease, (2) no decrease, (3) decrease by less than one degree. The first two are absurd because they would lead to contradictions (the shadow would equal the celestial sphere, or the sun would rise without effect). Only the third possibility remains, which shows that the speed of the shadow's shortening varies continuously without rests. Hence, the difference between fast and slow motion is not due to intercalated rests.

Key Terms
خشبة
ظل
ارتفاع الشمس
بطء
سرعة
تفاوت
تخلل السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shadow argument is merely an astronomical observation about the sun and shadows.
Correction: The argument is a logical refutation of atomistic motion, using shadow lengths to demonstrate that speed variation is continuous, not caused by intercalated rests.

Checkpoint

A) The shadow decreases by exactly one degree
B) The shadow does not change
C) The shadow decreases by less than one degree
D) The shadow increases

Key Takeaway
The shadow argument demonstrates that motion is continuous because the shadow's length changes smoothly, which would be impossible if speed variation were caused by intercalated rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The shadow argument shows that small, continuous changes reveal a deeper truth about motion. In your spiritual life, are you looking for dramatic, instant transformations (like a one-to-one change) or are you attentive to the gradual, subtle growth that Allah places each day? Which approach does this lesson suggest is more real, and what might you be missing by demanding quick results?
?
The trilemma forces a choice: either the shadow doesn't change (contradicts observation), or it changes too much (impossible consequence), or it changes just enough (the truth). Can you identify an area where your assumptions about how Allah works are like the first two options—contradicted by what you see or leading to absurd conclusions? Are you willing to accept the 'less than one degree' truth that may require patience and humility?
?
The conclusion is that speed differences are not due to rests (sakanāt) but to continuous motion. Reflect on a slow change in your character—is it that you have been 'stopping' (resting) in your efforts, or is it that the motion itself is naturally slow? What would change if you stopped blaming yourself for 'rests' and instead focused on the direction and persistence of your daily actions?

The Walking Person Argument: Psychological Proof Against Rests
A psychological proof using conscious action to refute rests in slow motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the walking person argument uses conscious awareness to show that slowness cannot be due to alternating motion and rest.

Core Idea
If slowness required rests, the walker would know them; he doesn't, so it doesn't.

This lesson examines a fascinating psychological argument that challenges the idea that slow motion is composed of motion interspersed with rests. By reflecting on our own experience of walking slowly, we can test the logical implications of that theory. Let's follow the author's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Set up the scenario
Understand that a rational person walking slowly is the example. Note that if slowness were motion+rest, then the walker would be voluntarily performing both motion and rest.
2
Identify conditions for voluntary action
The author states that voluntary action requires intention, choice, and knowledge of the specific action. Therefore, the walker would need to know each rest and motion.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since the walker is not aware of any rests (he perceives continuous motion), slowness cannot be due to rests. This refutes the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثامن : إن الإنسان العاقل ، قد يمشي مشياً بطيئاً . فلو كان البطء عبارة عن كونه ساكناً في بعض الأحياز، ومتحركاً في بعضها . فمن المعلوم أن تلك الحركة البطيئة عبارة عن الحركات المختلطة بالسكونات ، لكان ذلك الإنسان قد فعل باختياره في بعض الأحياز حركة ، وفي بعضها سكوناً . لكن من المعلوم : أن الفعل الاختياري لا يحصل إلا بالقصد والاختيار . والفعل الحاصل بالقصد والاختيار ، لا يحصل إلا مع العلم ، بالفعل المقصود والأمر المطلوب . فكان يجب فيمن يمشي مشياً بطيئاً ، أن يعلم بالضرورة : أنه تحرك في الحيز الفلاني ، وأنه وقف في الحيز الفلاني . لأن الفعل الذي فعله بقصده واختياره ، لا بد وأن يكون عالماً بأنه كيف اختاره ؟ وكيف أوجده ؟ ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، بل الذي يمشي مشياً بطيئاً ، يعتقد أنه متحرك في جميع صفة البطء . علمنا : أن البطء في الحركة ، لا يمكن أن يكون لأجل تخلل السكنات
The eighth aspect: A rational person may walk with a slow walk. For if slowness consisted in his being stationary in some places and moving in others, then it would be known that that slow motion consists of motions mixed with rests, so that person would have done by his choice in some places motion and in some places rest. But it is known that voluntary action does not occur except with intention and choice. And action occurring with intention and choice does not occur except with knowledge of the intended action and the desired matter. So it would be necessary for one who walks slowly to know necessarily that he moved in such-and-such a place and that he stopped in such-and-such a place, because the action he did by his intention and choice must be such that he knows how he chose it and how he brought it about. Since this is not the case—rather, the one who walks slowly believes that he is moving throughout the entire state of slowness—we know that slowness in motion cannot be due to the intercalation of rests.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (al-wajh al-thāmin), a psychological argument based on conscious awareness. The author assumes that if slowness were caused by alternating moments of rest and motion, then a person walking slowly would be performing a series of voluntary actions of motion and rest. But voluntary actions require intention, choice, and knowledge of each specific action. Therefore, the walker would necessarily be aware of each place where he stops and each place where he moves. However, a person walking slowly does not have such awareness; he perceives his movement as continuous. Thus, the slowness cannot be due to intercalated rests.

Key Terms
إنسان عاقل
مشية بطيئة
اختيار
قصد
علم
تخلل السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a slow walker could be unaware of rests because they are too brief or subconscious.
Correction: The argument does not rely on the duration of rests; it relies on the fact that if the walker were voluntarily performing rests (as the theory implies), he would necessarily have knowledge of them. Since he lacks that knowledge, the theory fails.

Checkpoint
What does the walking person argument imply about the awareness of a slow walker?
A) He is aware of every moment of rest
B) He believes his motion is continuous
C) He intentionally rests in some places
D) He knows exactly where he stops

Key Takeaway
The walking person argument proves that slowness in motion is not caused by intercalated rests because a slow walker is consciously unaware of any such rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action that you think is slow or deliberate, are you truly aware of every moment of that action? If the argument proves that slowness is not composed of rests because you would know them, what does that say about your own awareness of your sins? What change must you make today to turn that continuous slowness into continuous movement towards Allah?

The argument shows that voluntary actions require knowledge of their components. Yet many of your daily actions are done without conscious awareness—eating, walking, even praying. Commit to performing one prayer today with full consciousness of every part.

Conclusion: Infinite Divisibility of Body and Time
Derivation of infinite divisibility of body and time from motion comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the comparison of fast and slow motion leads to the conclusion that both body and time are infinitely divisible.

Core Idea
Motion differences demand infinite divisibility of space and time.

This lesson concludes the investigation into the nature of slow and fast motion. After showing that slowness is not caused by intercalated rests, the author draws a crucial metaphysical consequence: if motion is continuous, then the atom (jawhar) and time must be infinitely divisible. The proof is simple but powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and observe its structure.
Notice that the passage begins by summarizing previous arguments that slowness is not due to rests, then announces a necessary consequence, and finally provides a proof by considering fast and slow movers.
The passage is incomplete at the end; we work with what is given.
2
Understand the summary: slowness not from rests.
The first part restates that all the proofs introduced earlier converge on the idea that the difference between fast and slow motion does not involve intercalated rests (takhallul). This refutes an atomist explanation of motion.
Intercalated rests would mean motion is discontinuous.
3
Grasp the necessity: infinite divisibility.
The author asserts that since motion is continuous without rests, body and time must be infinitely divisible. This is a metaphysical claim about the structure of space and time.
This challenges the kalam atomistic view that atoms are indivisible.
4
Follow the proof step by step.
The proof uses a fast mover and a slow mover traversing one atom (jawhar) in some time. If the slow mover took the same time as the fast mover over one atom, they would be equal—impossible. Thus the slow mover must cover less than one atom in that time, implying the atom can be divided. Similarly, the fast mover must take less time than the slow mover over one atom, implying time can be divided.
The argument relies on the given that motions are continuous without rests.
5
Reflect on the conclusion.
This conclusion refutes the atomist theory of indivisible atoms and moments, aligning with the view that space and time are continua. Note the incomplete final sentence; the original text was cut off.
The proof is valid under the assumption of continuous motion.

Know that there are many aspects used by the proponents of the leap (ṭafra). We transmit them and show that they indicate that there may exist two motions free from intercalation, while one is faster than the other. Then all those aspects become indicative that slowness is not due to intercalation of rests. So it is established by these aspects that the difference between fast and slow motion is not due to intercalation of rests. And when this is established, we say: It is necessary that body be divisible infinitely, and also time be divisible infinitely. The proof for it: The fast mover, if it moves over one atom (jawhar) in a measure of time, then in such a time if the slow mover moves over one atom, it would necessitate that the slow is like the fast—which is impossible. Therefore the slow mover must move in such a duration over less than one atom. That necessitates the division of the atom. Also: The slow mover, if it moves over one atom in a measure of time, then the fast mover either moves over that same atom in the same time or in less time. The first is false, otherwise the fast would equal the slow—which is impossible. The second remains: that the fast moves over the one atom in less time than that in which the slow moves over the one atom. So it is established that when the fast and slow are equal in time, the distance...

Commentary
This concluding passage summarizes the multiple proofs (wujūh) that have been presented, all of which show that fast and slow motion differ without any intercalated rests. The author then draws a decisive metaphysical consequence: the physical atom (jawhar) and time must be infinitely divisible. The proof proceeds by considering a fast mover and a slow mover traversing the same atomic distance. If they took the same time, they would be equal—absurd. Therefore the slow mover must cover less than one atom in the same time, implying the atom can be divided, and similarly time must also be divisible to account for the fast mover's shorter time over one atom. This establishes that continuous motion requires continuous divisibility of both space and time, contradicting the atomism of some theologians.

Response to objection about motion and rest
Author refutes objection by distinguishing motion as existent attribute and rest as non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that many motions imply many rests fails, based on the distinction between existent attributes and non-existents.

Core Idea
Rest is not an attribute; it is the absence of motion, so it cannot be perceived.

In this passage, the author addresses a specific objection: if motion and rest interpenetrate, why do we see many motions but not many rests? He first concedes part of the objection, then introduces a crucial distinction between existent attributes (motion) and non-existents (rest). This distinction allows him to argue that rests are imperceptible, so the objection does not apply. He then contrasts this with the case of mixing colors where both are perceptible, further clarifying the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Let the argument unfold naturally. Notice how the author first concedes a point, then denies the critical premise.
Pay attention to the phrase 'non-existents are not perceptible'.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The argument has a concession, a denial, and an analogy with colors. Find each part.
The analogy with black and white colors clarifies the logic.
3
Understand the key distinction
Why does the author say rests are non-existents? How does this distinction resolve the objection?
This distinction is central to the author's response.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The author contrasts motion/rest with black/white. Why does the objection hold in the color case but not here?
This helps cement the concept of perceptibility of existents vs. non-existents.

From his rests many, so we say: this is conceded, no dispute in it. As for his saying: 'If it were so, it would be necessary that those many motions not appear among those rests,' we say: this is not conceded. That is because motions are existent attributes. As for rests, they are expressions of the absence of motions, and non-existents are not perceptible nor visible at all. On this estimation, motions are visible and perceptible, and rests are not perceptible at all. So what you mentioned does not follow. This is unlike when parts described by whiteness mix with parts described by blackness, and the predominant is the parts described by blackness. That is because in this case, both colors, I mean black and white, are perceptible, so what you mentioned follows. But here, motion is perceptible, either essentially or accidentally, while rest is not perceptible. So the difference is clear.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector claimed that if motion and rest were interpenetrated, then many motions should appear among many rests. The author responds in stages: First, he concedes the premise that many motions appear. Second, he denies that rests are observable because rests are non-existence, unlike motions which are existent attributes. Third, he contrasts with the case of mixing black and white where both colors are perceptible, so the objection would apply there. But here, motion is perceptible while rest is not, so the objection fails. The key distinction is between existent attributes (motion) and non-existents (rest).

Heavy Body and Speed Limitation: The Objection and Its Answer
Author argues that increased heaviness does not necessarily increase speed beyond a maximal limit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that increased weight does not necessarily increase speed when motion is already at its maximum limit.

Core Idea
Speed has a natural maximum—extra weight cannot push beyond it.

This lesson explores a key objection to the relationship between weight and speed. The objector claims that if a heavy body's motion is pure (without rests), then increasing weight must increase speed. The author responds by introducing the concept of a maximal limit for speed, arguing that when speed is already at its peak, additional weight has no effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objector's claim: when a body is so heavy that its fall has no rests, further weight must increase speed.
This is the claim the author is responding to.
2
Understand the initial answer
The author replies that a cause only works if the effect is possible; speed has a limit, so extra weight cannot increase speed beyond that limit.
Notice the key concept: 'limit of speed' (حد معين).
3
Examine the further objection
The objector raises a counterargument: the weight sufficient for pure speed is complete; adding weight would either prevent descent or cause an effect without a cause.
This is introduced by 'لا يقال'.
4
See the refutation
The author shows both possibilities are impossible, concluding that the objector's position reduces to the point of dispute.
The author does not accept the objector's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني: وهو قولهم : ( إن الجسم إذا بلغ في الثقل ، بحيث صارت حركاته عند الهوى خالية عن السكنات ، فإذا ازداد ثقله ، وجب أن تزداد سرعة حركاته. فقد حصل التفاوت ههنا في السرعة والبطء ، لا لتخلل السكنات ) فالجواب عنه : إن المقتضي إنما يعمل عمله إذا كان الأثر ممكن الحصول . فإذا بلغت الحركة في السرعة إلى حد معين ، لا يمكن الزيادة عليه . لم يلزم من ازدياد الثقل، ازدياد السرعة. فإن بينتم أن السرعة تقبل الزيادة أبداً ، حصل مقصودكم . إلا أن هذا هو أول المسألة. لا يقال : الدليل عليه : وهو أن الثقل الوافي بإيجاب السرعة الخالصة في الحركة تام لحصول تلك السرعة . فإذا جعل ذلك الجسم أثقل مما كان ، فهذا القدر الزائد من الثقل . لو انفرد لكان مستقلاً باقتضاء لا لمؤثر . فهو أيضاً محال . لأن على التقدير الأول يلزم أن يقال : لما جعل الجسم الثقيل ، أثقل مما كان . فإنه لا ينزل ولا يهوي. وذلك محال . وعلى هذا التقدير الثاني ، يلزم حصول الممكن لا عن مؤثر . وهو محال ، ولما بطلت هذه الأجسام كلها ، فحينئذ لا يبقى إلا أن يقال : إنه لما ازداد الثقل ، وجب أن تزداد السرعة في الحركة . وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب هذا تمام تقرير هذا الكلام .
As for the second aspect, it is their saying: 'When a body reaches such a weight that its motions during fall become free from rests, then if its weight increases, its speed must increase. So variation here in speed and slowness occurs not due to interpolation of rests.' The answer to it: The cause only works if the effect is possible. So when motion reaches a certain limit of speed that cannot be exceeded, no increase in speed follows from increase in weight. If you show that speed always admits increase, then you have attained your objective—except that this is the very point of dispute. It cannot be said: The proof of it is that the weight sufficient to necessitate pure speed in motion is complete for obtaining that speed. So if that body is made heavier than it was, then this extra amount of weight, if it were alone, would be independent in requiring effect without a cause—that is also impossible. Because on the first supposition, it would follow that when the heavy body is made heavier than it was, it does not descend or fall—that is impossible. And on the second supposition, it would follow the occurrence of a possible thing without a cause—that is impossible. So since all these bodies are invalid, then nothing remains except to say that when weight increases, speed in motion must increase—and then the desired is attained. This is the complete exposition of this speech.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a response to the second objection. The objector argues that if a heavy body reaches a weight such that its motions are pure without any rest, then increasing weight should increase speed. The author replies that the cause only works if the effect is possible; speed has a maximum limit, so if it is already at its maximum, extra weight does not increase speed. Then the objector raises an objection (لا يقال): the weight that alone causes pure speed is sufficient for that speed; adding weight would either lead to the absurdity that the heavier body does not fall, or to an effect without a cause. The author refutes both options, showing they are impossible, and concludes that the only remaining possibility is that increased weight increases speed, which is exactly the point under dispute. The author maintains that this is the crux of the matter.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Priority in existence implies priority in causality.

In discussions of causality, one might argue that what comes first deserves greater causal power. This lesson presents an objection that uses this idea to challenge the view that motion arises from total weight. The author's response is unfortunately incomplete, but the force of the objection is clear.

This objection is part of a larger debate on causality.

Two Heavy Bodies and the Impossibility of Two Independent Causes
Analysis of possibilities for speed from two heavinesses and refutation of dual causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speed cannot be caused by both heavinesses independently, because it leads to a logical contradiction of each cause being both necessary and unnecessary.

Core Idea
Two independent causes for a single effect is logically impossible.

This lesson examines a subtle objection about how heaviness can cause both motion and rest. The author analyzes the speed produced when two heavy bodies combine, dividing the possibilities into three. He then refutes the first possibility—that speed comes from both heavinesses—by showing it leads to a contradiction. This argument is a classic example of Kalam reasoning about causality and independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting its argumentative structure. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of reasoning.
The passage is incomplete; focus on the part that is present.
2
Identify the three divisions
The author divides the possible source of speed into three: from both heavinesses, from one, or from neither. Locate these in the text.
Only the first division is fully discussed; the second is started but cut off.
3
Understand the refutation of the first division
The author argues that if speed came from both independent causes, each cause would necessitate the effect, making the effect independent of the other. This leads to each cause being both needed and not needed—a contradiction.
This is the core logical argument against two independent causes for a single effect.

وأما الوجه الثالث: وهو قولهم : ولما كان الثقل موجباً للنزول والهوى . فلم صار بحيث يوجب الحركة في بعض أجزاء المسافة ، ويوجب السكون في البعض الآخر ؟ فنقول : إما أن نثبت كون السرعة أيضاً . فلما اجتمع الثقلان . فالقدر الحاصل من السرعة في الحركة . إما أن يحصل بهذين المقدارين من الثقل ، أو يحصل بأحدهما دون الثاني ، أو لا يحصل بواحد منهما ، والأول باطل . لأنه يقتضي وقوع الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين بالاقتضاء ، وذلك محال . لأن الأثر مع المؤثر المستقل بالاقتضاء يكون واجب الحصول . وما يكون واجب الحصول، كان غنيا عن غيره . فإذا اجتمع على الأثر الواحد : مستقلان مؤثران ، لزم أن يستغني بكل واحد منهما ، عن كل واحد منهما . فيلزم أن يصدق على كل واحد منهما كونه محتاجاً إليه ، وكونه مستغنياً عنه . وإنه محال . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن تقع تلك السرعة بأحد المؤثرين دون الثاني
As for the third aspect, it is their saying: Since heaviness necessitates descent and fall, why does it cause motion in some parts of the distance and cause rest in others? We say: Either we establish that speed also ... So when the two heavinesses combine, the amount of speed obtained in the motion either occurs by these two amounts of heaviness, or occurs by one of them without the other, or occurs by neither of them. The first is invalid because it requires a single effect from two independent causes, which is impossible. For the effect with an independent cause is necessary in occurrence, and what is necessary in occurrence is independent of others. So if two independent causes combine upon a single effect, then it would be that each one makes it independent of the other, leading to each being both needy of the other and independent of it—that is impossible. As for the second division, that the speed occurs by one of the two causes without the other ...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is the third objection and answer. The objector asks: If heaviness causes descent, why does it sometimes cause motion and sometimes rest? The author analyzes the speed resulting from two heavinesses. He divides the possibilities into three: the speed comes from both, from one, or from neither. He then refutes the first: two independent causes for a single effect are impossible because each independent cause would necessitate the effect, making it independent of the other. This leads to a contradiction where each cause is both needed and not needed. The second and third possibilities are left unfinished, but the argument is a classic Kalam refutation of multiple independent causes for the same effect.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple causes can independently work together to produce a single effect without logical issue.
Correction: The author shows that if each cause is fully independent, each would necessitate the effect, making it independent of the other—leading to a logical contradiction. Therefore, two independent causes cannot produce one effect.

Checkpoint

A) Because two causes cannot exist simultaneously.
B) Because each independent cause would make the effect necessary and independent, causing contradiction.
C) Because heaviness does not cause speed.

Key Takeaway
Two independent causes for a single effect are logically impossible because each would make the effect independent of the other, leading to contradiction.

?

?
If every good effect must have a single independent cause, then for every blessing you have, you should thank only Allah and no partner. Think of a recent blessing you attributed to someone else's effort. How can you repent from that and reorient your gratitude exclusively to Allah? Why Weight Causes Alternating Motion and Rest
The author refutes the claim that weight alone determines motion and rest by examining three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why weight cannot be the sole cause of both motion and rest, and how the interaction of weights with the medium explains alternating motion and rest.

Core Idea
Weight alone cannot cause both motion and rest; they alternate due to the medium's resistance.

In this passage, the author tackles a challenge to the claim that weight is the cause of descent. If weight always causes descent, why does it sometimes produce motion and sometimes rest? The answer lies in examining how weights combine and how the medium interacts with the falling body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three possibilities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author lists three possibilities for how the speed is produced when two weights combine.
The three possibilities are: speed from both weights, from one, or from none.
2
Understand why the first two possibilities are impossible
The first possibility (both weights cause speed) leads to two independent causes for one effect, which is impossible. The second (one weight causes speed) requires unjustified preference, also impossible.
These arguments rely on principles of causality: one effect cannot have two independent causes, and preference without a preponderator is impossible.
3
Analyze the third possibility and its implications
The third possibility (neither weight causes speed, i.e., they cancel) is then discussed in terms of the world being a plenum (mala') or void (khala'). In a plenum, air resistance causes alternating motion and rest; in a void, the stone would fall at maximum speed.
This shows that the observed alternation is due to the medium, not weight directly causing rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثالث : وهو قولهم : ولما كان الثقل موجباً للنزول والهوى . فلم صار بحيث يوجب الحركة في بعض أجزاء المسافة ، ويوجب السكون في البعض الآخر ؟ فنقول : إما أن نثبت كون السرعة أيضاً . فلما اجتمع الثقلان . فالقدر الحاصل من [ السرعة ] في الحركة . إما أن يحصل بهذين المقدارين من الثقل ، أو يحصل بأحدهما دون الثاني ، أو لا يحصل بواحد منهما ، والأول باطل . لأنه يقتضي وقوع الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين بالاقتضاء ، وذلك محال . لأن الأثر [ مع ] المؤثر المستقل بالاقتضاء يكون واجب الحصول . وما يكون واجب الحصول، كان غنيا عن غيره . فإذا اجتمع على الأثر الواحد : مستقلان مؤثران ، لزم أن يستغني بكل واحد منهما ، عن كل واحد منهما . فيلزم أن يصدق على كل واحد منهما كونه محتاجاً إليه ، وكـونه مستغنياً عنه . وإنه محال. وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن تقع تلك السرعة بأحد المؤثرين دون الثاني فهو أيضاً باطل . لأن كل واحد من الثقلين، مستقل باقتضاء ذلك القدر من السرعة . فلو ترجح أحدهما على الآخر، في كونه مقتضياً لذلك الأثر ، لزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، وهو محال. وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يندفع كل واحد منهما بالآخر ، ولا يحصل الأثر ، أو يحصل العالم ملاء أو تجوز حصول الخلاء فيه فإن قلنا : بالملاء فلا شك أن الحجر النازل ، لا بد وأن يخرق اتصال الهواء فيه ، عند نزول الحجر ، ويتصلب . وإذا كان كذلك ، فالهواء إذا تصلب وتلبد ، وقف الحجر ، وإذا وقف زالت تلك الصلابة ، فحينئذ ينزل ولا تزال هذه الأحوال تتعاقب وبسبيها تتعاقب الحركات والسكنات . وأما إذا أثبتنا داخل العالم ، فعلى هذا التقرير ، لا نقول : العالم كله خلاء . لأنا نرى أن الهواء إذا تموج فقد يبلغ تموج الهواء في القوة إلى حيث يقلع الجبال ، ويهدم الصخور ، ويموج البحار والعدم المحض لا يكون كذلك ، وحينئذ يعود الوجه الذي ذكرناه على تقرير كون العالم ملاء نعم . لو قدرنا أحيازاً خالية عن جميع الأجسام . فعلى ذلك التقدير ، وجب أن يبلغ نزول الحجر إلى أقصى درجات السرعة.
As for the third face: it is their statement: 'Since weight is a cause of descent and fall, why did it become such that it causes motion in some parts of the distance and causes rest in others?' So we say: either we affirm the existence of speed as well. Then when the two weights combine, the amount of speed that occurs in the motion either comes about by these two amounts of weight, or comes about by one of them without the other, or does not come about by either of them. The first is false because it entails the occurrence of one effect by two independent efficient causes, and that is impossible. Because the effect with an independent efficient cause is necessary in existence, and whatever is necessary in existence is independent of other than itself. So if two independent efficient causes come together upon one effect, it would follow that [the effect] is independent of each one by each one, so that each one would be both in need of the other and independent of it, and that is impossible. As for the second division, that that speed occurs by one of the two causes without the other, it is also false. Because each of the two weights is independent in requiring that amount of speed. So if one of them is preferred over the other in being a requirer of that effect, it would entail the preponderance of one of the two sides of a possible over the other without a preponderator, and that is impossible. As for the third division, that each of the two is repelled by the other and the effect does not occur, or the world is full (mala') or it is permissible that there is void (khala') in it. If we say it is full, then there is no doubt that the descending stone must break the continuity of the air when the stone descends, and it becomes compacted. And if that is so, then when the air becomes compacted and matted, the stone stops, and when it stops, that compactness disappears, so then it descends, and these states continue to alternate, and due to them motion and rest alternate. As for if we affirm [something] inside the world, then according to this exposition we do not say that the entire world is void, because we see that when air undulates, its undulation may reach such force that it uproots mountains, demolishes rocks, and agitates seas, and pure non-existence (al-'adam al-mahd) is not like that. Then the face we mentioned returns upon the exposition that the world is full. Yes, if we suppose spaces empty of all bodies, then upon that supposition the descent of the stone must attain the utmost degree of speed.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that weight (al-thiql) necessarily causes motion in some parts of the distance and rest in others. The author presents the problem: why does weight, which causes descent, sometimes cause motion and sometimes rest? He then examines three possibilities regarding the speed produced when two weights combine. First possibility: the speed comes from both weights together. This is impossible because it would require two independent causes producing one effect, leading to the contradiction that each cause is both needed and not needed. Second possibility: the speed comes from only one of the two weights. This is also impossible because it would require one cause to be preferred over another equally capable cause without a preponderator (tarjih bila murajjih). Third possibility: neither weight produces the speed, i.e., they cancel each other out. The author then discusses this in terms of whether the world is a plenum (mala') or void (khala'). If plenum, the stone's descent compresses the air, causing it to harden and stop the stone; then the air relaxes, the stone falls again, creating alternating motion and rest. If void, the stone would fall uninterrupted at maximum speed. This shows that the observed alternation is due to air resistance, not weight directly causing rest.

مؤثر (mu'aththir)
أثر (athar)
حركة (harakah)
سكون (sukūn)
ملاء (mala')
خلاء (khala')
قدر (qadr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that weight independently determines both motion and rest, i.e., weight directly causes rest in some parts of the distance.
Correction: The author shows this leads to absurd consequences. Instead, motion and rest alternate due to the resistance of the medium (air), not weight itself causing rest.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The observed alternation of motion and rest is not due to weight alone but to the interaction of gravitational force with air resistance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

But this lesson shows that even weight does not directly cause both; it is the interaction with the medium. ?

The Eighth Answer: Fatigue as a Cause of Alternating Motion and Rest
The author answers the eighth objection by citing fatigue as a cause for alternating motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author answers the objection that motion and rest occur without cause by proposing fatigue as a natural explanation.

Core Idea
Fatigue explains why a walker alternates between motion and rest.

In this passage, the author addresses the eighth objection by providing a simple yet profound example: a walker who becomes tired must pause to rest. Once rested, strength returns and motion resumes. This demonstrates that alternating motion and rest can have a natural cause, undermining the objection that such alternation is arbitrary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: the author uses a question format to present his answer.
The passage is a single rhetorical question that contains the whole argument.
2
Identify key terms
Find terms like weakness (al-'ajz), fatigue (al-i'ya'), strength (al-quwwah), motion (harakah), rest (sukūn), and places (ahyāz). These are essential to understand the causal chain.
3
Analyze the causal chain
Trace the cause: fatigue leads to a pause, rest allows recovery of strength, then motion resumes. This shows the alternation has a natural cause.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this answer refutes the objection: by showing a plausible cause, the objector's claim of no cause is invalid. The author does not need to prove fatigue actually occurs, only that it is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثامن . فالجواب عنه : أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الماشي حصل في أعضائه العجز والإعياء ، فلأجل حصول هذه الحالة ، يتوقف على بعض الأحياز. فإذا توقف قليلا ، زال ذلك الإعياء ، وعادته القوة ، فيقوى على الحركة ، فلاجل هذا السبب ، يفعل ذلك الفاعل في بعض الأحياز: الحركة ، وفي بعضها : السكون ؟
As for the eighth face, the answer to it is that it may be said: why is it not permissible that the walker experiences weakness (al-'ajz) and fatigue (al-i'ya') in his limbs, so due to this state he pauses in some places. When he pauses for a while, that fatigue goes away and his strength returns, so he becomes able to move. Therefore, due to this reason, that agent does motion in some places and rest in others?
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the eighth objection. The author proposes a plausible explanation for why a moving object might alternately move and rest. Using the example of a walker, he suggests that internal physical states—weakness and fatigue—can cause pauses. When the walker rests, fatigue disappears and strength returns, allowing motion to resume. Thus, the alternation of motion and rest is not arbitrary but has a cause (fatigue and recovery). This shows that the objection that motion and rest occur without cause is invalid because a natural cause (fatigue) can explain the pattern.
