Motion in a Plenum and the Supposition of Void
Al-Razi examines the third case of motion: plenum vs. void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi analyzes the third case of motion, considering both a plenum and a void, and why he argues that motion in a pure void would be instantaneous.

Core Idea
Motion in a void would be instantaneous with maximum speed.

Al-Razi continues his investigation of motion by examining the third case: what happens when two forces oppose each other and no effect occurs, or when considering the world as either a plenum or containing void. In this lesson, we explore his detailed analysis of a falling stone in a plenum and the implications of supposing void.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of the third case
The passage begins by stating the third division. It then presents two sub-scenarios: plenum or void. Clarify this structure.
Notice that the passage starts with 'وأما القسم الثالث' which sets the context.
2
Examine the plenum scenario
Read how al-Razi describes the stone's descent causing air to solidify, stopping the stone, then relaxing, leading to alternating motion and rest.
The key words are 'تصلب' (solidify) and 'تلبد' (compact).
3
Consider the void scenario
Note that he does not assert the world is entirely void; rather, he argues that if we suppose empty regions, the stone would achieve maximum speed because no resistance exists.
He says 'لا نقول: العالم كله خلاء' — we do not say the whole world is void.
4
Reflect on the argument's conclusion
The passage ends by stating that on the supposition of void, the stone's descent must reach the utmost degree of speed, implying instantaneous motion.
This is a key conclusion for kalam discussions of void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يندفع كل واحد منهما بالآخر ، ولا يحصل الأثر ، أو يحصل العالم ملاء أو تجوز حصول الخلاء (۲) فيه فإن قلنا : بالملاء فلا شك أن الحجر النازل ، لا بد وأن يخرق اتصال الهواء فيه ، عند نزول الحجر ، ويتصلب . وإذا كان كذلك ، فالهواء إذا تصلب وتلبد ، وقف الحجر ، وإذا وقف زالت تلك الصلابة ، فحينئذ ينزل ولا تزال هذه الأحوال تتعاقب وبسبيها تتعاقب الحركات والسكنات . وأما إذا أثبتنا داخل العالم ، فعلى هذا التقرير ، لا نقول : العالم كله خلاء . لأنا نرى أن الهواء إذا تموج فقد يبلغ تموج الهواء في القوة إلى حيث يقلع الجبال ، ويهدم الصخور ، ويموج البحار والعدم المحض لا يكون كذلك ، وحينئذ يعود الوجه الذي ذكرناه على تقرير كون العالم ملاء نعم . لو قدرنا أحيازاً خالية عن جميع الأجسام . فعلى ذلك التقدير ، وجب أن يبلغ نزول الحجر إلى أقصى درجات السرعة
As for the third division: it is that each of the two [forces] is repelled by the other, and no effect occurs, or the world is a plenum, or void (khalaʾ) is assumed to exist in it. For if we say: [the world is] a plenum, then there is no doubt that a descending stone must necessarily rupture the continuity of the air in it, when the stone descends, and [the air] becomes solid. And when this is the case, when the air becomes solid and compacted, the stone stops, and when it stops that solidity disappears, then it descends, and these states keep alternating, and due to them motions and rests alternate. And if we posit [void] inside the world, according to this formulation we do not say: the whole world is void. For we see that when air undulates, its undulation may reach such force that it uproots mountains, demolishes rocks, and agitates seas, and pure non-being is not like that. Then the argument we mentioned returns upon the formulation that the world is a plenum. Yes, if we suppose regions devoid of all bodies, then upon that supposition, the descent of the stone must reach the utmost degree of speed.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage classifies the third case in the discussion of motion and void. It presents two sub-scenarios: either the world is a plenum (completely filled with matter) or there is void within it. In the plenum scenario, the author explains that when a stone falls, it compresses the air, which then solidifies and stops the stone; the stone then falls again as the air relaxes, causing alternating motion and rest. In the void scenario, however, if we assume empty spaces, the stone would achieve maximum speed because no medium resists. The author notes that we do not claim the entire world is void, because air can exert force (e.g., wind), which pure nothingness cannot. Thus, the argument that motion in void would be instantaneous holds only if there are regions devoid of bodies.

Key Terms
القسم الثالث
ملاء
خلاء
تصلب
تموج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Razi is defending the existence of void.
Correction: He only considers void as a supposition to argue that motion in pure void would be instantaneous; he does not assert its actual existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the air when a stone descends in a plenum?
A) The air becomes void and allows maximum speed.
B) The air becomes solid and stops the stone, then relaxes.
C) The air moves along with the stone.
D) The air disappears.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that in a plenum, motion involves alternating compression and relaxation of the medium, while in a pure void, motion would be instantaneous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think you are moving toward a goal with no obstacles, are you ignoring the real forces that will compress and stop you? How have you prepared for the alternating cycles of effort and pause that every meaningful journey requires?
?
Al-Razi shows that even in a plenum, motion is possible through alternating compression and release. Where in your life have you mistaken a temporary stop for permanent failure? What would change if you saw the stop as part of the movement itself?
?
The void is a supposition that leads to extreme speed—but Al-Razi does not affirm it. Where do you act as if your life exists in a void, independent of all influence? How is that assumption dangerous for your faith and actions?

Answer to the Eighth Objection: The Walker's Fatigue
Al-Razi answers the eighth objection by explaining the walker's pauses due to fatigue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's explanation that a walker's pauses are due to temporary fatigue and recovery, not an external necessity.

Core Idea
Fatigue causes pause; recovery causes movement.

Al-Razi now addresses an objection that might assume motion must be continuous. He provides a simple physical example: a walker tires, rests, and regains strength, showing that alternation of motion and rest arises from internal states, not metaphysical compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: objection to answer.
The passage begins with 'وأما الوجه الثامن' (And as for the eighth argument).
2
Identify the cause of the pause
Find the phrase that explains why the walker pauses. The text says 'حصل في أعضائه العجز والإعياء' (acquires incapacity and fatigue).
The pause is due to internal physical state, not external force.
3
Understand the cyclical explanation
Trace the cycle: fatigue → pause → recovery → strength → movement. This shows how motion and rest alternate naturally.
The cycle is presented as a reasonable explanation for intermittent motion.
4
Note the conclusion
The passage ends with 'وههنا آخر الكلام في الجواب عن هذه الكلمات' (Here ends the speech). This concludes the set of objections and answers.
Al-Razi ends with 'والله أعلم' (And God knows best).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثامن . فالجواب عنه : أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الماشي حصل في أعضائه العجز والإعياء ، فلأجل حصول هذه الحالة ، يتوقف على بعض الأحياز. فإذا توقف قليلا ، زال ذلك الإعياء ، وعادته القوة ، فيقوى على الحركة ، فلاجل هذا السبب ، يفعل ذلك الفاعل في بعض الأحياز: الحركة ، وفي بعضها : السكون ؟ وههنا آخر الكلام في الجواب عن هذه الكلمات . والله أعلم
And as for the eighth argument, the answer to it is: it is said: why not say that the walker has acquired incapacity and fatigue in his limbs, and due to the occurrence of this state, he pauses in some places. Then if he pauses a little, that fatigue disappears, and strength returns to him, so he is able to move. Therefore, for this reason, that agent performs motion in some places and rest in others? And here ends the speech on the answer to these words. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the eighth objection, which apparently claimed that motion must be continuous or something similar. The answer is that a walker experiences fatigue (iʿyāʾ) and incapacity (ʿajz) in his limbs, so he pauses. After a brief stop, fatigue disappears and strength returns, allowing him to move again. Thus, the alternation of motion and rest is due to internal physical states, not an external necessity. The passage concludes the discussion of these arguments with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms

الإعياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the answer denies the possibility of continuous motion entirely.
Correction: The answer does not deny continuous motion; rather, it explains why motion can be intermittent due to internal states like fatigue and recovery.

Checkpoint
What causes the walker to pause according to the answer to the eighth objection?
A) External obstacles.
B) Fatigue and incapacity in his limbs.
C) The nature of void.
D) The will to stop.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses the natural cycle of fatigue and recovery to argue that the alternation of motion and rest in a walker is due to internal causes, not external necessity.

The Pulley Proof: Infinite Divisibility and Speed Variation
Rāzī's third proof using a pulley to establish infinite divisibility and speed variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the pulley thought experiment demonstrates the infinite divisibility of distance and time, and the possibility of speed variation without intervening rests.

Core Idea
A pulley's motion proves that space and time are infinitely divisible and speed can vary without pauses.

Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī presents a clever physical analogy to establish three key metaphysical claims about motion. By examining a simple pulley system, he shows that the same time can accommodate vastly different distances, implying that both space and time must be infinitely divisible. This lesson unpacks the logic of his third proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the scenario. Then read the translation to understand the logic.
Note the specific numbers: a cubit vs 64 cubits, then a handspan vs 100 million cubits.
2
Identify the three conclusions
Look for the phrase المطالب الثلاثة and list them: انقسام المسافة أبداً, انقسام الزمان أبداً, and التفاوت في السرعة والبطء من غير تخلل السكنات.
These are the core claims that the proof establishes.
3
Understand the logical mechanism
The key is that the same time interval accommodates vastly different distances. This implies that time cannot be atomic, and distance must be continuous.
The pulley demonstrates a proportional relationship: the bucket moves faster than the hook without pausing.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these conclusions matter for natural philosophy: they oppose atomistic views of space and time and allow continuous motion.
This proof is part of a larger debate about the nature of motion and time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انجر ذلك المعلاق إلى رأس البئر ، انجر الدلو من أسفل البشر إلى أعلاه ففي الزمان الذي انتقل المعلاق الأعلى إلى رأس البئر وهو مقدار ذراع انتقل الدلو من أسفل البئر إلى أعلاه ، وهو أربعة وستون ذراعاً . واعلم : أنا لو فرضنا طول البئر مائة ألف ألف ذراع، وعملنا العمل المذكور ، فإنه حال ما يتحرك المعلاق الأعلى : شبراً ، فإنه يجب أن ينتقل الدلو من الأسفل إلى الأعلى ، مع كون تلك المسافة مائة ألف ألف ذراع . وإذا عرفت ذلك ، فهذا يدل على ثبوت المطالب الثلاثة : وهي انقسام المسافة أبداً ، وانقسام الزمان أبداً ، وحصول التفاوت في السرعة والبطء من غير تخلل السكنات
That hook dragged to the top of the well, it dragged the bucket from the bottom of the well to its top. So in the time that the upper hook moved to the top of the well – which is the measure of a cubit – the bucket moved from the bottom of the well to its top, which is sixty-four cubits. Know that if we suppose the depth of the well a hundred thousand thousand cubits, and we perform the mentioned operation, then at the moment the upper hook moves a handspan, the bucket must move from the bottom to the top, despite that distance being a hundred thousand thousand cubits. And when you have understood that, this indicates the establishment of three claims: the infinite divisibility of distance, the infinite divisibility of time, and the occurrence of variation in speed and slowness without the interpolation of rests.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujjah al-thālithah) for the indivisibility of distance and time and the variation of speed without rests. The author, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī, uses the example of a pulley (al-miʿlāq) drawing water from a well. When the upper hook moves a small distance (a cubit), the bucket moves a much larger distance (sixty-four cubits) in the same time. This demonstrates that the ratio of distances is not fixed; the same time accommodates different distances. Then he extends the thought experiment: even if the well were extremely deep (100 million cubits), a slight movement of the hook (a handspan) would cause the bucket to traverse that enormous distance instantly (in the same time). The conclusion is that this proves three things: (1) distance is infinitely divisible (because the bucket's motion covers a huge distance without discrete steps), (2) time is infinitely divisible (since the motion occurs in a single continuous time), and (3) variation in speed and slowness occurs without necessity of intervening rests (because the bucket moves faster than the hook without pausing).

Key Terms
المعلاق
الدلو
انقسام المسافة
انقسام الزمان
التفاوت في السرعة والبطء
السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pulley proof is a physical demonstration of actual pulleys.
Correction: It is a logical thought experiment to show necessary properties of motion, not an empirical claim about real pulleys.

Checkpoint
What three conclusions does the pulley proof establish?
A) Existence of atoms, finite time, and uniform speed
B) Infinite divisibility of distance, infinite divisibility of time, and variation in speed without rests
C) That motion requires rests, that space is finite, and that time is atomic
D) That the bucket moves slower than the hook, and that distance is indivisible

Key Takeaway
The pulley proof shows that motion requires continuous infinite divisibility of space and time, and allows speed variation without pauses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make small daily efforts (like a hook moving a handspan), do you trust that they lead to large outcomes (like the bucket rising)? This lesson shows that in God's creation, small motions produce great movements without gaps. How does this challenge your impatience with slow progress?
?
The proof shows that speed variation occurs without rests. Where in your spiritual life are you seeking a 'rest' that might actually be a pause in your movement toward Allah? What if the journey requires continuous motion, even at varying speeds, without stopping?
?
Reflect: If time is infinitely divisible, every moment is filled with divine action. When you find yourself thinking 'this moment is empty' or 'nothing is happening', what does this lesson tell you about hidden movement? How can you become more aware of Allah's constant action in your life?

Ship and Man in Opposite Motion
Al-Razi presents the fourth proof using a ship and a man moving opposite its motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ship-and-man analogy demonstrates relative motion and its implications for the possibility of motion without rests.

Core Idea
Equal opposite motions cancel; greater opposite motion forces either leap or continuous variation.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from al-Razi's argumentation. It uses a simple thought experiment involving a ship and a man walking opposite to its motion to illustrate how relative motion can lead to apparent stationariness or necessitate doctrines about the nature of motion itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: what happens when the man's motion equals the ship's motion versus when it is greater.
Pay attention to the terms 'ṭafrah' (leap) and 'تفاوت في السرعة والبطء' (variation in speed and slowness).
2
Identify the two cases
The proof considers two scenarios: (1) the man moves exactly as much as the ship moves, and (2) the man moves more. Work through each scenario logically.
The first case leads to the man being stationary; the second case branches into two possible interpretations.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on why this proof is relevant to kalam debates about motion: if equal opposite motions cancel, that explains apparent stationariness. If the man moves more, it supports either discontinuous motion (ṭafrah) or continuous variation.
The mention of 'wandering planets' (الكواكب المتحيرة) shows this proof was used in astronomy.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and consider the common misunderstanding to solidify your grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إذا فرضنا سفينة تتحرك ، إلى جانب . وفرضنا إنساناً كان في تلك السفينة ، وهو يتحرك إلى ضد حركة السفينة . ففي الزمان الذي تحركت السفينة بمقدار جزء. إن تحرك الرجل بمقدار جزء ، ذهب الزائد بالناقص . فيلزم : أن يبقى الرجل واقفاً في مكانه . وهذا هو السبب في وقوف الكواكب المتحيرة في الرؤية . وأما إن تحرك أكثر ، لزم القول بالطفرة على قول البعض ، والتفاوت في السرعة والبطء على قول الحكماء
The fourth proof: If we suppose a ship moving to one side, and we suppose a man in that ship moving opposite to the ship's motion. Then in the time that the ship moved by a unit, if the man moves by a unit, the excess cancels the deficiency. So it follows that the man remains stationary in his place. This is the cause of the apparent stationariness of the wandering planets. But if he moves more, then the doctrine of leap (ṭafrah) follows according to some, and variation in speed and slowness according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-ḥujjah al-rābiʿah). It imagines a ship moving in one direction, and a man on the ship walking in the opposite direction. If the ship's displacement in a given time is one unit, and the man walks exactly one unit relative to the ship, then his net displacement relative to the shore is zero – he appears stationary. This explains why retrograde planets appear stationary at times. If the man walks more than one unit, then he moves relative to the shore; this leads either to the doctrine of leap (ṭafrah) – a discontinuous motion – according to some, or to variation in speed and slowness without rests, according to the philosophers. The author presents this as an argument for the possibility of motion without intervening rests.

Key Terms
السفينة
الحركة المضادة
الطفرة
التفاوت في السرعة والبطء
الكواكب المتحيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is about real ships and man walking on a ship in a practical sense.
Correction: The proof is a thought experiment about relative motion. It abstracts away many physical details to illustrate a logical point about motion and rest.

Checkpoint
What does the ship-and-man proof imply if the man moves more than the ship's displacement?
A) The man must move backwards relative to the ship
B) Either the doctrine of leap (ṭafrah) or variation in speed and slowness
C) The ship must stop
D) The man's motion is impossible

Key Takeaway
The ship-and-man proof shows that opposite motions can cancel, and if they don't, it forces a choice between discontinuous motion (leap) or continuous variation in speed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the 'motions' of your worldly pursuits and your spiritual duties, are they moving in opposite directions such that they cancel each other out? What would it mean for your soul to move 'more' in the direction of the truth, even if it requires a leap?
?
The philosophers argued for continuous variation, while the mutakallimun posited leaps. In your own journey of faith, do you expect gradual change or sudden transformation? Which belief drives your actions today, and are you actively seeking either?
?
The man in the ship appeared stationary to an outside observer. In what areas of your life do you feel you are moving (effort, worship) yet appear stationary in Allah's sight because of opposing motions? How can you break that cancellation before you meet Him?

The Fifth and Sixth Proofs: Instantaneous Light
Al-Rāzī enumerates two proofs from light's instantaneous arrival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments from instantaneous light that are used to support the theory of leap (ṭafrah) and the concept of infinite speed gradation.

Core Idea
Instantaneous light suggests either leap or unlimited speed.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī presents two proofs based on the phenomenon of light reaching us instantly. These proofs are used by proponents of atomistic leap (ṭafrah) and those who argue for varying speeds without rest. Let's examine how sensory observations of light lead to these metaphysical conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the concise style of proof presentation.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two proofs: the rising sun and the unblocked window. Locate each and note the phenomenon described.
3
Understand the two possibilities
Both proofs offer two explanatory possibilities: leap (ṭafrah) or infinite gradation of speed. Consider what each means.
The text says 'لا يمكن إلا بالطفرة، أو لأجل أنه لا نهاية المراتب السرعة'.
4
Notice the concluding summary
Al-Rāzī states that these are all the arguments in the chapter and notes that both groups (proponents of leap and proponents of varying speed) use them.
The phrase 'والله أعلم' is a standard closing.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Each proof is a conditional: if light travels instantly, then either leap or infinite speed. Consider the implications for atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الشمس كلما تطلع ، وصلت الأنوار في الحال إلينا ، دفعة واحدة . والأنوار أجسام. وقطع هذه الأجسام ، هذه المسافة العظيمة ، في هذه اللحظة اللطيفة ، لا يمكن إلا بالطفرة ، أو لأجل أنه لا نهاية المراتب السرعة . الحجة السادسة : إنا إذا سددنا الكوة . خرجت الأجزاء النورانية ، دفعة واحدة . وهذا لا يمكن إلا بالطفرة . فهذه جملة الوجوه المذكورة في هذا الباب . والقائلون بالطفر يتمسكون بها في إثبات الطفرة ، والقائلون بحصول حركة ، أسرع من حركة أخرى ، مع خلوها عن مخالطة كل السكنات . قد يتمسكون بها أيضاً . والله أعلم .
The fifth proof: Whenever the sun rises, the lights reach us instantly, all at once. Lights are bodies. And these bodies traversing this immense distance in this tiny moment is possible only by leap (ṭafrah), or because there is no limit to the ranks of speed. The sixth proof: When we block a window [and then open it], the light-parts come out all at once. This is possible only by leap. So these are the sum of the arguments mentioned in this chapter. The proponents of leap use them to prove leap, and the proponents of motion faster than another motion, free from any mixture of rests, also use them. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and sixth proofs (al-ḥujjah al-khāmisah wa al-sādisah). The fifth proof: sunlight reaches us instantly at sunrise. Since light is corporeal (ajsām), traversing the vast distance in an instant implies either discrete leap (ṭafrah) – jumping over intermediate points – or that speed has no limit. The sixth proof: when we unblock a window, light-parts emerge instantaneously, which also implies leap. The author then summarizes that these are all the arguments mentioned in this chapter. He notes that both the proponents of leap and the proponents of continuous motion with varying speed use these same arguments. The phrase 'والله أعلم' indicates a standard closing.

Key Terms
الأنوار
الطفرة
لا نهاية المراتب السرعة

الأجزاء النورانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about physical light transmission as we understand it today (e.g., photons traveling at finite speed).
Correction: Al-Rāzī uses a thought experiment: if light appears instantaneously, then either it leaps over intermediate points or there is no limit to speed gradation. The argument is metaphysical, not empirical physics.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The instantaneous arrival of light provides empirical support for either atomistic leap or the possibility of infinite speed gradation.

Response to the First Two Proofs on the Indivisible Part
Proponents of the indivisible part answer the first two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents of the indivisible part respond to the first two proofs by denying a key premise rather than rejecting the conclusion.

Core Idea
Denying a premise is a valid response: the possibility of relative motion is not obvious.

In this passage, the advocates of the indivisible part (al-juzʾ alladhī lā yatajazzaʾ) directly address the first two proofs against their position. Instead of conceding or offering a full refutation, they point out a hidden assumption—that a located body can move relative to its moving place in either direction. They challenge this assumption, arguing that its possibility must be demonstrated. This is a classic dialectical move in kalam: undermining the premise rather than the argument itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording come through.
Notice the repeated structure: 'they answered them by saying...'
2
Identify the denied premise
Find the assumption that the proponents of the indivisible part reject. It is the claim that 'it is possible for the located entity to move, when its place moves, to the direction of its place's motion, or opposite that direction.'
They deny the possibility (jawāz) of such motion, not its actual occurrence.
3
Understand the dialectical tactic
Reflect on why this response is effective: by denying a premise, they force the opponent to provide evidence for that premise. The burden of proof shifts, and the argument is suspended until that is done.
This is a common strategy in kalām debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بإثبات الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ : فقد أجابوا عنها : أما الحجة الأولى والثانية : فقد أجابوا عنهما : بأن قالوا : إنها مبنية على أن المتمكن يجوز أن يتحرك ، حال حركة مكانه ، إلى جهة حركة مكانه ، أو إلى خلاف تلك الجهة . وهذا الجواز ممنوع . فلا بد من إقامة الدلالة على الجواز .
And as for those who affirm the indivisible part, they answered them: As for the first and second proofs, they answered them by saying: They are based on the [assumption] that it is possible for the located entity to move, when its place moves, to the direction of its place's motion, or opposite that direction. This possibility is denied. So proof of its possibility must be given.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage gives the response of the proponents of the indivisible part (al-juzʾ alladhī lā yatajazzaʾ) to the first and second proofs. They argue that those proofs assume that a body located in a moving place can move either with the motion of its place or against it. But this assumption is not granted; it requires proof. In other words, it is not evident that a body in a moving ship, for example, can move relative to the ship in any direction while the ship moves; this possibility must be demonstrated. By denying this possibility, they undermine the first two proofs.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
المتمكن
حركة المكان
الجواز
الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proponents of the indivisible part reject the proofs outright and assert that motion in a moving place is impossible.
Correction: They do not assert impossibility; they deny that the possibility (jawāz) is evident. They demand proof for that possibility, leaving open whether it might be possible or not until evidence is provided.

Checkpoint
On what assumption do the first two proofs rely according to the response?
A) That motion is impossible in a vacuum
B) That a located body can move relative to its moving place in either direction
C) That space is indivisible
D) That time is atomic

Key Takeaway
The proponents of the indivisible part respond to the first two proofs by denying the premise that a body can move relative to its moving place in opposite directions; they demand proof of that possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Response to the Third Proof: Intervening Rests
الرد على الحجة الثالثة بادعاء تخلل السكنات في حركة المعلاق

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents of atomism respond to the continuity of motion argument by invoking intervening rests or lateral motion.

Core Idea
Intervening rests or sideways motion prevent infinite divisibility in the pulley.

In this passage, the author addresses the third proof that attempted to show that a smooth pulley motion requires infinite divisibility. The response is straightforward: the hook's motion is not perfectly continuous; it either has rests (sakanāt) or moves side to side. Without these, the rope would snap. This shows the opponent's thought experiment fails in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the compact argument structure.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Find the two possibilities offered for the hook's motion: either it is interspersed with rests, or it moves right and left.
These are presented as necessary conditions for the bucket to move.
3
Understand the consequence
If neither condition holds, the rope would break. This is the logical 'or else' clause that refutes the opponent's assumption of smooth motion.
4
Reflect on the refutation strategy
Consider why this response works: by denying the premise of continuous motion, the atomist avoids the conclusion that motion requires infinite divisibility.
This is a typical kalam method of rejecting a hypothetical scenario with physical constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثالثة : فالجواب عنها : إن حركة المعلاق . لا بد وأن يتخللها السكنات ، وإما أن يذهب ذلك المعلاق يميناً ويساراً . فإن خلا المعلاق عن هذين النوعين ، فلا نسلم إمكان حركة الدلو ، بل ينقطع ذلك الحبل
As for the third proof, the answer to it is: The motion of the hook must be interspersed with rests, or else that hook moves right and left. If the hook is free of both these types, then we do not grant the possibility of the bucket's motion; rather, that rope would break.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage gives the response to the third proof (the pulley). The proponents of the indivisible part argue that the hook's motion is not continuous; it is either interspersed with rests (sakanāt) or it involves lateral motion (right and left). If neither occurs, then the rope would break and the bucket could not move. In other words, the seemingly smooth motion of the pulley actually consists of discrete jerks or oscillations, which prevent the infinite divisibility claim. By asserting that rests or lateral motions are present, they deny the premise that the motion is continuous without rests.

Key Terms
السكنات
المعلاق
اليمين واليسار
انقطاع الحبل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The response accepts the thought experiment as presented and merely offers an alternative explanation.
Correction: The response does not accept the premise of smooth continuous motion; it asserts that physical constraints (rope breaking) would occur, thus rejecting the entire scenario.

Checkpoint
What two possibilities does the response offer for the hook's motion in the third proof?
A) It moves in a straight line or a circle
B) It is interspersed with rests or moves right and left
C) It moves up and down
D) It moves with the wind

Key Takeaway
The third proof is refuted by showing that pulley motion is not perfectly continuous but necessarily involves rests or lateral movements, otherwise the rope would break.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The rope would break if the hook moved without breaks or oscillation. Consider something you are driving forward relentlessly—a habit, a goal, a relationship. If you refuse to allow rests or adjustments, what 'rope' might snap? What would a wise rest or change of direction look like?
?
The third proof is based on a theoretical abstraction that ignores physical reality. Are there areas of your faith where you treat purely theoretical ideals as necessary, ignoring the concrete realities of your human limitations? How can you ground your practice in what is actually commanded and possible for you, rather than an abstract standard? Write down one such ideal and one practical adjustment.

Impossible Co-location: Refuting Continuous Motion
Refutation of the ship-man argument for atoms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of a body being in two places at once refutes the argument that continuous motion without rests allows relative movement.

Core Idea
Continuous motion without rests would force a body into two opposite places simultaneously.

In this passage, the author responds to a subtle objection that tries to prove the existence of indivisible atoms using the ship-man example. The opponent claims that if the ship moves continuously (no rests), the man can still move voluntarily in the opposite direction. But the author shows that this would require the man's body to occupy two different places at the same instant, which is rationally impossible. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage silently, then read the English translation. Notice how the author structures the objection and his response.
Pay attention to the logical flow: premise, supposition, consequence, conclusion.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms: 'السكنات' (rests), 'الحركة الاختيارية' (voluntary motion), 'الحيزان المختلفان' (two different places), 'المحال' (impossible).
These terms are central to understanding the argument.
3
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Imagine a ship moving smoothly without any pauses. A man on the ship chooses to walk in the exact opposite direction of the ship's movement. The author says this would force the man to be in two places at once.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Why is being in two places at once impossible? In Islamic theology, a body cannot occupy two spatial locations simultaneously. This is a basic rational principle.
The author uses this principle to show the opponent's assumption leads to a contradiction.
5
Connect to the broader debate
This argument refutes a key proof for the indivisible part (atom). If the fourth proof fails, the opponent loses one support for their position.
Understand that this is part of a larger dialectical exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : فلا نسلم أن حركة السفينة ، إذا لم يتخللها السكنات . فإن الرجل الجالس فيها ، يمكنه أن يتحرك . والدليل عليه : أن السفينة إذا تحركت إلى جهة تحركات خالية عن تخلل السكنات . فإذا فرضنا أن الرجل الجالس فيها إذا تحرك إلى خلاف جهة السفينة ، بحركته الاختيارية . فمن المعلوم : أن من تحرك إلى جهة ، فإنه يحصل في تلك الجهة . فإذا تحرك إلى جهتين متضادتين : إحداهما بالقصد والاختيار ، والأخرى بتبعية حركة السفينة . فحينئذ يلزم أن يحصل الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة ، في حيزين مختلفين . وذلك محال
As for the fourth proof, we do not grant that the ship's motion, if it is free from the interpolation of rests, then the man sitting in it can move. The evidence for this is: If the ship moves to a direction by movements devoid of the interpolation of rests, and we suppose that the man sitting in it moves opposite the direction of the ship by his voluntary motion, then it is known that whoever moves to a direction, he occupies that direction. So if he moves to two opposite directions – one by intention and choice, the other by following the ship's motion – then it would necessitate that one body occupy two different places at once. That is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage gives the response to the fourth proof (ship and man). The proponents of the indivisible part deny that if the ship moves without rests, the man inside can move relative to it. Their reasoning: Suppose the ship moves continuously (without rests) in one direction, and the man voluntarily walks in the opposite direction. Then the man would be moving in two opposite directions simultaneously – the ship's motion carries him one way, and his own motion carries him the opposite way. This would require him to be in two different places at the same instant, which is impossible. Therefore, the man's motion relative to the ship is not possible under the assumption of continuous motion without rests. This refutes the premise of the fourth proof.

Key Terms
السكنات
الحركة الاختيارية
الحيزان المختلفان
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that voluntary motion can combine with forced motion without contradiction, as long as they are on the same body.
Correction: The author shows that if both motions are continuous (no rests), the body would be in two opposite places at once, which is impossible. Thus, at least one motion must involve rests to prevent co-location.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what would happen if the man moves opposite to the ship's continuous motion?
A) He would remain stationary
B) He would be in two different places at once, which is impossible
C) The ship would stop
D) His motion would be slower

Key Takeaway
Continuous motion without rests cannot coexist with voluntary motion in the opposite direction because it would force a single body to occupy two places at once, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed plausible until you followed its logical consequences to an absurd conclusion, forcing you to abandon it? What was that belief, and how did the confrontation change you?
?
The man in the ship assumed he could move freely despite the ship's continuous motion. Where in your spiritual life have you assumed you could progress in one direction (e.g., toward worldly gains) while simultaneously moving toward Allah, without the necessary 'rests' of reflection and self-accounting?
?
This argument reveals that contradiction arises from ignoring necessary pauses. In your daily walk with Allah, what 'rests' (like prayer, contemplation, or repentance) have you neglected that are actually essential for coherent motion toward Him? Will you commit to reinstating them, even if it feels like slowing down?

Light is an Accident, Not a Body
Response to the fifth and sixth proofs: light is an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the fifth and sixth proofs: that light is an accident generated in the recipient, not a body moving from the sun.

Core Idea
Light is a quality, not a traveling body.

The fifth and sixth proofs rely on the assumption that light is a physical body that separates from the sun and descends. The response denies this premise, asserting that light is actually an accident—a quality—that arises in the facing recipient. This refutes the need for instantaneous motion or leaps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a denial followed by an affirmation.
2
Identify the assumption
The assumption is that light is a body that detaches from the sun and travels down. This is what the response denies.
The word 'ممنوع' indicates rejection.
3
Grasp the alternative
The response states that light is an accident (كيفية) that occurs in the recipient when it faces the luminous source. No body moves.
4
Connect to the proof
If light is not a body, then arguments about a body moving instantly or leaping do not apply. The proof is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الخامسة والسادسة : فالجواب عنهما : أن ذلك بناء على أن النور جسم ، ينفصل من جوهر الشمس، وينزل إلى هذا العالم ، وذلك ممنوع . بل النور كيفية تحدث من المضيء في القابل المقابل .
As for the fifth and sixth proofs, the answer to them is: That is based on the notion that light is a body that separates from the substance of the sun and descends to this world, and that is denied. Rather, light is an accident that is generated from the luminous source in the opposite recipient.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage gives the response to the fifth and sixth proofs (about light). The proponents of the indivisible part reject the premise that light is a body. They assert that light is not a body but an accident (kayfiyyah) – a quality – that is produced by the luminous source (al-muḍīʾ) in the facing recipient (al-qābil al-muqābil). Therefore, the argument that a body traverses distance instantly does not apply; light is not a body moving through space, so no leap or infinite speed is needed. This undermines both proofs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light is a physical body moving from the sun to Earth.
Correction: Light is an accident generated in the recipient, not a moving body.

Key Takeaway
Light is not a body but an accident generated in the recipient, so the proofs based on body motion fail.

The Argument of a Person Rotating on His Heel
The author presents a physical argument that the body remains intact during rotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a simple physical experiment (rotation on the heel) to refute the claim that bodily parts disintegrate during motion, and how the Mu'tazila's view on death strengthens the argument.

Core Idea
Rotation does not disintegrate the body; sensory experience and the Mu'tazila's own criteria prove it.

This passage presents the third argument in a series against a claim that bodies disintegrate under certain conditions. The author uses a common experience — turning on one's heel — to show that the body remains intact. He also invokes the Mu'tazila's principle that separation of parts causes death to further prove his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure 'لزم أن يقال' and the refutation 'والحس يدل'.
Pay attention to the word 'تفككت' (disintegrated).
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The passage assumes that if a person rotates on his heel, his parts would necessarily disintegrate. Find this conditional statement in the text.
3
Understand the sensory evidence refutation
The author argues that sense perception directly contradicts the disintegration claim. The person himself knows his body remains continuous. Reflect on the strength of empirical evidence.
This is a key example of using observable reality to disprove a theoretical claim.
4
Appreciate the Mu'tazila's role
The author adds that the Mu'tazila believe separation of parts causes death. Since the person does not die, this further confirms that no disintegration occurred. Ask yourself: how does this strengthen the argument?
The Mu'tazila's own doctrine is turned against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إن الإنسان لو وضع عقبه على الأرض ، ثم أدار نفسه على عقبه ، دورة تامة. لزم أن يقال : إنه في تلك الحالة ، تفككت أجزاؤه وتفرقت وتمزقت . والحس يدل على أن ذلك باطل . فإن هذا الإنسان يعلم من نفسه : أنه بقي متصل الأجزاء ، كما كان قبل ذلك . لا سيما وعند المعتزلة : أن افتراق أجزاء البنية يوجب الموت .
And the third: If a man placed his heel on the ground, then turned himself on his heel a complete rotation, it would be necessary to say that in that state his parts had disintegrated, separated, and torn. But sense perception indicates that this is false. For this man knows from himself that he remained continuous in parts, as he was before that. Especially since according to the Mu'tazila, the separation of the parts of the structure necessitates death.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument (likely in a series) against a claim of disintegration. The argument is based on empirical observation: if a person rotates on his heel, his body would supposedly disintegrate if the claim were true. But the person himself knows that his body remains continuous and intact. The author adds that according to the Mu'tazila, the separation of bodily parts causes death, which further strengthens the argument: since the person does not die, the parts have not separated. Thus, the original claim that bodies disintegrate under rotation is false. This is a straightforward reductio ad absurdum based on sensory evidence.

المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Actually, the argument uses the absence of death to show that disintegration did not occur; the Mu'tazila view is used as a premise to strengthen the refutation.

Key Takeaway
By using a simple rotation on the heel, the author demonstrates that the body remains intact, and the Mu'tazila's own principle — that separation causes death — actually proves that no separation occurred.

The Millstone Argument for Infinite Divisibility
The author proves infinite divisibility using concentric millstone circles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the millstone argument demonstrates that space and time are infinitely divisible.

Core Idea
Concentric circles force space and time to divide endlessly.

Imagine a millstone with concentric circles. When the outer circle moves a certain distance, the inner circle must move a smaller distance—otherwise contradictions arise. This simple observation leads to a profound conclusion: space and time can be divided without end. In this lesson, we'll follow the author's geometric reasoning step by step, and see why it supports the kalām view of the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a puzzle about disintegration, then the mutakallimūn's solution, then the geometric proof.
The passage contains a lacuna (missing text) that has been filled from another manuscript; it's marked in brackets.
2
Understand the problem setup
The author sets up a problem: if millstone parts disintegrate, each part would need to 'know' how to move to preserve its position, which is impossible for inanimate stones. The mutakallimūn answer that God directly controls each part.
This shows the theological context: the argument is used to support occasionalism.
3
Analyze the geometric argument
Focus on the key logical steps: (1) When the large circle moves one part, the small circle cannot move a whole part and cannot remain still. (2) Therefore it must move less than a part. (3) That implies magnitude is divisible beyond any finite limit.
This is the core of the proof for infinite divisibility.
4
Connect to space and time
The author then extends the reasoning: the large circle divides time, the small circle divides space. So both space and time are infinitely divisible.
The argument works for celestial spheres too, and is even stronger there.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider what it means for space and time to be potentially (not actually) infinite in divisibility. The argument does not claim that we can physically separate to infinity, only that no finite division is the last.
This distinguishes the kalām view from atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن القول بتفكك أجزاء الرحى ، يقتضي أن يقال : إن كل واحدة من تلك الدوائر ، يجب أن يعلم : أنه كم ينبغي أن يسكن ؟ وكم ينبغي أن يتحرك ؟ حتى لا تتغير تلك الأجزاء عن مساماتها ومناسباتها ، التي كانت موجودة . ومعلوم : أن أعقل الناس لا يهتدي إلى هذه الحالة ، فضلا عن أجزاء الحجر ، مع أنها جمادات خالية عن الفهم والإدراك . إلا أن المتكلمين يقولون : إن إله العالم يحرك كل واحد منها في بعض الأحياز ، ويسكنها في البعض ، على وجه تبقى تلك المسامات والممناسبات كما كانت . ومثل هذا الفعل من الإله الحكيم غير مستبعد . ولما ثبت بالدليل : أنه لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه عند حركة الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً ، تتحرك الدائرة الصغيرة أيضاً جزءاً تاماً . وثبت : أنه لا يجوز أن يقال : عند حركة الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً [ لا يتحرك من الصغيرة شيء البتة . بقي أن يقال : إن عند حركة العظيمة ] تتحرك الدائرة الصغيرة ، أقل من جزء . وذلك يفيد كون المقدار قابلاً للقسمة ، إلى غير النهاية . واعلم : أن هذه الحجة تقتضي انقسام الزمان والمسافة معاً إلى غير النهاية . لأن الكبرى إذا قطعت جزءاً ، ففي مثل ذلك الزمان ، قطعت الدائرة الصغرى ، أقل من جزء . فتنقسم المسافة والدائرة الكبيرة . وإذا قطعت مثل المقدار الذي قطعتها الدائرة الصغيرة ، فإنها تقطع مثل ذلك المقدار، في أقل من ذلك الزمان . فثبت : أن الدائرة الصغرى قاسمة للمسافة ، والكبرى قاسمة للزمان . واعلم : أنه لا يختلف وجه الاستدلال . سواء فرضتم الكلام في استدارة الفلك . بل الكلام ههنا أقوى وأولى ، للوجوه التي تقدم ذكرها .
The fourth: Indeed, the statement about the disintegration of the parts of the millstone necessitates that it be said: each one of those circles must know how much it should rest and how much it should move, so that those parts do not change from their pores and proportions that were existing. And it is known that the most intelligent of people cannot attain this state, let alone parts of stone, while they are inanimate and devoid of understanding and perception. However, the mutakallimun say: Indeed, the God of the world moves each one of them in some spaces and rests them in others, in such a way that those pores and proportions remain as they were. And such an act from the wise God is not improbable. And since it was established by proof that it is not permissible to say that when the large circle moves a part, the small circle also moves a complete part, and it was established that it is not permissible to say that when the large circle moves a part [the small circle moves nothing at all. It remains to be said that when the large circle moves a part] the small circle moves less than a part. And that implies that magnitude is divisible ad infinitum. And know: this proof necessitates the division of time and space together ad infinitum. For if the large circle cuts a part, then in such a time the small circle cuts less than a part. So space and the large circle are divided. And if it cuts the same amount that the small circle cut, then it cuts that same amount in less than that time. So it is established that the small circle divides space, and the large circle divides time. And know: the manner of inference does not differ, whether you suppose the discussion regarding the rotation of the celestial spheres; rather, the discussion here is stronger and more appropriate, for the reasons previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth argument (the millstone argument) that demonstrates the infinite divisibility of space and time. The author sets up a problem: if the parts of a millstone were to disintegrate, each part would need to know how much to move and rest to maintain its position, which is impossible for inanimate stones. The mutakallimun resolve this by asserting that God directly controls each part. Then the author uses the geometry of concentric circles: when the large circle moves a certain distance, the small circle must move less than that distance, because if it moved an equal amount or nothing, contradictions would arise. Consequently, the magnitude moved is divisible beyond any finite part, implying infinite divisibility. Furthermore, the relationship between the circles shows that space is divided by the small circle and time is divided by the large circle, leading to the conclusion that both space and time are infinitely divisible. The author notes that this reasoning applies equally to the rotation of celestial spheres, and in fact is stronger there.

Key Terms
الرحى
دوائر
قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية
زمان
مسافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that space and time are actually divided into an infinite number of parts in reality.
Correction: The argument proves potential infinite divisibility: no matter how many divisions you make, you can always divide further. It does not assert an actual infinite number of divisions.

Checkpoint
According to the millstone argument, what happens to the small circle when the large circle moves one part?
A) It moves exactly one part
B) It moves less than one part
C) It does not move at all
D) It moves more than one part

Key Takeaway
The millstone argument shows that space and time are infinitely divisible because concentric circles force a never‐ending series of smaller movements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even a millstone's parts require God's direct intervention to maintain their order, how much more do you need to rely on Him for every moment of your life? Are you living as if your smallest actions are under His control, or do you act independently?
?
The proof demonstrates infinite divisibility—meaning God's knowledge and power extend to the infinitely small. Do you truly believe He cares about the tiniest details of your thoughts and actions? If so, how should that change your daily habits?
?
The mutakallimun argued that inanimate matter cannot direct itself; only God can. In your own life, where have you been acting as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every breath and movement is sustained by Him?

The Argument of the Two Concentric Circles
Geometrical proof using concentric circles to refute atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a geometrical proof using concentric circles demonstrates the continuous divisibility of space and time, refuting atomism.

Core Idea
Concentric circles show that motion and distance are always divisible.

This passage presents a clever geometrical argument against atomism, the idea that space and time are made of indivisible parts. By considering two concentric circles rotating together, the author shows that both distance and time can be divided infinitely. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Imagine two concentric circles rotating together. The larger circle has a greater circumference than the smaller one.
The circles are attached to the same center and rotate at the same angular speed.
2
Follow the reasoning on distance
When the larger circle travels a certain part of its circumference, the smaller circle travels less than that part in the same time. This implies that distance can be divided into smaller portions.
The 'part' here is a unit of distance; the smaller circle covers a fraction of it.
3
Follow the reasoning on time
When the smaller circle travels a given distance, the larger circle covers that same distance in less time. This implies that time can also be divided.
The larger circle's greater speed means it covers the same distance quicker.
4
Note the generalization
The author emphasizes that this argument applies to any rotating system, not just celestial spheres, and is even stronger in the context of the celestial sphere.
This broadens the refutation of atomism to all motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النهاية . لأن الكبرى إذا قطعت جزءاً ، ففي مثل ذلك الزمان ، قطعت الدائرة الصغرى ، أقل من جزء . فتنقسم المسافة والدائرة الكبيرة . وإذا قطعت مثل المقدار الذي قطعتها الدائرة الصغيرة ، فإنها تقطع مثل ذلك المقدار، في أقل من ذلك الزمان . فثبت : أن الدائرة الصغرى قاسمة للمسافة ، والكبرى قاسمة للزمان. واعلم : أنه لا يختلف وجه الاستدلال . سواء فرضتم الكلام في استدارة الفلك . بل الكلام ههنا أقوى وأولى ، للوجوه التي تقدم ذكرها .
The end. Because when the large (circle) cuts a part, in such a time, the small circle cuts less than a part. So distance and the large circle are divided. And if it cuts the same amount that the small circle cut, it cuts that amount in less than that time. So it is established: that the small circle is a divisor of distance, and the large circle is a divisor of time. Know: that the manner of reasoning does not differ, whether you suppose the discussion is concerning the rotation of the celestial sphere. Rather the discussion here is stronger and more appropriate, for the reasons mentioned previously.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a geometrical argument against atomism based on two concentric circles rotating together. The author begins by stating the premise: when the larger circle travels a certain part (presumably of its circumference), the smaller circle travels less than that part in the same time. This implies that distance can be divided (since the smaller circle covers a fraction of the larger's part) and also that the larger circle's motion divides time (since it covers a given distance in less time). The conclusion is that the small circle divides space, and the large circle divides time. The author then notes that this reasoning applies regardless of whether one considers the celestial sphere or any other rotating system; indeed, the argument is even stronger here for reasons already mentioned. The argument aims to show that indivisible atoms (the 'part' that cannot be further divided) are impossible because motion and distance are continuously divisible.

The Argument of the Shadow
A proof from shadow length changes to show the impossibility of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how changes in shadow length disprove the existence of indivisible parts through a logical analysis of three possibilities.

Core Idea
Shadow geometry disproves indivisible parts by showing they lead to absurdity.

This lesson presents the second argument against atomism, using a simple physical example: a stick's shadow at sunrise. By analyzing what must happen to the shadow when the sun rises by one 'part', the author shows that assuming indivisible parts leads to logical and geometric contradictions. The argument is a classic example of rational deduction in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the three possibilities
The author sets out three possible outcomes when the sun rises by one part. Read them carefully to understand the logical options.
2
Examine the rejection of the first possibility
The first option is rejected because it would imply the shadow is as long as the celestial quadrant, which is absurd. Notice the reasoning.
3
Examine the rejection of the second possibility
The second option is rejected with two sub-arguments: first, a simple reductio; second, a geometric proof about lines and angles. Follow the geometric reasoning closely.
The geometric proof involves imaginary lines from the sun to the stick's tip and the shadow tip.
4
Understand the conclusion
With the first two options false, the third option must be true: the shadow decreases by less than a part. This contradicts the idea of an indivisible part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الخشبة المغروزة في الأرض . عند طلوع الشمس يقع ظلها في جانب المغرب ، وعند ارتفاع الشمس بمقدار جزء ، إما أن ينتقص من الظل بمقدار جزء ، وإما أن لا ينتقص شيء من الظل اصلا ، وإما أن ينتقص من الظل بمقدار أقل من جزء . والأول باطل . وإلا لزم أن يكون طول الظل ، مثل مدار ربع الفلك الأعظم . والثاني أيضاً باطل لوجوه : الأول : إنه لو جاز أن ترتفع الشمس جزءاً ، مع بقاء الظل كما كان . فلم لا يجوز أن ترتفع بمقدار جزءين وثلاثة وأربعة ، مع بقاء الظل كما كان ؟ ومعلوم أنه باطل . والثاني : إن الشمس حين كانت حاصلة في نقطة معينة من ذلك المدار ، فإنه يخرج من مركز الشمس خط على الاستقامة ، ويمر برأس تلك الخشبة ، وينتهي إلى طرف ذلك الظل . فإذا انتقلت الشمس من تلك النقطة إلى نقطة أخرى . فهل يخرج خط آخر من مركز الشمس ، ويمر بالاستقامة على طرف تلك الخشبة ، وينتهي إلى طرف ذلك الظل ؟ فلو قدرنا أن طرف الظل باق بحاله في الوقتين ، لزم أن يحصل للخط المستقيم رأسان ، في الجانب الذي يتعلق بالشمس . وذلك محال . والدليل عليه : أن النقطة التي منها انشعب الرأسان ، وافترق الخطان . إذا فرضنا قيام خط عليها ، فوجب أن يكون ذلك الخط قائماً على هاتين الشعبتين، فيلزم أن تكون الزاويتان الحادثتان من الجانبين : قائمة . والقوائم كلها متساوية . فيلزم أن يكون الزائد مثلا للناقص . وهو محال . ولما بطل القسمان الأولان المذكوران في الدليل ، ثبت : أنه مهما ارتفعت الشمس بمقدار جزء، فإنه ينتقص من الظل أقل من جزء . وذلك يوجب القول بفساد الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ .
The second proof: the stick fixed in the ground. At sunrise, its shadow falls on the west side, and when the sun rises by the measure of a part, either the shadow decreases by a part, or nothing decreases from the shadow at all, or the shadow decreases by less than a part. The first is false; otherwise, the length of the shadow would be like the quadrant of the great celestial sphere. The second is also false for several reasons: First: if it were permissible for the sun to rise by a part while the shadow remains as it was, then why is it not permissible for it to rise by two, three, or four parts while the shadow remains as it was? And it is known that this is false. Second: when the sun is located at a specific point on that orbit, a straight line extends from the center of the sun, passes through the top of that stick, and ends at the tip of that shadow. So when the sun moves from that point to another point, does another line emerge from the center of the sun, pass straight through the top of that stick, and end at the tip of that shadow? If we suppose that the tip of the shadow remains the same in both times, then a straight line would have two heads on the side related to the sun; that is impossible. The proof of this: the point from which the two heads diverged and the two lines separated: if we assume a line standing upon it, that line must stand upon these two branches, so the two angles formed on both sides are right angles; all right angles are equal; thus the larger would be equal to the smaller, which is impossible. Since the first two divisions mentioned in the proof are false, it is established that whenever the sun rises by a part, the shadow decreases by less than a part. That necessitates the invalidity of the indivisible part.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against atomism, using the example of a stick's shadow at sunrise. The author considers three possibilities when the sun rises by one indivisible part (juzʾ): the shadow decreases by exactly one part, or does not decrease at all, or decreases by less than a part. The first possibility is rejected because if each unit of solar rise caused a unit decrease in shadow, the total shadow length would equal the quadrant of the celestial sphere—absurd. The second possibility is rejected with two sub-arguments: (1) if a one-part rise left the shadow unchanged, then multiple parts would also leave it unchanged, which is clearly false; (2) geometrically, if the shadow tip remained fixed, the straight line from the sun to the tip would have two endpoints at the sun's side (since the sun moves), which is impossible. A further geometric proof shows this would imply an inequality of equal angles. Thus the only remaining possibility is that the shadow decreases by less than a part, proving that the indivisible part is invalid because continuous quantities like shadow length can be divided beyond any assumed minimal part.

Key Terms
جزء
ظل
خشبة مغروزة
شمس
خط مستقيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shadow argument is based on empirical observation of actual shadows.
Correction: It is actually a logical deduction that assumes indivisible parts and derives a contradiction; it does not depend on actual measurements.

Checkpoint
In the shadow argument, why is the possibility that the shadow remains unchanged when the sun rises by a part considered false?
A) Because the shadow would then be longer than the Earth
B) Because the same reasoning would allow the sun to rise by many parts without shadow change
C) Because the stick would break
D) Because the sun's light would be blocked

Key Takeaway
The shadow argument demonstrates that assuming indivisible parts leads to contradictions in simple physical phenomena, proving that such parts cannot exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that an assumption we might take for granted—that things are made of tiny indivisible parts—leads to logical absurdity. Where in your own life have you clung to a simple, comfortable explanation that, when examined closely, does not hold up? Are you willing to let that explanation go and embrace a more complex reality, even if it feels unsettling?

If the universe is infinitely divisible, even in something as ordinary as a shadow, what does that say about the precision and depth of Allah's creation? How does this affect the way you see the world around you—do you now look at a simple shadow with more awe and recognition of divine wisdom?
?
The argument forces you to reason step by step, rejecting false possibilities until only the truth remains. Are you disciplined enough to apply that same logical rigor to your own beliefs and assumptions about your faith? Is there a belief you hold that you have never truly tested for inner contradictions? The Compass Argument Against Atomism
A proof using a three-legged compass to refute atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a three-legged compass argument demonstrates the impossibility of indivisible parts (atoms).

Core Idea
The compass argument shows that indivisible parts must be divisible.

This lesson presents one of the most ingenious proofs against atomism, using a simple geometric device: a three-legged compass. By considering what happens when the outer leg moves by one indivisible part, the argument forces a contradiction, showing that the very concept of an indivisible part leads to absurdity. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the compass setup
Visualize a compass with three legs: one fixed at the center, one touching a small circle (50 parts), and one touching a larger concentric circle (100 parts).
The numbers are only for illustration; the argument works for any ratio.
2
Identify the three possibilities
When the outer leg moves by exactly one indivisible part, what happens to the inner leg? The author lists three mutually exclusive possibilities: it moves by exactly one part, it does not move at all, or it moves by less than a part.
3
Analyze each possibility's implication
Each possibility leads to a contradiction: (1) if both move by one part, the circles would be equal; (2) if the inner leg doesn't move, the compass would break; (3) if it moves by less than a part, the part is divisible.
This is a classic argument by cases.
4
Conclude the refutation of atomism
Since all possibilities are impossible, the initial assumption that parts are indivisible must be false. Therefore, parts are divisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إذا أخذنا فرجاراً ذا شعب ثلاثة ، ووضعنا رأس الشعبة الأولى منه على مركز الدائرة ، ورأس الشعبة الثانية منه على محيط دائرة مركبة من خمسين جزءاً ، ورأس الشعبة الثالثة منه على محيط دائرة أخرى محيطة بالأولى ، مركبة من مائة جزء . ثم نقول : إما أن يقال : مهما قطع رأس الشعبة الثالثة الموضوعة على الدائرة المحيطة المركبة من مائة جزء : جزءاً . فإنه يقطع رأس الشعبة الثانية الموضوعة على الدائرة المركبة من خمسين جزءاً بتمامه . وإما أن لا يتحرك البتة . وإما أن يتحرك على أقل من جزء . والأول يقتضي أن تكون الدائرة الصغيرة مساوية للدائرة العظيمة . والثاني يقتضي لنكسار ذلك الفرجار . والثالث يقتضي انقسام الجزء .
The third proof: if we take a compass with three legs, and place the tip of the first leg at the center of the circle, the tip of the second leg on the circumference of a circle composed of fifty parts, and the tip of the third leg on the circumference of another circle surrounding the first, composed of one hundred parts. Then we say: either (it is said that) whenever the tip of the third leg placed on the surrounding circle composed of one hundred parts cuts a part, the tip of the second leg placed on the circle composed of fifty parts cuts exactly that entire part; or it does not move at all; or it moves by less than a part. The first implies that the small circle is equal to the large circle; the second implies the breaking of that compass; the third implies the division of the part.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against atomism, using a three-legged compass. The compass is arranged with one leg at the center, the second leg at a point on a circle of 50 units (presumably parts), and the third leg at a point on a concentric circle of 100 units. The argument considers three possibilities when the outer leg moves by exactly one indivisible part: (1) the inner leg also moves by exactly one part, which would imply the two circles are equal in circumference — false; (2) the inner leg does not move at all, which would break the compass since the legs are rigidly connected — absurd; (3) the inner leg moves by less than a part, which implies that the part is divisible (since the inner leg covers a fraction of it). The author thus concludes that the indivisible part must be divisible, refuting atomism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works because the numbers 50 and 100 are used.
Correction: The argument uses arbitrary numbers to illustrate; it works for any ratio of radii. The key is that the inner and outer circles are different sizes, so moving by one indivisible part on the outer cannot correspond to one on the inner without equality.

Checkpoint

B) The compass breaks
C) The part is divisible
D) The compass legs are flexible

Key Takeaway
The compass argument refutes atomism by showing that indivisible parts cannot exist because any motion would force their division.

The inner leg must move; it cannot stay still without the compass breaking. Is there an area of your spiritual life where you have remained motionless, assuming that staying still is safe? What would movement toward change cost you, and what is the cost of remaining still?

The Argument of Adjacent Atoms Begins
Setting up a proof from motion against atomism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of an argument against atomism based on the motion of an atom across adjacent atoms.

Core Idea
Motion across adjacent atoms forces a choice: when does motion occur?

This lesson introduces an interesting line of critique against the idea that matter is made of indivisible atoms. The argument focuses on what happens when an atom moves from one point to an adjacent one. It presents three logical possibilities for when the atom is actually in motion, and then begins to eliminate them. Though the text cuts off, the structure is instructive for understanding how kalam arguments work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the setup: two adjacent atoms and a moving atom above.
The passage is incomplete, but pay attention to the logical structure.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The author lists three possibilities for when the moving atom can be described as in motion. List them in your own words.
3
Note the first possibility is rejected
The text says the first is false. Think about why it might be false (the commentary will help).
The argument is cut off, but you can still see the pattern.
4
Consider the missing conclusion
The passage ends abruptly. Based on similar proofs, what do you think the author intended to show?
Your task is to recognize the argument's form, not to complete it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة من الدلائل المبنية على الحركة : إنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين متماسين ، وفرضنا فوق أحدهما جوهراً . ثم انتقل ذلك الجوهر من مكانه ، إلى الجوهر الثاني . فنقول : هذا الجوهر المتحرك إما أن يكون موصوفاً بالحركة ، حال بقائه على الجوهر الأول ، أو حال حصوله في الجوهر الثاني . أو يقال : إنه إنما يكون موصوفاً بالحركة فيما بين هاتين الحالتين . والأول باطل .
The fourth proof from those based on motion: if we suppose two adjacent atoms, and we suppose above one of them an atom. Then that atom moves from its place to the second atom. We say: this moving atom is either described by motion while it remains on the first atom, or at the time of its arrival at the second atom, or it is said that it is described by motion in between these two states. The first is false.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth proof against atomism, based on the motion of an atom across two adjacent atoms. The author sets up a scenario: two atoms (jawharayn) are in contact, and a third atom moves from its position above the first atom to the second. The question is: when is the moving atom actually in motion? Three possibilities are proposed: (1) it is in motion while still on the first atom, (2) while on the second atom, or (3) in between the two states. The author declares the first possibility false, but the text ends here. The remainder of the argument (presumably showing contradictions for the other possibilities and concluding the necessity of divisibility) is missing due to page cutoff. Despite incompleteness, the logical structure is initiated.
