The Millstone Objection Reversed: Atomists Strike Back
Presents an objection from atomists and a reply about preserved alignment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the atomist objection uses the millstone example against anti-atomists and how the answer relies on the concept of alignment (samt) to preserve connection.

Core Idea
Alignment saves connection from speed differences.

The atomists turn the tables: they argue that the very millstone disintegration argument used against atoms would also apply to those who deny atoms. This passage presents that objection and the subtle answer based on the preservation of alignment (samt). Understanding this exchange refines our grasp of how kalam thinkers argued about motion and connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from atomists
Start by reading the objection in Arabic and translation. Notice how the atomist claims that the millstone's outer and inner circles moving at different speeds must disintegrate if connected, and that this follows even without atoms.
The objection is an attempt to show that the argument backfires.
2
Study the answer about alignment
Read the answer carefully. The key is that slower motion can preserve the alignment (samt) with faster motion. The answerer denies that disintegration is necessary.
Alignment here means directional correspondence.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this exchange matters for the larger debate. It shows that sophisticated responses exist and that the millstone argument is not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا سؤال آخر : وهو أن بعض مثبتي الجوهر الفرد ، قال : « القول بتفكك حجر الرحى ، لازم أيضاً على القائلين بنفي الجوهر الفرد . وذلك لأن حجر الرحى ، قد حصلت الدائرة الكبيرة فيه ، وحصلت الدائرة الصغيرة في داخل تلك الدائرة الكبيرة . ولا شك أن المحيط متصل بالمحاط [ به ] فإذا تحرك المحيط حركة سريعة ، وتحرك المحاط به حركة بطيئة ، لزم تفكك إحداهما عن الأخرى . فثبت : أن القول بالتفكك لازم ) ولمجيب أن يجيب عن هذا السؤال : فيقول : لا نسلم أنه يلزم من كون إحدى الدائرتين أسرع من الدائرة الأخرى ، وقوع التفكك بينهما . وذلك لأن إحداهما وإن تحركت قليلا إلا أن تلك الحركة القليلة يبقى سمتها مع الشيء ، الذي تحرك كثيراً . كما كان قبل ذلك . ولما بقي السمت بسبب هذا القدر من الحركة . لم يلزم وقوع التفكك . فهذا هو الكلام على هذا الدليل
And here is another question: Some proponents of the indivisible atom said: 'The doctrine of the disintegration of the millstone is also necessary for those who deny the indivisible atom. That is because in the millstone, there is a large circle and a small circle inside that large circle. There is no doubt that the surrounding is connected to the surrounded. So if the surrounding moves quickly and the surrounded moves slowly, disintegration of one from the other necessarily occurs. So it is established that the doctrine of disintegration is necessary.' And the answerer can answer this question by saying: We do not accept that from one circle being faster than the other, disintegration necessarily occurs between them. That is because even if one moves a little, that little movement preserves its alignment with the thing that moves much, as it was before. Since the alignment remains due to this amount of movement, disintegration does not occur. So this is the discussion on this proof.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an additional argument from some proponents of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). They claim that the objection about the millstone (hajar al-raha) disintegrating actually applies equally to those who deny the atom. The reason: in a millstone, the outer circle moves faster than the inner circle, so if they are connected, the difference in speed should cause them to separate—thus disintegration is also necessary on the anti-atomist view. The author then provides the answer: the answerer says that faster motion does not necessarily cause disintegration because the alignment (samt) between the circles is preserved. Even if one moves slower, it maintains its directional correspondence with the faster moving part, so no separation occurs. This is a technical response about relative motion preserving spatial alignment.

Key Terms

حجر الرحى
الدائرة الكبيرة
الدائرة الصغيرة
المحيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The millstone argument decisively refutes atomism.
Correction: The argument is rebutted by pointing out that alignment can be preserved, so disintegration does not necessarily occur.

Checkpoint
Why does the answerer claim that disintegration does not occur in the millstone despite different speeds?
A) Because the circles are not actually connected.
B) Because the slower motion preserves alignment with the faster motion.
C) Because the millstone is indivisible.
D) Because the faster motion prevents separation.

Key Takeaway
The preservation of spatial alignment (samt) is a key concept in kalam that explains how connected parts can move at different speeds without disintegrating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see things falling apart in your life – relationships, routines, even your own resolve – do you assume it's because of different 'speeds' or misalignment? This lesson teaches that alignment can preserve connection despite differences. Are you aligned with what truly matters even when your pace slows? What one specific alignment do you need to restore today?
?
The atomists argued that different speeds necessarily cause disintegration. But the answerer said no – alignment can keep things together. Think of a relationship where you feel you're moving at a different 'speed' than someone else. Instead of expecting disintegration, what 'samt' – what common direction or purpose – could you intentionally maintain to prevent real separation? Will you commit to that this week?
?
This lesson is about a millstone, but it's really about what holds things together when everything seems to pull apart. What is the 'alignment' your soul needs to stay connected to your Creator when your life's pace changes? Can you name one specific practice that preserves that alignment, and will you do it today?

The Shadow Argument: Occasionalist Refutation
Refutes the shadow argument by invoking divine agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ashʿarī occasionalist framework resolves the argument from the shadow's motion by denying natural causation.

Core Idea
The shadow's movement is not caused by the sun but by God's direct action.

In this passage, the author addresses the third argument against atomism, which appeals to the continuous motion of a shadow. The problem only arises if one assumes that the sun's rising naturally causes the shadow to shorten. The author cuts this argument at its root by rejecting the premise of natural efficient causes, attributing the shadow's movement instead to the free agent (God). This is a classic occasionalist move that also appears earlier in the millstone argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side to get the author's wording and structure.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if we say... then problem; but if we attribute... then problem disappears.'
2
Understand the argument being refuted
The third argument claims that the shadow's continuous motion disproves atomism because it implies infinite divisibility. Identify the premise that the sun's rising is the cause of the shadow's shortening.
3
Examine the author's response
The author does not deny the shadow's movement but shifts the causal attribution from the sun to God. This shows the foundational role of occasionalism in Ashʿarī kalam.
The term 'al-fāʿil al-mukhtār' (the free agent) is key—it emphasizes God's direct creation of each event.
4
Connect to the millstone argument
The author references a prior discussion (dalīl al-rahā). Reflect on how this pattern of refutation—rejecting natural causality—is used consistently throughout the text.
This intertextual reference reinforces the systematic approach of the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهي التمسك بحال انتقال الظل فنقول : هذا الإشكال إنما يلزم ، لو قلنا : المؤثر في انتقاص الظل ، هو ارتفاع الشمس فأما إذا أسندنا ذلك إلى الفاعل المختار . فالإشكال زائل وتمام الكلام في المعارضات سبق في دليل الرحى
As for the third argument, which is adhering to the case of the shadow's movement, we say: This problem only follows if we say that the effect in the diminution of the shadow is the rising of the sun. But if we attribute that to the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār), then the problem disappears. And the full discussion of the counter-arguments preceded in the millstone argument.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage addresses the third argument against the indivisible atom, which involves the movement of a shadow. The argument likely claimed that the gradual shortening of a shadow requires continuous motion that cannot be composed of discrete parts. The author responds by saying that this problem only arises if one assumes that the sun's rising is the natural cause (muʾaththir) of the shadow's change. However, if we attribute the shadow's movement directly to God (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār, the free agent), who creates each state directly, then no difficulty remains. The author then notes that the complete treatment of such counter-arguments has already been given earlier in the discussion of the millstone argument (dalīl al-rahā). This is a standard Ashʿarī move: appealing to occasionalism to avoid physical objections.

Misconception: The author ignores the objection or fails to answer it.
Correction: The author does not ignore it; he rejects the underlying premise of natural causality, which is not accepted in the Ashʿarī occasionalist framework.

A) By denying that the shadow moves.
B) By attributing the shadow's change to God's direct action.

The shadow argument is resolved by denying natural causation and attributing all motion directly to God.

The author rejects the sun as an independent cause. What 'suns' in your own life—people, habits, circumstances—do you treat as independent causes, and how can you begin to attribute them directly to God?

The Principle of Gradual Change
Definition of gradual change as succession of distinct events

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a truly single thing cannot undergo gradual change—any gradual change implies a succession of distinct things.

Core Idea
Gradual change is a sequence of discrete events, not a smooth flow.

This lesson establishes a crucial metaphysical principle: a single unified entity cannot originate or cease to exist gradually. What we perceive as gradual change is actually the origination or disappearance of a series of distinct things one after another. This principle underpins the forthcoming argument about the shadow's divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author asserts that gradual origination and non-existence are only conceivable as successions of multiple things.
Pay attention to the key terms: تدريج (gradual) and تعاقب (succession).
2
Identify the core claim
The author claims that a single true unity (شيء واحد وحدة حقيقية) can only come into existence or cease to exist instantaneously (دفعة), not gradually.
3
Understand the implication
If gradual change occurs, it must consist of multiple distinct entities, each with its own instantaneous origin or cessation. This sets the stage for the shadow argument.
This principle refutes the idea of a smooth, continuous change of one thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا وجه آخر في السؤال زائد على ما تقدم وهو أنا نقول : إنا قد دللنا على أن حدوث الشيء على سبيل التدريج : غير معقول ، وكذا عدمه بل الحدوث على سبيل التدريج : عبارة عن حدوث أشياء ، على سبيل التعاقب . والعدم على سبيل التدريج : عبارة عن عدم أشياء على سبيل التعاقب . والشيء الواحد وحدة حقيقية لا يحدث إلا دفعة ، ولا يعدم إلا دفعة . ويستحيل أن يكون الذي عدم قبل ، هو عين ذلك الذي عدم بعد . بل ذلك عدم قبل : شيء . والذي عدم بعده : شيء آخر ، مغاير للأول إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن هذا الظل لا يعدم دفعة في الحس ، وإنما يعدم على التدريج فوجب أن يكون معنى عدمه : هو أنه يعدم منه شيء فشيء . وكل واحد مما عدم في نفسه ، فهو في نفسه شيء واحد ، وإنما عدم دفعة . فإذا كان هذا العدم مستمراً ، وقد دللنا على أن هذا العدم المستمر ، معناه : عدم أشياء على التعاقب والتلاصق . فلو كان هذا الخط الحاصل من الظل منقسما إلى غير النهاية ، وجب أن يحصل عند عدمه ، عدم أمور متتالية متلاصقة إلى غير النهاية ، وكل واحد منها يقع في آن واحد : فيلزم : تتالي آنات غير متناهية بالفعل، لا سيما لما تعاقبت تلك الآنات ، وتوالت . ويمتنع يوجد منها اثنان دفعة واحدة . بل الحاصل هو العدم بعد العدم . وكل واحد من تلك العدمات واقع في الآن ، لزم منه القول بحصول آن بعد آن . وإذا كانت تلك العدمات غير متناهية بالفعل، لزم أن تكون تلك الآنات غير متناهية بالفعل ، فيلزم أن يكون ما لا نهاية له من الأمور الموجودة بالفعل ، قد وجد ، مع كونها محصورة بين حاصرين. وذلك محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القول بأن عدم ذلك الظل ، عدم واحد متصل : قول باطل . وإنه لو كان الحق أن ذلك الظل يقبل انقسامات لا نهاية لها، توجب أن يحصل هناك عدمات متغايرة بالفعل لا نهاية لها ، وأن يحصل هناك آنات بالفعل لا نهاية لها ولما كان ذلك باطلاً فاسداً ، علمنا : أن ذلك الظل لا يقبل إلا انقسامات متناهية . وهو المطلوب وهذا هو بعينه الجواب عن الفرجار الذي يحصل له شعب ثلاثة.
And here is another aspect regarding the question, additional to what has preceded, which is that we say: We have demonstrated that the origination of a thing gradually is inconceivable, and likewise its non-existence. Rather, origination gradually is an expression for the origination of things in succession. And non-existence gradually is an expression for the non-existence of things in succession. And a single thing, having a true unity, does not originate except at once, and does not cease to exist except at once. And it is impossible that that which ceased to exist before is the very same as that which ceased to exist after. Rather, that which ceased to exist before is a thing, and that which ceased to exist after it is another thing, different from the first. If this is established, we say: This shadow does not cease to exist at once in sensation, but rather it ceases to exist gradually. So it must be that the meaning of its non-existence is that parts of it cease to exist one after another. And each part that ceases to exist is in itself a single thing, and only ceases to exist at once. So since this non-existence is continuous, and we have demonstrated that this continuous non-existence means the non-existence of things in succession and contiguity, if this line formed by the shadow were divisible infinitely, then at its non-existence there would necessarily occur a succession of contiguous things ad infinitum, each occurring in an instant, leading to a sequence of an actual infinite number of instants, especially since those instants succeed one another. And it is impossible for two of them to occur at once. Rather, what occurs is non-existence after non-existence. And each of those non-existences occurs in an instant, necessitating the occurrence of instant after instant. And if those non-existences are actually infinite, then those instants must be actually infinite, necessitating that an actual infinity of existing things has come to exist while being bounded between two boundaries. That is impossible. So it is established by what we mentioned that the statement that the non-existence of that shadow is a single continuous non-existence is false. And if the truth were that the shadow admits infinite divisions, it would necessitate that there occur actually distinct infinite non-existences and actually infinite instants. Since that is false and corrupt, we know that the shadow only admits finite divisions. That is what was sought. And this itself is the answer to the compass which has three branches.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a proof via reductio ad absurdum. The author first establishes a metaphysical principle: a single true unity cannot originate or cease gradually; gradual change implies a succession of distinct things. He then applies this to the shadow. Since the shadow is perceived to cease gradually, it must consist of discrete parts that cease one by one. If the shadow were infinitely divisible, then its gradual non-existence would entail an infinite series of non-existences, each occurring in a distinct instant. This would result in an actual infinite number of instants, which is impossible because an actual infinite cannot be bounded. Therefore, the shadow can only have finite divisions. The author concludes that this reasoning also resolves a previous objection about a compass with three branches, effectively linking this argument to a broader discussion of physical divisibility.

Key Terms
حدوث (origination)
عدم (non-existence)
تدريج (graduality)
تعاقب (succession)
دفعة (at once)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gradual change is a smooth, continuous process affecting a single unchanging substance.
Correction: According to this principle, gradual change always involves a succession of distinct things; a single entity changes only instantaneously.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does a single true unity (شيء واحد وحدة حقيقية) originate or cease to exist?
A) Gradually
B) At once (دفعة)
C) Over time in a continuous manner
D) It never originates or ceases

Key Takeaway
Gradual change is not a property of a single entity but a sequence of instantaneous events involving multiple distinct things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not a continuous flow but a series of discrete moments created by God each instant, how does that change your awareness of His constant sustaining power? Will you pause today to acknowledge that you exist only because He brings you into being right now?
?
The shadow at dawn and at noon are not the same thing—each is a new creation. Consider a failure or sin you cling to from your past: is that 'you' anymore? How does seeing your life as a succession of new selves allow you to let go of old shame and turn wholeheartedly to God?
?
If a truly singular thing cannot change gradually, then every change in your character—every step of growth—must involve God creating a new 'you' at each stage. How does this truth affect your patience with your own spiritual progress? Will you ask God to create in you the person He wants you to become, moment by moment?

The Shadow Argument for Finite Divisibility
Proof by reductio ad absurdum that the shadow's divisibility is finite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the gradual disappearance of a shadow proves that it must be composed of a finite number of parts, because infinite divisibility leads to an impossible actual infinite.

Core Idea
The shadow's gradual fading shows it has finite, discrete parts.

Now we apply the principle from the previous lesson to a concrete example: the shadow. We observe that the shadow does not vanish all at once but fades gradually. According to the principle, this gradual non-existence implies that distinct parts of the shadow cease to exist one after another. If the shadow were infinitely divisible, an infinite number of such parts would have to cease in succession, leading to an actual infinite series of instants—a logical impossibility. Therefore, the shadow must have only finite divisions.

Guided Path
1
Apply the principle to the shadow
The shadow is perceived to disappear gradually. Therefore, it must be composed of discrete parts that vanish one by one, not a single continuous entity.
The principle from Lesson 1: gradual non-existence means multiple things cease in succession.
2
Assume infinite divisibility
Suppose the shadow's line (its boundary) is divisible infinitely. Then its gradual disappearance would require an infinite number of distinct parts to cease, each in an instant.
This leads to an infinite number of instants, each separate from the next.
3
Derive the contradiction
An actual infinite number of instants cannot exist because they would be bounded between the start and end of the shadow's disappearance, yet an infinite cannot be bounded.
The argument concludes: the original assumption (infinite divisibility) is false; hence the shadow has finite divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Refutation of the Intermediate State in Motion
Refutes the claim of an intermediate state in motion via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim of an intermediate state in motion by using the analogy of instantaneous transition from non-existence to existence.

Core Idea
The intermediate state in motion is an illusion, just like in the transition from non-existence.

The opponent argues that a moving part must have an intermediate state of motion between two positions. The author refutes this by showing that the same logic would imply an intermediate state between non-existence and existence, which is absurd. Instead, the transition is instantaneous, and the intermediate state exists only in the imagination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
Read the opponent's claim that a moving part is not moving on the starting point or destination, so it must have an intermediate state of motion.
This is the claim the author will refute.
2
Follow the author's refutation by analogy
Notice how the author draws an analogy with the transition from non-existence to existence, which also seems to imply an intermediate state but is actually instantaneous.
The author argues that the same logic leads to an absurdity, proving the opponent's claim false.
3
See the application to motion
Read how the author applies the same reasoning to the part's motion: it goes from contiguity to contiguity without an intermediate state.
The trilemma that follows is incomplete in the passage, but the main refutation is clear.

وأما الحجة الرابعة : وهي قولهم : ( الجزء إذا انتقل من جزء إلى جزء آخر، فإنه لا يكون متحركاً حال بقائه على الجزء الأول . لأنه ما دام كذلك . فإنه بعد لم توجد الحركة ، ولا يكون متحركاً حال حصوله على الجزء الثاني . لأنه إذا صار كذلك ، فقد حصلت الحركة وانقطعت وانقرضت . بل إنما يكون متحركاً فيها بين الحالتين ، فنقول : هذا باطل . وذلك لأنا نعلم : أن الصورة والصفة قد يكون معدوماً ، ثم يصير موجوداً . فنقول : إنه ما دام يكون معدوماً ، فهو بعد لم ينتقل إلى الوجود ، وإذا صار موجوداً ، فقد حصل الانتقال وانقرض وانقضى ، فوجب أن يقال : إنما يكون منتقلاً من العدم إلى الوجود في الحالة المتوسطة ، بين كونه معدوماً وبين كونه موجوداً . فيلزم على هذا : إثبات حالة متوسطة بين كونه معدوماً وبين كونه موجوداً . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ثم إنه لا جواب عن هذا الخيال ، إلا أن يقال : إنه كان في الآن الأول معدوماً محضاً ، وفي الآن الثاني صار موجوداً محضاً . ولا واسطة بين كونه معدوماً وبين كونه موجوداً . وأن هذا الخيال الحاصل من كونه متنقلا من العدم إلى الوجود : عمل الوهم وتصوير الخيال ، وليس له حقيقة أصلاً البتة. وإذا ثبت أنه لا بد من المصير إلى هذا الجواب ، في هذا المقام . فهو بعينه جوابنا عما ذكروه من الشبهة . فإنا نقول : الجزء كان مماساً للجزء الأول ، ثم صار مماساً للجزء الثاني . وليس بين هاتين المماستين حالة متوسطة مغايرة لهما في الحقيقة. بل هذه الحالة المتوسطة موجودة في الوهم وفي الخيال . والذي يدل على أن الأمر كذلك : أنه لو حصل بين كون ذلك الجزء مماساً للجزء الأول ، وبين كونه مماساً للجزء الثاني : حالة الحركة . فنقول : ذلك الجزء عند حصول تلك الحالة المتوسطة . إما أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين، أو يكون حاصلاً في حيز غير معين ، أو لا يكون حاصلاً في شيء من الأحياز أصلاً. فإن كان الأول ، وهو حال كونه
As for the fourth argument: Their statement: 'If a part transfers from one part to another part, it is not moving while remaining on the first part. Because as long as it is like that, the motion has not yet occurred. Nor is it moving while being on the second part, because when it becomes like that, the motion has already occurred and ceased. Rather, it is only moving in between the two states.' We say: This is false. That is because we know that a form or attribute may be non-existent, then becomes existent. We say: As long as it is non-existent, it has not yet transferred to existence. When it becomes existent, the transfer has occurred and ended. So it must be said that it only transfers from non-existence to existence in the intermediate state between being non-existent and being existent. This necessitates affirming an intermediate state between non-existence and existence. And it is known that this is false. Then there is no answer to this imagination except to say that it was in the first instant purely non-existent, and in the second instant it became purely existent, with no intermediary between being non-existent and being existent. And that this imagination of it transferring from non-existence to existence is the work of illusion and the picturing of imagination, and has no reality at all. And if it is established that we must resort to this answer in this case, it is exactly our answer concerning what they mentioned of the objection. For we say: The part was contiguous to the first part, then it became contiguous to the second part. And there is no intermediate state between these two contiguities that is different from them in reality. Rather, this intermediate state exists in illusion and imagination. That which indicates that the matter is so: If there were between the part being contiguous to the first part and its being contiguous to the second part a state of motion, we would say: That part during that intermediate state is either located in a specific location, or located in an unspecified location, or not located in any location at all. If it is the first, which is while it is
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The opponent argues that a moving part cannot be in motion while on the starting point or while on the destination, so it must have an intermediate state of motion. The author refutes this by drawing an analogy with the transition from non-existence to existence, which also seems to imply an intermediate state but is actually instantaneous. He argues that the idea of an intermediate state is a product of illusion (wahm) and imagination (khayāl), not reality. The correct answer is that the transition occurs in two instants with no middle state. Applying this to the part's motion, the part goes from being contiguous to one part to being contiguous to another without an intermediate state. The author then begins to analyze the hypothetical intermediate state, presenting a trilemma (specific location, unspecific, no location) but the argument is cut off due to missing pages. The core refutation is complete: the intermediate state is impossible.

Key Terms
جزء (part)
حركة (motion)
مماسة (contiguity)
حالة متوسطة (intermediate state)
وهم (illusion)
خيال (imagination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A moving object must pass through an infinite number of intermediate positions.
Correction: The author argues that the transition is discrete and instantaneous, with no real intermediate state; the idea of an intermediate state is an illusion.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that a part transfers through an intermediate state of motion?
A) By proving that motion is continuous.
B) By comparing it to the transition from non-existence to existence, which is instantaneous.
C) By accepting the intermediate state but denying it exists in reality.
D) By arguing that the part does not actually move.

Key Takeaway
The intermediate state in motion is not real; it is an illusion created by the imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a habit you want to change, do you imagine a gradual 'in-between' state where you are neither fully in the old nor fully in the new? The passage shows that such an intermediate is an illusion. What would it mean for you to treat your change as an instantaneous decision, with no middle ground? Will you set a definite moment to turn away from that habit?
?
The author argues that the transition from non-existence to existence is immediate. Consider a time when you knew you had to change a belief or action but delayed it, thinking you needed time to transition. How might seeing that transition as instantaneous affect your willingness to act now? What is stopping you from making that change in this very moment?

The Structure of the Argument for Circles and Spheres
Introduction: planar surfaces, circles, and the argument's structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the overall argument structure used to prove that circles and spheres are possible and that this refutes atomism, including the two positions and two ways.

Core Idea
Proving circles and spheres dismantles atomism.

This passage sets up the logical framework for the entire discussion. It first categorizes planar surfaces, then focuses on circles and spheres, asserting that their proof entails the falsehood of indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard). The author then outlines two main positions (maqam) and, under the first, two alternative ways (tariq) for proceeding.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of surfaces
The passage begins by stating that planar surfaces are either circles or polygons, starting with triangles and squares. Note the order.
This sets the stage for focusing on circles.
2
Identify the two positions (maqam)
The author states that two positions must be established: first, that circles and spheres are true; second, that if true, atomism is false.
3
Understand the two ways (tariq) within the first position
The philosophers have two ways: either prove the circle first and derive the sphere, or prove the sphere first and derive the circle.
The author chooses the first way for detailed exposition.
4
Note the introduction of two aspects (wajh)
Within the first way, there are two aspects (wajh) for proving the circle. This lesson prepares us to examine those proofs next.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أما المقام الأول . وهو إثبات الكرة والدائرة. فللفلاسفة فيه طريقان : تارة يقيمون الدلالة على إثبات القول بالدائرة . وإذا ثبت القول بالدائرة ، لزم منه إثبات القول بالكرة . وتارة يعكسون هذا الطريق ، فيقيمون الدلالة على إمكان وجود الكرة . وإذا ثبت القول بإمكان الكرة ، لزم منه القول بإثبات الدائرة .
أما الطريق الأول : فتقريره : أنا نقيم الدلالة على إثبات الدائرة . ثم إذا بينا بالدليل ثبوتها فحينئذ فرعنا على إثباتها ، القول بإثبات الكرة . فنقول :
الذي يدل على إمكان وجود الدائرة : وجهان :
الأول : إنا نتخيل بسيطاً مستوياً ، ونتخيل خطاً مستقيماً متناهياً في ذلك البسيط ، ونتخيل إحدى نهايتي ذلك الخط ثانية ، ونتخيل جميع ذلك الخط متحركاً في ذلك البسيط ، حول تلك النهاية الثابتة ، إلى أن يعود إلى الموضع الذي منه بدأ بالحركة . فحينئذ يحدث من هذه الحركة دائرة. لأن رأس الخط إذا تحرك على الاستدارة ، إلى أن عاد إلى موضعه الأول . فلا شك أن طرفه المتحرك ، قد تحرك على مسافة ما . فتلك المسافة طول ما . والنقطة لا مساحة لها . فتلك المسافة التي تحركت عليها النقطة ليس لها عرض . فهي إذن خط محيط بذلك السطح. والنهاية الثابتة الساكنة من هذا الخط المستقيم ، هي حاصلة في وسط هذا السطح المستدير . وكل الخطوط المستقيمة الخارجة من ذلك الوسط إلى ذلك المحيط : متساوية . لأن كل خط يخرج من ذلك الوسط ، إلى ذلك المحيط . فإنه ينطبق عليه الخط الفاعل للدائرة بحركته . والأشياء المتساوية لشيء واحد : متساوية . فثبت : أن هذا الشكل دائرة .
الوجه الثاني في إثبات الدائرة : ما ذكره الشيخ " أبو علي بن سينا ، في الشفاء ) و ( النجاة ، فقال : ( نفرض جسماً ثقيلاً رأسه أعظم قدراً من أصله . وتفرض كونه قائماً على بسيط مسطح قياماً مستويـاً . ولا شك أن هذا الذي فرضناه : أمر ممكن الوجود . لأنه لما كان ثقل الجانب الأعلى ، متساوياً من كل الجوانب . فحينئذ لا يكون بأن يميل إلى بعض الجوانب ، أولى بأن يميل إلى الباقي . لأن الثقل الموجب للنزول حاصل في جميع الجوانب على السوية . فإن حصل الميل إلى جانب واحد مع هذا الغرض، لزم الترجيح من غير مرجع . مع أنه محال . وإذا امتنع هذا القسم ، لم يبق بعده إلا أنه يبقى واقفا في الهواء . ثم لنفرض في هذا الجسم أنه زائل عن الاستقامة ، حتى سقط . فههنا لا يخلو . إما أن يقال : إنه حال نزوله . بقيت النقطة التي في أسفل ذلك الجسم : مماساً للموضع الذي كان مماساً له حال وقوفه ، أو يقال : إنه بقيت في هذه الحالة تلك المماسة . فإن كان الأول لزم أن يقال : إن كل نقطة مفروضة في رأس ذلك الجسم ، فقد فعلت ربع دائرة. وذلك يفيد المطلوب. وإن كان الثاني ، فنقول : تلك النقطة. إما أن يقال : إنها انجرت على ذلك السطح ،
Know: that planar surfaces are either circles or polygons. The first of the polygons is the triangle, then the square. And thus it proceeds according to the order of numbers. So let us begin with mentioning the circle and sphere: and we say: They mentioned that the evidence indicates the possibility of existence of the circle and sphere. And whenever the statement about them is valid, the statement about the invalidity of the individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard) is necessary. So we need in the exposition of this evidence to explain two positions (maqam): the first: that the statement about the correctness of the circle and sphere is true. And the second: that whenever the statement about the circle and sphere is true, the statement about the individual substance is false.
As for the first position (al-maqam al-awwal), it is the proof of the sphere and circle. The philosophers have two ways (tariqan) regarding it: sometimes they establish the proof for the statement about the circle. And if the statement about the circle is established, then from it follows the establishment of the statement about the sphere. And sometimes they reverse this way, so they establish the proof for the possibility of existence of the sphere. And if the statement about the possibility of the sphere is established, then from it follows the statement about the establishment of the circle.
As for the first way (al-tariq al-awwal): its exposition: we establish the proof for the circle. Then when we show by evidence its existence, then at that point we branch from its establishment to the statement about the establishment of the sphere. So we say:
That which indicates the possibility of existence of the circle: two aspects (wajhan):
The first: we imagine a simple plane, and we imagine a straight line finite in that plane, and we imagine one of the two endpoints of that line fixed, and we imagine the entire line moving in that plane, around that fixed endpoint, until it returns to the position from which it began moving. Then from this motion a circle is generated. Because when the head of the line moves circularly until it returns to its first position, there is no doubt that its moving endpoint has moved along some distance. That distance is a certain length. And the point has no area. So that distance upon which the point moved has no width. It is therefore a line surrounding that surface. And the fixed stationary endpoint of this straight line is located at the center of this circular surface. And all straight lines emerging from that center to that circumference are equal. Because every line emerging from that center to that circumference coincides with the line that generated the circle by its motion. And things equal to one thing are equal. So it is established: that this shape is a circle.
The second aspect (al-wajh al-thani) in proving the circle: what the Shaykh Abu Ali ibn Sina mentioned in (al-Shifa) and (al-Najat): he said: we suppose a heavy body whose head is greater in extent than its base. And we suppose it standing on a flat plane with an upright stance. And there is no doubt that what we have supposed is a possible existence. Because when the heaviness of the upper side is equal from all sides, then it is not such that it inclines to some sides more than it inclines to the rest. Because the heaviness that causes descent is present in all sides equally. For if inclination to one side occurs with this condition, it would necessitate preference without a preferrer. And that is impossible. And when this category is rendered impossible, nothing remains except that it remains suspended in the air. Then let us suppose in this body that it deviates from uprightness until it falls. Then here it is not free from either: it is said that during its descent, the point at the bottom of that body remained tangent to the position that it was tangent to at the time of its standing, or it is said that it remained in this state of tangency. If the first, then it would necessitate that it be said: every point supposed on the head of that body has traced a quarter circle. And that yields the required. And if the second, then we say: that point, either it is said that it dragged along that surface,
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of circles and spheres, structured as a refutation of atomism (al-jawhar al-fard). The author begins by stating that planar surfaces are either circles or polygons, and then focuses on circles and spheres. He asserts that evidence shows the possibility of a circle and sphere, and if these are possible, then atomism is false. The proof is divided into two main positions (maqam): first, proving the truth of circles and spheres; second, showing that if they are true, atomism is false. The text details the first position.
The first position is approached via two ways (tariq). The first way establishes the circle first, then derives the sphere. Within this first way, two sub-arguments (wajh) are given for the circle.
The first sub-argument is a geometric construction: we imagine a plane and a finite straight line. We fix one endpoint and rotate the line around it until it returns to its starting point. This motion generates a circle because the moving endpoint traces a path of length but no width—forming a circumference—and the fixed endpoint becomes the center. All lines from the center to the circumference are equal because they coincide with the generating line during rotation.
The second sub-argument is attributed to Ibn Sina. It involves a heavy body with a head larger than its base, standing upright on a plane. Since the heaviness is equal on all sides, the body cannot tip over without a preferrer (murajjih), so it remains suspended. Then if we suppose it deviates and falls, two possibilities arise regarding the bottom point: either it remains tangent to the original contact point, or it drags along the surface. The first case implies that each point on the head traces a quarter circle, proving a circular motion. The second case is introduced but not fully presented due to the cut-off. The overall aim is to show that circular motion and thus circles are possible.
The passage uses logical deduction and physical reasoning to establish the possibility of circles, which then leads to the impossibility of indivisible atoms, as circles require a continuum.

جوهر فرد
مسطح

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The discussion of circles and spheres is purely geometric and has no theological relevance.
Correction: The entire argument is aimed at refuting atomism (al-jawhar al-fard), a key Kalam position on the nature of matter. Proving the possibility of continuous shapes like circles undermines the idea that matter is composed of indivisible atoms.

Checkpoint

A) One

Key Takeaway
Proving the possibility of circles and spheres is a two-step process: first prove the shapes, then derive the falsehood of atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Two Proofs for the Possibility of the Circle
Two aspects: geometric construction and Ibn Sina's physical argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments for the possibility of a circle: a geometric construction and a physical thought experiment by Ibn Sina, the latter being incomplete in our source.

Core Idea
Rotating a line around a fixed point generates a circle.

Having laid out the overall structure, the author now presents two specific proofs for the circle's possibility. The first is a clean geometric construction that anyone can visualize. The second, attributed to Ibn Sina, uses a heavy body in equilibrium to argue for circular motion; however, this proof is cut off mid-sentence in our manuscript.

Guided Path
1
Read the first aspect: geometric construction
Read the steps carefully: imagine a plane, a straight line, fix one endpoint, rotate the line around it until it returns. This creates a circle.
Pay attention to why the moving point traces a line with length but no width.
2
Confirm the equality of radii
The passage argues that all lines from the center to the circumference are equal because they coincide with the generating line during rotation.
3
Read the second aspect: Ibn Sina's thought experiment
Read the setup: a heavy body with a large head standing upright on a plane, with symmetrical weight. The text then considers what happens when it deviates and falls.
This argument is incomplete in our source.
4
Analyze the two descent cases
The text presents two possibilities for the bottom point: it either remains tangent or drags along the surface. The first leads to a quarter circle traced. The second is not fully explained.
The cut-off occurs while discussing the second case.

الذي يدل على إمكان وجود الدائرة : وجهان :
الأول : إنا نتخيل بسيطاً مستوياً ، ونتخيل خطاً مستقيماً متناهياً في ذلك البسيط ، ونتخيل إحدى نهايتي ذلك الخط ثانية ، ونتخيل جميع ذلك الخط متحركاً في ذلك البسيط ، حول تلك النهاية الثابتة ، إلى أن يعود إلى الموضع الذي منه بدأ بالحركة . فحينئذ يحدث من هذه الحركة دائرة. لأن رأس الخط إذا تحرك على الاستدارة ، إلى أن عاد إلى موضعه الأول . فلا شك أن طرفه المتحرك ، قد تحرك على مسافة ما . فتلك المسافة طول ما . والنقطة لا مساحة لها . فتلك المسافة التي تحركت عليها النقطة ليس لها عرض . فهي إذن خط محيط بذلك السطح. والنهاية الثابتة الساكنة من هذا الخط المستقيم ، هي حاصلة في وسط هذا السطح المستدير . وكل الخطوط المستقيمة الخارجة من ذلك الوسط إلى ذلك المحيط : متساوية . لأن كل خط يخرج من ذلك الوسط ، إلى ذلك المحيط . فإنه ينطبق عليه الخط الفاعل للدائرة بحركته . والأشياء المتساوية لشيء واحد : متساوية . فثبت : أن هذا الشكل دائرة .
الوجه الثاني في إثبات الدائرة : ما ذكره الشيخ " أبو علي بن سينا ، في الشفاء ) و ( النجاة ، فقال : ( نفرض جسماً ثقيلاً رأسه أعظم قدراً من أصله . وتفرض كونه قائماً على بسيط مسطح قياماً مستويـاً . ولا شك أن هذا الذي فرضناه : أمر ممكن الوجود . لأنه لما كان ثقل الجانب الأعلى ، متساوياً من كل الجوانب . فحينئذ لا يكون بأن يميل إلى بعض الجوانب ، أولى بأن يميل إلى الباقي . لأن الثقل الموجب للنزول حاصل في جميع الجوانب على السوية . فإن حصل الميل إلى جانب واحد مع هذا الغرض، لزم الترجيح من غير مرجع . مع أنه محال . وإذا امتنع هذا القسم ، لم يبق بعده إلا أنه يبقى واقفا في الهواء . ثم لنفرض في هذا الجسم أنه زائل عن الاستقامة ، حتى سقط . فههنا لا يخلو . إما أن يقال : إنه حال نزوله . بقيت النقطة التي في أسفل ذلك الجسم : مماساً للموضع الذي كان مماساً له حال وقوفه ، أو يقال : إنه بقيت في هذه الحالة تلك المماسة . فإن كان الأول لزم أن يقال : إن كل نقطة مفروضة في رأس ذلك الجسم ، فقد فعلت ربع دائرة. وذلك يفيد المطلوب. وإن كان الثاني ، فنقول : تلك النقطة. إما أن يقال : إنها انجرت على ذلك السطح ،
Know: that planar surfaces are either circles or polygons. The first of the polygons is the triangle, then the square. And thus it proceeds according to the order of numbers. So let us begin with mentioning the circle and sphere: and we say: They mentioned that the evidence indicates the possibility of existence of the circle and sphere. And whenever the statement about them is valid, the statement about the invalidity of the individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard) is necessary. So we need in the exposition of this evidence to explain two positions (maqam): the first: that the statement about the correctness of the circle and sphere is true. And the second: that whenever the statement about the circle and sphere is true, the statement about the individual substance is false.
As for the first position (al-maqam al-awwal), it is the proof of the sphere and circle. The philosophers have two ways (tariqan) regarding it: sometimes they establish the proof for the statement about the circle. And if the statement about the circle is established, then from it follows the establishment of the statement about the sphere. And sometimes they reverse this way, so they establish the proof for the possibility of existence of the sphere. And if the statement about the possibility of the sphere is established, then from it follows the statement about the establishment of the circle.
As for the first way (al-tariq al-awwal): its exposition: we establish the proof for the circle. Then when we show by evidence its existence, then at that point we branch from its establishment to the statement about the establishment of the sphere. So we say:
That which indicates the possibility of existence of the circle: two aspects (wajhan):
The first: we imagine a simple plane, and we imagine a straight line finite in that plane, and we imagine one of the two endpoints of that line fixed, and we imagine the entire line moving in that plane, around that fixed endpoint, until it returns to the position from which it began moving. Then from this motion a circle is generated. Because when the head of the line moves circularly until it returns to its first position, there is no doubt that its moving endpoint has moved along some distance. That distance is a certain length. And the point has no area. So that distance upon which the point moved has no width. It is therefore a line surrounding that surface. And the fixed stationary endpoint of this straight line is located at the center of this circular surface. And all straight lines emerging from that center to that circumference are equal. Because every line emerging from that center to that circumference coincides with the line that generated the circle by its motion. And things equal to one thing are equal. So it is established: that this shape is a circle.
The second aspect (al-wajh al-thani) in proving the circle: what the Shaykh Abu Ali ibn Sina mentioned in (al-Shifa) and (al-Najat): he said: we suppose a heavy body whose head is greater in extent than its base. And we suppose it standing on a flat plane with an upright stance. And there is no doubt that what we have supposed is a possible existence. Because when the heaviness of the upper side is equal from all sides, then it is not such that it inclines to some sides more than it inclines to the rest. Because the heaviness that causes descent is present in all sides equally. For if inclination to one side occurs with this condition, it would necessitate preference without a preferrer. And that is impossible. And when this category is rendered impossible, nothing remains except that it remains suspended in the air. Then let us suppose in this body that it deviates from uprightness until it falls. Then here it is not free from either: it is said that during its descent, the point at the bottom of that body remained tangent to the position that it was tangent to at the time of its standing, or it is said that it remained in this state of tangency. If the first, then it would necessitate that it be said: every point supposed on the head of that body has traced a quarter circle. And that yields the required. And if the second, then we say: that point, either it is said that it dragged along that surface,
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of circles and spheres, structured as a refutation of atomism (al-jawhar al-fard). The author begins by stating that planar surfaces are either circles or polygons, and then focuses on circles and spheres. He asserts that evidence shows the possibility of a circle and sphere, and if these are possible, then atomism is false. The proof is divided into two main positions (maqam): first, proving the truth of circles and spheres; second, showing that if they are true, atomism is false. The text details the first position.
The first position is approached via two ways (tariq). The first way establishes the circle first, then derives the sphere. Within this first way, two sub-arguments (wajh) are given for the circle.
The first sub-argument is a geometric construction: we imagine a plane and a finite straight line. We fix one endpoint and rotate the line around it until it returns to its starting point. This motion generates a circle because the moving endpoint traces a path of length but no width—forming a circumference—and the fixed endpoint becomes the center. All lines from the center to the circumference are equal because they coincide with the generating line during rotation.
The second sub-argument is attributed to Ibn Sina. It involves a heavy body with a head larger than its base, standing upright on a plane. Since the heaviness is equal on all sides, the body cannot tip over without a preferrer (murajjih), so it remains suspended. Then if we suppose it deviates and falls, two possibilities arise regarding the bottom point: either it remains tangent to the original contact point, or it drags along the surface. The first case implies that each point on the head traces a quarter circle, proving a circular motion. The second case is introduced but not fully presented due to the cut-off. The overall aim is to show that circular motion and thus circles are possible.
The passage uses logical deduction and physical reasoning to establish the possibility of circles, which then leads to the impossibility of indivisible atoms, as circles require a continuum.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only way to prove a circle is through geometry; physics cannot be used.
Correction: Ibn Sina provides a physical thought experiment to demonstrate circular motion, showing that the concept of a circle can be derived from physical principles as well. The geometric proof is not the only one.

Checkpoint
In the geometric proof of the circle, why are all lines from the center to the circumference equal?
A) Because they are all radii
B) Because each coincides with the generating line during rotation
C) Because the moving point ensures it
D) Because the circle is perfect

Key Takeaway
The geometric rotation of a line around a fixed point directly yields a circle, with all radii equal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How do you know that something is possible? When you face a difficulty, do you rely only on logical reasoning, or do you also consider physical realities? Which proof (geometric or physical) resonates more with your way of understanding?
?
The first proof builds a circle from a simple line. Think of a 'line' in your life—a simple daily action or habit. If you 'rotate' that action around a fixed intention (e.g., sincerity), what kind of 'circle' could it trace over time? Are you moving in a closed loop or expanding?
?
Ibn Sina uses the impossibility of preference without a reason to prove equilibrium. Where in your life do you assume things happen 'without reason'—unfair events, personal failures—and how does this principle challenge you to seek hidden causes or trust in divine wisdom?

Ibn Sina's Physical Proof of the Circle
Ibn Sina's proof via a heavy body thought experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's physical thought experiment that demonstrates the possibility of circular motion.

Core Idea
A falling heavy body reveals the circle through a natural–forced motion pair.

Ibn Sina's proof for the circle is not abstract geometry but a physical reasoning using a heavy body falling from equilibrium. By carefully analyzing the possible motions of the body's parts, he shows that the only coherent account involves arcs of circles. This lesson walks through his ingenious thought experiment step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup and the dilemma
Read the passage to grasp the initial conditions: a heavy body with a larger top standing upright on a flat surface. Then the body is tilted until it falls. The key dilemma is whether the bottom point remains tangent to the same spot.
This is the starting point of the proof.
2
Follow the first branch of the dilemma
If the bottom point stays tangent, then the top point traces a quarter circle. This directly gives the desired result of circular motion.
This branch is straightforward.
3
Analyze the second branch
If the bottom point does not stay tangent, we must consider two subcases: either it drags along the surface or it is lifted. Ibn Sina rejects dragging because a heavy body's natural motion is not dragging. The only remaining possibility is that the upper half descends naturally, forcing the lower half upward. This yields two halves rotating about a center, each tracing an arc of a circle.
This branch leads to the same conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في إثبات الدائرة : ما ذكره الشيخ « أبو علي بن سينا ، في الشفاء ) و ( النجاة ، فقال : ( نفرض جسماً ثقيلاً رأسه أعظم قدراً من أصله . وتفرض كونه قائماً على بسيط مسطح قياماً مستويـاً . ولا شك أن هذا الذي فرضناه : أمر ممكن الوجود . لأنه لما كان ثقل الجانب الأعلى ، متساوياً من كل الجوانب . فحينئذ لا يكون بأن يميل إلى بعض الجوانب ، أولى بأن يميل إلى الباقي . لأن الثقل الموجب للنزول حاصل في جميع الجوانب على السوية . فإن حصل الميل إلى جانب واحد مع هذا الغرض، لزم الترجيح من غير مرجع . مع أنه محال . وإذا امتنع هذا القسم ، لم يبق بعده إلا أنه يبقى واقفا في الهواء . ثم لنفرض في هذا الجسم أنه زائل عن الاستقامة ، حتى سقط . فههنا لا يخلو . إما أن يقال : إنه حال نزوله . بقيت النقطة التي في أسفل ذلك الجسم : مماساً للموضع الذي كان مماساً له حال وقوفه ، أو يقال : إنه بقيت في هذه الحالة تلك المماسة . فإن كان الأول لزم أن يقال : إن كل نقطة مفروضة في رأس ذلك الجسم ، فقد فعلت ربع دائرة. وذلك يفيد المطلوب. وإن كان الثاني ، فنقول : تلك النقطة. إما أن يقال : إنها انجرت على ذلك السطح ، أو يقال : إن حال نزول الطرف الأعلى إلى الجانب الأسفل ، فإن الطرف الأسفل : تحرك إلى الجانب الأعلى . والقسم الأول باطل لأن تلك الحركة لو حصلت . لكانت إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية . والأول باطل لأن هذا الجسم ثقيل . والجسم الثقيل لا تكون حركته الطبيعية : الانجرار على السطح . والثاني أيضاً باطل . لأن هذا القاسر ليس إلا نزول الطرف الأعلى منه إلى الأسفل . وهذا المعنى لا يوجب انجرار الطرف الأسفل على السطح . فإن هذا القسم إنما حصل من حيث أن النصف الأعتلى من هذا الجسم متصل بالنصف الأسفل منه ، اتصالاً قوياً مانعاً من الانفصال . وهذا يقتضي أن يقال : إن النصف الأعلى ، إذا أخذ في النزول ، فإنه يأخذ النصف الأسفل في الصعود . وعلى التقدير ، فإن هذا الجسم ينقسم في الوهم إلى قسمين . فالنصف الأعلى ينزل من الأعلى إلى الأسفل طبعاً ، والنصف الأسفل إلى الأعل قسراً . وبين القسمين حد معين - هو مركز للحركتين - وكل واحد من النصفين قد فعل بحركته قوساً من الدائرة . وذلك يقتضي أن يكون القول بالدائرة أمر ممكن الوجود. فثبت بهذين الموجهين : أن القول بالدائرة حق حيح.
The second method for proving the circle: what the shaykh Abū ʿAlī ibn Sīnā mentioned in al-Shifāʾ and al-Najāh, saying: Let us assume a heavy body whose top is greater in magnitude than its base. And assume it standing upright on a flat plane. There is no doubt that what we have assumed is possible of existence. Because since the weight of the upper side is equal from all sides, then it is not more likely to incline to some sides than to others. For the weight causing descent is present in all sides equally. So if inclination to one side occurred together with this equal distribution, it would require preference without a preponderating factor, which is impossible. And when this case is impossible, nothing remains except that it remains stationary in the air. Then let us assume in this body that it deviates from uprightness until it falls. Then here it is inevitable: either it is said that during its descent the point at the bottom of that body remained tangent to the place it was tangent to when it was stationary, or it is said that this tangency remained in this state. If the first, then it follows that every assumed point on the top of that body has traced a quarter circle. And that yields the desired. And if the second, then we say about that point: either it is said that it dragged along that surface, or it is said that during the descent of the upper end to the lower side, the lower end moved to the upper side. The first division is false, because if that motion occurred, it would have to be either natural, or forced, or voluntary. The first is false because this body is heavy, and a heavy body's natural motion is not dragging on a surface. The second is also false, because this forcing agent is nothing but the descent of its upper end downward, and this meaning does not necessitate dragging the lower end on the surface. For this division occurs only because the upper half of this body is connected to its lower half with a strong connection preventing separation. This requires that it be said: when the upper half begins descending, it brings the lower half upward. On this account, this body is divided in estimation into two halves. The upper half descends from top to bottom naturally, and the lower half ascends to the top forcibly. And between the two halves there is a fixed boundary—it is the center of the two motions—and each of the two halves has traced by its motion an arc of a circle. And that necessitates that the statement about the circle is possible of existence. So by these two methods it is established that the statement about the circle is true and sound.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Sina's proof for the possibility of the circle, presented as a physical thought experiment. The author begins by assuming a heavy body with a top larger than its base, standing upright on a flat plane. Due to equal weight distribution, it remains stationary. Then the body is tilted until it falls. A dilemma is posed: either the bottom point remains tangent to the same spot (case 1), or it does not (case 2). If case 1, then the top point traces a quarter circle, proving the circle. If case 2, then the bottom point must either drag along the surface or be lifted. Dragging is impossible because a heavy body's natural motion is falling, not dragging, and no external force causes dragging; the only force is the top's descent. Instead, the top's descent forces the bottom upward due to rigid connection. The body is thus divided into two halves: the upper half descends naturally, the lower half ascends forcibly. Between them is a fixed boundary (the center), and each half traces an arc of a circle. Therefore, the circle is possible. This proof, together with the first, establishes the circle's possibility.

بسيط مسطح
ثقل
حركة طبيعية
حركة قسرية
مركز
قوس دائرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Sina's proof requires an actual physical experiment.
Correction: The proof is a mental estimation (وهم) of possible motions, not a real experiment.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's proof, what prevents the bottom point from dragging on the surface?
A) The body is too heavy
B) Natural motion of a heavy body is not dragging
C) The surface is too rough
D) The body is not solid

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's thought experiment shows that circular motion is logically necessary to account for the fall of a heavy body when the bottom point does not drag.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina proves circular motion through a mental estimation, not a physical experiment. In your own life, where do you assume that only material evidence is trustworthy? Are there truths about yourself—your intentions, your soul—that you refuse to accept because you cannot see them? What would it cost you to trust the unseen realities that are more certain than what your eyes show you?
?
The proof divides the body into two halves with a fixed center. What is the central truth of your life that holds you together when two opposing forces—duty and desire, fear and hope—pull you in opposite directions? Do you know that center, or are you being dragged by the world without realizing it?
?
In the proof, the upper half descends naturally, while the lower half is forced upward. In your spiritual journey, what in your life moves toward Allah by nature, and what moves only by force? Are you content with forced obedience, or will you seek to transform your forced movements into natural ones?

Proving the Circle and Sphere via Motion
Proves the possibility of the circle and sphere via motion and semicircle rotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of the circle and sphere is established through an analysis of motion and the rotation of a semicircle.

Core Idea
The possibility of circular motion proves the possibility of the circle, and rotating a semicircle generates the sphere.

This lesson presents the first way from the Kalam tradition to demonstrate that the circle and sphere are possible existents. The argument uses a physical thought experiment involving a heavy body and the classification of motion into natural, forced, and voluntary. By analyzing the motion of its parts, the necessity of circular arcs emerges, leading to the possibility of the circle and then the sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: scenario, classification, rejection, actual explanation, conclusion for circle, then sphere.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three types of motion
The passage lists three types of motion: natural, forced, and voluntary. See why natural and forced are rejected for the lower end.
Natural motion is rejected because the body is heavy; forced motion is rejected because the forcer is only the upper end descending.
3
Understand the actual cause
The strong connection between the upper and lower halves forces the lower half to ascend when the upper half descends. This splits the body in imagination into two parts with a fixed center.
This is the key insight that leads to circular motion.
4
Trace the generation of the circle
Each half traces an arc of a circle around the fixed center. This shows that circular motion is possible, hence the circle itself is possible.
The argument uses possibility, not actual existence.
5
Move from circle to sphere
By taking a semicircle and rotating it around its diameter (the line between poles), a sphere is generated. Since the rotation is possible, the sphere is possible.
This concludes the first way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يقال : إن حال نزول الطرف الأعلى إلى الجانب الأسفل ، فإن الطرف الأسفل تحرك إلى الجانب الأعلى . والقسم الأول باطل لأن تلك الحركة لو حصلت لكانت إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية . والأول باطل لأن هذا الجسم ثقيل . والجسم الثقيل لا تكون حركته الطبيعية الانجرار على السطح . والثاني أيضاً باطل . لأن هذا القاسر ليس إلا نزول الطرف الأعلى منه إلى الأسفل . وهذا المعنى لا يوجب انجرار الطرف الأسفل على السطح . فإن هذا القسم إنما حصل من حيث أن النصف الأعلى من هذا الجسم متصل بالنصف الأسفل منه ، اتصالاً قوياً مانعاً من الانفصال . وهذا يقتضي أن يقال : إن النصف الأعلى ، إذا أخذ في النزول ، فإنه يأخذ النصف الأسفل في الصعود . وعلى التقدير ، فإن هذا الجسم ينقسم في الوهم إلى قسمين . فالنصف الأعلى ينزل من الأعلى إلى الأسفل طبعاً ، والنصف الأسفل إلى الأعلى قسراً . وبين القسمين حد معين - هو مركز للحركتين - وكل واحد من النصفين قد فعل بحركته قوساً من الدائرة . وذلك يقتضي أن يكون القول بالدائرة أمر ممكن الوجود. فثبت بهذين الموجهين : أن القول بالدائرة حق صحيح . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : وجب أن يكون القول بالكرة حقاً . وذلك لأنا إذا أخذنا نصف دائرة ، وأثبتنا خط القطر بين نقطتين هما القطبان - وأدرنا القوس حتى تعود إلى موضعها الأول ، فحينئذ تحصل منه الكرة . ولما كان جميع ما فرضناه أمراً ممكناً ، وثبت أن اللازم على الممكن ممكن ، ثبت : أن القول بالكرة أمر ممكن . فهذا هو الطريق الأول .
Or it is said: The case of the descent of the upper end to the lower side, then the lower end moves to the upper side. And the first division is false because if that motion occurred, it would be either natural or forced or voluntary. The first is false because this body is heavy. And the heavy body's natural motion is not dragging on the surface. The second is also false because this forcer is nothing but the descent of its upper end downward. And this meaning does not necessitate the dragging of the lower end on the surface. For this division only occurred because the upper half of this body is connected to the lower half of it with a strong connection preventing separation. And this necessitates that it be said: the upper half, when it begins to descend, it takes the lower half in ascent. And on this estimate, this body divides in the imagination into two parts: the upper half descends from above to below naturally, and the lower half ascends to above forcibly. And between the two parts is a fixed boundary – it is the center of the two motions – and each of the two halves has performed with its motion an arc of the circle. And that necessitates that the statement about the circle be a possible existent. So it is established by these two points: that the statement about the circle is true and correct. And when this is established, we say: it is necessary that the statement about the sphere be true. That is because if we take a semicircle and establish the line of the diameter between two points which are the poles – and rotate the arc until it returns to its first position, then from it the sphere is obtained. And since everything we hypothesized is a possible matter, and it is established that the necessary consequence of the possible is possible, it is established: that the statement about the sphere is a possible matter. So this is the first way.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the first way to prove the possibility of the circle and sphere. It begins by analyzing a physical scenario: a heavy body with an upper end descending and a lower end moving upward. The author classifies motion into natural, forced, and voluntary. Natural motion is impossible because the body is heavy and heavy bodies do not drag on surfaces naturally. Forced motion is also impossible because the only forcer is the descent of the upper end, which does not necessitate dragging the lower end. The actual cause is the strong connection between the two halves, so when the upper half descends, it pulls the lower half upward. This divides the body in imagination into two parts: the upper descending naturally and the lower ascending forcibly. Between them is a fixed boundary acting as a center, and each half traces an arc of a circle. This establishes that a circular motion is possible and thus the circle is possible. Then, by rotating a semicircle around its diameter (the line between two poles), the sphere is generated. Since the rotation is possible, the sphere is also possible. Thus the first way concludes that both the circle and sphere are possible existents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument demonstrates that heavy bodies actually move in circular arcs.
Correction: The argument only shows conceptual possibility through imaginary division, not actual physical motion of heavy bodies.

Checkpoint

A) Natural and voluntary
B) Natural and forced
C) Forced and voluntary
D) Linear and circular

Key Takeaway
By analyzing motion and rotation, the possibility of the circle and sphere is logically derived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof starts from a simple observation—an upper end descending—and builds a logical case for the possibility of a circle. When you look at the circular motions in the universe (the sun, the moon, or even a spinning wheel), do you ever pause to realize that their very possibility is rationally established before you witness them? How does this affect your trust in reason as a means to uncover deeper truths about creation?
?
The author uses imagination (wahm) to divide the body and trace arcs, concluding that the circle is possible. Are there areas in your own faith where you rely on reasoned imagination—things you cannot see but know must be true? What is one such belief, and how does this method of proof strengthen your certainty in it?
?
From the possibility of a sphere derived from a rotating semicircle, everything we see in the physical world is built from possibilities. If the circle and sphere are possible, then the entire cosmos is contingent upon the One who gives existence. Does contemplating that the very shapes around you are only possible—not necessary—drive you to reflect on the Necessary Being who caused them to exist? What response does that demand from you today?

Second Way: Sphere and Circle from Simplicity
Proves the sphere and circle via simplicity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphysical argument that the simple nature of first bodies necessitates a spherical shape, and how the existence of the sphere implies the existence of the circle.

Core Idea
Simplicity demands uniformity; only the sphere is uniform in all parts.

This lesson explores the second way to prove the existence of the sphere and circle. The argument begins with the nature of body, distinguishing between simple and composite. Through reasoning about what shape a simple body must have, we arrive at the sphere, and from there, the circle follows by geometric necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument's structure
Read the passage and notice it has two main parts: first proving the sphere exists, then showing the circle follows from the sphere.
The argument uses a case analysis (simple vs composite body).
2
Analyze the simple body case
If a body is simple, its nature requires a simple shape. That shape must be uniform in all its parts (similar parts). Compare polygons and the sphere: polygons have distinct parts (angles, lines, surfaces), while the sphere has all parts alike.
Uniformity here means every point on the surface is equidistant from the center.
3
Handle the composite body case
If the body is composite, it is made of simples, so simples exist. Then the same reasoning applies to those simples, leading back to the sphere.
This ensures the argument covers all bodies.
4
Derive the circle
Once the sphere is established, a straight cut through it produces a circle. Thus the circle necessarily exists.
This links the sphere to the circle.
5
Reflect on the metaphysical principle
Consider why this reasoning is not about physical spheres but about the necessary form of simple substances. Avoid the misconception that all bodies are actually spheres.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الطريق الثاني : فهو أنا نبين : أن الكرة ممكنة الوجود في نفسها ، ثم نبين : أن الكرة لما كانت ممكنة الوجود ، كانت الدائرة أيضاً ممكنة الوجود . وتقريره : أن نقول : الدليل على وجود الكرة : أنه لا شك في وجود الجسم والجسم إما بسيط ، وإما مركب . فإن كان بسيطاً ، فلا بد له من شكل بسيط تقتضيه طبيعته البسيطة . ومقتضى البسيط شيء واحد ، متشابه الأجزاء ، وما سوى الكرة من الأشكال لا يكون متشابه الأجزاء . فإن المضلعات يكون جانب منها زاوية ، وجانب آخر خطاً ، وجانب آخر سطحاً وأما الكرة فإنها شكل متشابه الأجزاء ، فوجب أن يكون شكل البسيط هو الكرة . وأما إن كان الجسم مركباً ، فهذا المركب إنما تركب عن البسائط فالبسيط موجود . وحينئذ يعود الكلام الأول . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الكرة موجودة. وإذا ثبت القول بوجود الكرة ، لزم الاعتراف بوجود الدائرة . لأن الكرة إذا قطعت قطعاً مستقيماً ، فإنه لا بد وأن تحصل الدائرة من موضع القطع . فهذا تمام الكلام في إثبات الكرة والدائرة .
And as for the second way: it is that we show: that the sphere is possible in existence in itself, then we show: that since the sphere is possible in existence, the circle is also possible in existence. Its elaboration: we say: the evidence for the existence of the sphere: there is no doubt about the existence of body, and body is either simple or composite. If it is simple, then it must have a simple shape required by its simple nature. And the requirement of the simple is one thing, similar in parts, and other than the sphere among shapes are not similar in parts. For polygons have one side as an angle, another side as a line, and another side as a surface. But the sphere is a shape similar in parts, so it is necessary that the shape of the simple body is the sphere. And if the body is composite, this composite is only composed of simples, so the simple exists. Then the first discourse returns. So it is established by what we mentioned: that the sphere exists. And when the statement about the existence of the sphere is established, acknowledgment of the existence of the circle follows. Because if the sphere is cut straight, it is inevitable that the circle is obtained from the place of the cut. So this is the complete discussion in establishing the sphere and the circle.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the second way to establish the possibility and actual existence of the sphere and circle. The argument begins by noting that bodies are either simple or composite. If a body is simple, its nature requires a simple shape. The simple shape must be uniform in all parts, i.e., similar in every part. Polygons (like triangles, squares) have distinct parts (angles, lines, surfaces) so they are not uniform. Only the sphere has all parts alike (e.g., every point on the surface is equidistant from the center). Therefore, the simple body must be spherical. If a body is composite, it is composed of simples, so simples exist, and the same reasoning applies to those simples. Hence, the sphere exists. Once the sphere is established, the circle necessarily follows because a straight cut through a sphere produces a circular cross-section. Thus both sphere and circle are proven to exist.

Correction: The argument is not about physical bodies but about the metaphysical nature of simple substances. It claims that if a body is simple, its form must be spherical, but not all bodies are simple.

Key Takeaway

?

First Proof: Line of Atoms Cannot Form a Circle
Summarizes the argument that a line of atoms cannot form a circle, proving atomism false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of circles and spheres disproves the theory of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard).

Core Idea
If atoms were indivisible, circles would be impossible — but circles exist, so atomism is false.

This lesson presents the first rational argument against the theory of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows that atomism leads to an impossibility (circles cannot be formed), but since circles clearly exist in reality, the theory itself must be false. Follow the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice that the author states the conclusion first: 'Thus the indivisible atom is divisible.'
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify each step: why a line of indivisible parts cannot form a circle, what follows from that, and how the actual existence of circles leads to a contradiction.
The argument is tightly condensed; each sentence carries a logical move.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand that because circles exist, atomism cannot be true. The atom must be divisible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون الجوهر الفرد منقسماً . فهذا كله لبيان أن الخط المركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزا لا يمكن جعله دائرة. وإذا ثبت هذا ، وجب أن يمتنع تحصيل الدائرة مطلقاً . لأن على القول بالجوهر الفرد ، لا معنى للجسم ، إلا خطوط مضمومة بعضها إلى بعض . فلما كان واحد منها مانعا من تكوين الدائرة ، لزم أن تكون الدائرة ممتنعة عقلا فظهر بما قلنا : إن الدائرة والكرة : ممكنة الوجود . وثبت : أنه يلزم من وجودهما ، بطلان القول بالجوهر الفرد . فيلزم : أن يكون القول بالجوهر الفرد : باطلاً .
Thus the indivisible atom is divisible. All this is to show that a line composed of parts that are indivisible cannot be made into a circle. And if this is established, then it must be impossible to produce the circle absolutely. Because on the theory of the indivisible atom, body means nothing but lines joined to each other. Since one of them prevents the formation of the circle, it follows that the circle would be rationally impossible. So it appears from what we said that the circle and sphere are possible in existence. And it is established that their existence entails the falsity of the theory of the indivisible atom. Therefore, the theory of the indivisible atom must be false.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). The author states the conclusion first: the atom is divisible. He explains that a line composed of indivisible parts cannot form a circle. Since circles and spheres exist in reality, the theory that they are impossible is false. Therefore, the indivisible atom cannot exist; it must be divisible. The logic is a reductio ad absurdum: atomism implies circles are impossible, but circles are actual, so atomism is false.

Correction: The argument combines a rational geometric deduction (a line of points cannot form a circle) with empirical fact to produce a contradiction.
