The Diagonal Connection Argument Against Atomism
Second argument against indivisible atom using circle geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational argument against the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) based on the geometric necessity of diagonal connections in circles.

Core Idea
A circle forces atoms to be divided — geometry refutes atomism.

After showing how straight lines can be formed from indivisible atoms, the author now tackles curved figures. Circles, he argues, require a diagonal connection that forces the atom to have divisible parts. This passage is a clear geometric proof against the atomic theory of his opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the full English translation. Get a feel for the logical flow before diving into analysis.
The author uses a highly compressed style; the translation will help unpack it.
2
Understand how straight lines are formed
Notice that attaching a part to the right/left of an atom yields a horizontal line; attaching to top/bottom yields a vertical line. This establishes that straight lines are possible without dividing the atom.
This sets up the contrast with circles.
3
See why a circle needs a diagonal connection
A circle cannot be made from purely horizontal or vertical lines. It requires a part to be attached both to the right and below simultaneously — a diagonal connection. This forces the atom to be divisible.
Focus on the phrase 'between the right side and the bottom side.'
4
Follow the infinite gradation argument
The argument shows that circles vary infinitely in size (narrowness and width). Each size requires a different degree of inclination (myl). Since these degrees are infinite, the atom must be divisible into infinite parts.
This is the key step that forces infinite division.
5
Conclude: atom must be divisible
The conclusion is that the existence of circles (which are real) forces the indivisible atom to have infinite capacity for division, which is a contradiction. Therefore, the atom cannot be indivisible.
This refutes the theory of al-jawhar al-fard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان أنه يلزم من القول بالكرة والدائرة نفي الجوهر الفرد : هو أنا إذا وضعنا جوهراً فرداً. فإن الصقنا بيمينه أو بيساره : جزءاً . حصل منه خط ممتد من اليسار إلى اليمين. وإن الصقنا بأسفله أو بأعلاه جزءاً ، حصل منه خط ممتد من الأعلى إلى الأسفل . ولا تحصل الدائرة والكرة البتة . فثبت : أن الدائرة لا تحصل إلا إذا اتصل أحد الجزءين بالآخر، فيها بين الجانب [ الأيمن (۳) ] والجانب الأسفل . وكلما كانت الدائرة أوسع ، كان الميل إلى الجانب الأيمن أكثر ، وكلما كانت الدائرة أضيق ، كان إلى الجانب الأسفل أكثر . ولما كان لا نهاية لمراتب الدائرة في الضيق والسعة ، فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب ذلك الميل . وذلك يوجب انقسام ذلك الجزء إلى أقسام لا نهاية لها
The second aspect in demonstrating that the theory of the sphere and circle requires the negation of the indivisible atom is: If we posit an indivisible atom, and we attach to its right or left a part, then a line extends from left to right. If we attach to its bottom or top a part, then a line extends from top to bottom. But the circle and sphere are not produced at all. So it is established that the circle is only produced when one of the two parts connects to the other in the area between the right side and the bottom side. And the wider the circle, the more inclination to the right side; the narrower the circle, the more inclination to the bottom side. And since the degrees of the circle in narrowness and width are infinite, likewise the degrees of that inclination are infinite. And that necessitates the division of that part into infinite divisions.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is the second argument (wajh) against the indivisible atom. The author describes how horizontal or vertical lines can be formed by attaching parts to a central atom. However, a circle requires a diagonal connection—a part attached simultaneously to the right and below. To achieve the varying inclinations required for circles of different sizes, the atom must be divisible into infinite parts. Thus, the existence of circles forces division.

Key Terms

الجوهر الفرد
الميل
انقسام

Misconception: Some might think the argument assumes atoms are dimensionless points; it actually assumes they are extended but indivisible.
Correction: The argument works even if atoms have size; it is the necessity of infinite division that is problematic.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The existence of circles proves that atoms must be divisible, because circles require diagonal connections with infinite gradations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Are you willing to test your most cherished assumptions, or do you prefer the comfort of an 'indivisible' certitude that might actually be false?

The Sphere Argument: Why It Necessitates Division
Demonstrates that forming a sphere requires placing atoms on joins, necessitating division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the atomic theory of spheres leads to the necessity of division.

Core Idea
To form a sphere, atoms must straddle joins, forcing division.

The third argument in this series uses geometry to show that the atomists' account of a sphere inevitably requires atoms to be split. By examining what happens when circles are stacked, the author demonstrates that a sphere cannot form without division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow sink in before interpreting.
Note that the author uses a conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Understand the cylinder vs sphere distinction
Notice that stacking equal circles produces a cylinder. The author contrasts this with the sphere to highlight the different configuration needed.
This sets up the problem: equal circles are not enough for a sphere.
3
Grasp the placement condition and its implication
The key point: for a sphere, the second circle must be smaller and its atoms must sit on the joins of two atoms of the first circle. This placement forces division because a single atom cannot occupy two places without splitting.
Division here means the atom is conceptually divided, not physically broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في بيان أنه يلزم من القول بالدائرة والكرة ، حصول القسمة : وهو أنا إذا فرضنا دائرة فوضعنا [ فوق (4) ] كل جزء منها جزءاً آخر فحينئذ تحصل دائرة ثانية مساوية للأولى في السعة. ويصير ذلك سبيا لحدوث الإسطوانة ، لا لحدوث الكرة . فثبت : أن الكرة لا تحصل إلا إذا وضعنا على متصل كل جوهرين (٥) من الدائرة ، جوهراً من الدائرة الثانية . حتى تكون الدائرة الثانية أصغر مداراً من الدائرة الأولى ، حتى تصير سبباً لحدوث الكرة . ومتى وقع الجزء الواحد على متصل الجزءين ، لزم القول بالقسمة
The third aspect in demonstrating that the theory of the circle and sphere requires division is: If we suppose a circle and place above each part of it another part, then a second circle equal in width to the first is produced. And that becomes a cause for the occurrence of a cylinder, not for the occurrence of a sphere. So it is established that the sphere is only produced if we place on the juncture of every two atoms of the circle an atom from the second circle, so that the second circle has a smaller circumference than the first, until it becomes a cause for the occurrence of the sphere. And when one part falls on the juncture of two parts, division is necessarily implied.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument. The author explains that simply stacking equal circles on top of each other creates a cylinder, not a sphere. To form a sphere, the second circle must be smaller, placed on the joint between two atoms of the first circle. Since a single atom cannot simultaneously sit on two atoms without being split, division is required.

Key Terms

الكرة

Misconception: The argument is about physically splitting atoms into smaller pieces.
Correction: It is a rational deduction about the conceptual necessity of division given the geometry of atomic placement.

Checkpoint

A) A sphere.
B) A cylinder.
C) A cone.
D) A cube.

Key Takeaway
The sphere argument shows that atomic geometry unavoidably implies division.

Fourth Face: Every Atom is Sphere or Polygon, Both Divisible
Argues that each atom, being extended, is either a sphere or a polygon, both divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against atomism: that every atom, being extended and finite, must be either a sphere or a polygon, and in either case necessary divisible.

Core Idea
Every finite extended object has a shape, and both spheres and polygons imply divisibility.

This passage presents the fourth 'face' in a series of arguments refuting atomism. The author classifies atoms based on geometric necessity: since atoms are extended and finite, they must be either spherical or polygonal. Both possibilities lead to the conclusion of divisibility, thereby challenging the foundational claim of atomism that atoms are indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the clear classification into two shape categories.
2
Understand the shape classification
The argument begins by stating that every extended thing is finite, and every finite thing is bounded by either one boundary (sphere) or multiple boundaries (polygon). Thus each atom is either a sphere or a polygon.
This classification is exhaustive for finite volumes.
3
Analyze the sphere case
If the atom is a sphere, joining spheres creates gaps smaller than the atoms themselves, proving that the atom is divisible since something smaller exists.
This uses the principle that if something smaller exists, the original is divisible.
4
Analyze the polygon case
If the atom is a polygon, its corners are narrower and smaller than its sides, again indicating divisibility.
The polygon argument is even stronger because the corner is visibly a smaller part.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since both possible shapes lead to divisibility, all atoms must be divisible. This refutes the atomist claim of indivisible particles.
The argument is thus a geometric proof against atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع من الوجوه المبنية على إثبات الكرة والدائرة : أن نقول : كل واحد من هذه الأجزاء (۱) : متحيز وحجم . وكل متحيز فهو متناه . وكل متناه فإنه يحيط به حد واحد [ أو حدود (۲) ] والذي يحيط به حد واحـد هـو الكرة . والذي يحيط به حدود هو المضلعات . فكل واحد من هذه الأجزاء إما كرة وإما مضلع . فإن كان كرة لزم القول بكونه منقسماً . لأن الكرات إذا ضم بعضها إلى بعض ، حصلت الفرج فيما بينها ، ويكون كل واحد من تلك الفرج ، أصغر من تلك الأجزاء. وكلما وجد شيء أصغر منه ، كان منقسماً . فوجب كون تلك الأجزاء منقسمة . وأما إن كانت مضلعة ، كان القول بقبولها للقسمة أقوى . لأن جانب الزاوية يكون لا محالة أضيق وأقل حجماً ، من جانب الضلع . وما كان كذلك ، كان منقسماً . فثبت أن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء إما أن يكون كرة وإما أن يكون مضلعاً . وثبت أن على كلا التقديرين : وجب كونه قابلاً للقسمة . فيلزم القطع بكون تلك الأجزاء قابلة للقسمة . وهو المطلوب
The fourth aspect among the aspects built on affirming the sphere and circle: We say: Each one of these parts is extended and has volume. And every extended thing is finite. And every finite thing is surrounded by either one boundary or boundaries. That which is surrounded by one boundary is the sphere. That which is surrounded by boundaries is polygons. So each one of these parts is either a sphere or a polygon. If it is a sphere, it necessarily follows that it is divisible. Because if spheres are joined together, gaps occur between them, and each one of those gaps is smaller than those parts. And whenever something smaller than it exists, it is divisible. So it is necessary that those parts are divisible. And if they are polygons, the claim of their being receptive to division is stronger. Because the side of the angle is inevitably narrower and smaller in volume than the side of the side. And what is like that is divisible. So it is established that each one of those parts is either a sphere or a polygon. And it is established that on both assumptions, it must be receptive to division. Hence it necessarily follows with certainty that those parts are receptive to division. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. The author classifies each atom as either a sphere or a polygon because it is finite and bounded. If it is a sphere, joining spheres leaves gaps smaller than the spheres, so the spheres must be divisible. If it is a polygon, the corners are narrower and smaller, indicating divisibility. In both cases, the atom is divisible. Thus, atomism is refuted.

Misconception: Atoms have no shape and cannot be geometrically classified.
Correction: The argument shows that any extended finite object must have a boundary, thus a shape. Atomists often deny shape, but the author forces a geometric analysis on the atom.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument conclusively shows that atoms, as extended finite bodies, must be geometrically classifiable, and both sphere and polygon cases inevitably imply divisibility.

Fifth Face: Infinite Divisibility from Circle and Line
Geometric proof of infinite angular divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relationship between a straight line and a circular arc is used to prove that angles are infinitely divisible.

Core Idea
A wider circle gives a wider angle; infinite circles mean infinite angle variations.

This lesson presents the fifth kalam argument against atomism, using geometric intuition. The author shows that as circles vary in size, the angle formed by a straight line and an arc varies continuously. Since circles can be infinitely many, the angle must be infinitely divisible, and this applies even to right angles. The passage is incomplete but the core reasoning is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the complete part.
2
Identify the geometric setup
Notice the construction: a straight line and an arc from its endpoint form an angle. The arc's circle can vary in size.
3
Trace the reasoning about infinity
The author argues that since circles can have infinite degrees of width, the resulting angles also have infinite degrees, meaning the angle can be divided infinitely.
This is a potential infinite, not an actual infinite.
4
Understand the a fortiori conclusion
Because this infinite divisibility occurs in an angle less than a right angle, the right angle itself must be infinitely divisible 'with greater reason'.
This conclusion is key against atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Also, whenever the angle formed from the diameter of the circle [and the] arc is wider, the angle formed from the sharpness...

The intended conclusion likely reinforces infinite divisibility and the impossibility of indivisible atoms.

Turning the Tables: The Falling Body Argument and Unknown Causes
The author responds to an objection by conditionalizing the premise and appealing to unknown causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of forced motion with an unknown cause by appealing to consistency in accepting inferences based on proof.

Core Idea
The author uses the opponents' own logic to show that unknown causes are acceptable when proof demands it.

The opponents argue that if a falling body's lower end drags, the dragging must be either natural or forced, implying they know the cause. The author cleverly turns this around: why can’t the cause be unknown? He reminds them that they themselves accept unknown causes—like stillness causing a mountain to stand—when proof necessitates it. This passage demonstrates a powerful method of argument: using the opponent's accepted principles to defend one's own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the conditional acceptance at the start.
The author first says 'this is accepted if...' which sets up the condition.
2
Identify the objection and the counter
Identify the opponents' claim about the circular arc and the author's response that the lower end might drag. Then see how the author handles the natural/forced dichotomy.
The key move is the question 'Why is it not permissible that it is forced, except that the forcing cause is unknown?'
3
Analyze the analogy
The author draws an analogy with the opponents' own acceptance of stillness causing a mountain to stand despite unknown cause. Understand how this analogy forces consistency.
This is the heart of the argument: if they accept unknown causes in one case, they must accept it here.
4
Reflect on the argumentative strategy
Consider how the author uses the opponents' own premises against them. This is a common theological debating technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية : وهي قولهم : الجسم الواقف في الهواء إذا نزل ، فإنه يرتسم من حركة الطرف الفوقاني منه قوس دائرة ، فنقول : هذا مسلم لو ثبت أن في هذه الحالة بقي الطرف الأسفل منه في موضعه الأول ، ولم ينجر على السطح. أما قوله : لو انجر على السطح ، فذلك الانجرار إما أن يكون طبيعياً أو قسرياً ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون قسرياً إلا أن السبب القاسر الحصول هذه الحالة، غير معلوم على التفصيل ؟ ألستم قد ذكرتم : أن بين الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة ، لا بد من حصول السكون . وأن ذلك السكون يوجب وقوف الجبل في الهواء ؟ وقلتم : إن الدليل لما أوجب القول به ، وجب التزامه، وإن كنا لا نعرف سببه بعينه. فكذا ههنا . لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الجسم الواقف في الهواء إذا سقط . ففي حال سقوطه يجب أن ينجر طرفه الأسفل على السطح، وإن كنا لا نعرف الموجب لذلك الانجرار على التفصيل ؟
As for the second argument: it is their statement: 'The body stationary in the air, when it descends, a circular arc is traced from the movement of its upper end.' We say: This is accepted, if it is proven that in this state the lower end of it remained in its original position and did not drag on the surface. As for his statement: 'If it dragged on the surface, then that dragging is either natural or forced.' We say: Why is it not permissible that it is forced, except that the forcing cause for this occurrence is unknown in detail? Did you not mention that between upward and downward motion, there must occur stillness, and that stillness necessitates the mountain standing in the air? And you said: When the proof necessitated asserting it, it must be accepted, even if we do not know its specific cause. So here as well. Why is it not permissible to say: The body stationary in the air, when it falls, then in the state of its falling its lower end must drag on the surface, even if we do not know the necessitator for that dragging in detail?
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The opponents argue that when a body falls in air, its upper end traces a circular arc, implying some geometric property. The author accepts this conclusion conditionally: only if it is proven that the lower end remains fixed and does not drag on the surface. The opponents further claim that if the lower end dragged, the dragging must be either natural or forced. The author counters by asking why it cannot be forced, even if the specific forcing cause is unknown. The author then draws an analogy: the opponents themselves admit that between upward and downward motion there must be stillness, and that stillness causes a mountain to stand in air, even though they do not know the exact cause. Since they accept that principle based on proof, they should also accept that a falling body's lower end drags on the surface, even if the specific cause of dragging is unknown. The author thus turns the opponents' own reasoning against them, arguing for consistency in accepting unknown causes when proof demands it.

طبيعي

سبب قاسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the falling body argument proves motion geometry without considering the possibility of dragging.
Correction: The author shows that the argument assumes no dragging; if dragging occurs, the geometry changes, and the cause of dragging can be unknown but still accepted based on proof.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that the dragging of the lower end must be either natural or forced?
A) He agrees and concedes the argument.
B) He says it could be forced even if the cause is unknown, and cites their own acceptance of unknown causes elsewhere.
C) He denies the possibility of forced motion.
D) He says the dragging must be natural.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the possibility of forced motion with an unknown cause by citing the opponents' own acceptance of unknown causes when proof necessitates them.

Refuting the Circular Arc Argument for Falling Bodies
Refutes the claim that a descending body traces a circular arc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that a descending body must trace a circular arc by appealing to the opponent's own acceptance of unknown causes.

Core Idea
If the opponent accepts unknown causes for rest, they must accept unknown causes for forced dragging.

The second argument against the author's position claims that a body falling from rest traces a circular arc because its upper edge moves while the lower edge stays. The author cleverly turns the argument on its head: if the opponent insists the dragging of the lower edge must be natural or forced, they must also accept that forced motion can occur without a known specific cause — just as they themselves argued for the mountain's suspension due to rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opponent's claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The opponent claims that a body descending from rest traces a circular arc from the motion of its upper extremity. Notice the author's initial conditional concession.
2
Examine the author's key question
The author asks: why can't the dragging of the lower extremity be forced, even if the specific cause is unknown? This shifts the burden back to the opponent.
The opponent had categorized dragging as either natural or forced.
3
See how the author uses the opponent's own reasoning
The author reminds the opponent that they themselves accepted the mountain's suspension due to rest without knowing its specific cause. So consistency demands they accept forced dragging here with unknown cause.
This is a classic refutation by appealing to the opponent's own principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية : وهي قولهم : ( الجسم الواقف في الهواء إذا نزل . فإنه يرتسم من حركة الطرف الفوقاني منه قوس دائرة ، فنقول : هذا مسلم . لو ثبت أن في هذه الحالة بقي الطرف الأسفل منه في موضعه الأول ، ولم ينجر على السطح. أما قوله : « لو انجر على السطح ، فذلك الانجرار إما أن يكون طبيعياً أو قسرياً ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز ان يكون قسرياً . إلا أن السبب القاسر الحصول هذه الحالة، غير معلوم على التفصيل ؟ الستم قد ذكرتم : أن بين الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة ، لا بد من حصول السكون . وأن ذلك السكون يوجب وقوف الجبل في الهواء ؟ وقلتم : إن الدليل لما أوجب القول به ، وجب التزامه، وإن كنا لا نعرف سببه بعينه. فكذا ههنا . لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الجسم الواقف في الهواء إذا سقط . ففي حال سقوطه يجب أن ينجر طرفه الأسفل على السطح، وإن كنا لا نعرف الموجب لذلك الانجرار على التفصيل ؟ ) .
And as for the second argument: it is their saying: 'The body standing in the air when it descends, a circular arc is traced from the motion of its upper extremity.' So we say: This is conceded, if it is established that in this state its lower extremity remained in its first position and did not drag on the surface. As for his saying: 'If it drags on the surface, then that dragging is either natural or forced,' we say: Why is it not permissible that it be forced, except the forcing cause for this state is not known in detail? Have you not mentioned that between upward and downward motion, rest must occur, and that rest causes the mountain to stand in the air? And you said: Since the evidence necessitated affirming it, it is necessary to commit to it, even if we do not know its cause in particular. So likewise here. Why is it not permissible to say: The body standing in the air when it falls, in the state of its fall its lower extremity must drag on the surface, even if we do not know the cause of that dragging in detail?'
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage refutes the second argument, which claims that a body suspended in air, when it descends, traces a circular arc from the motion of its upper end. The author begins by conceding the point if it is established that the lower end remains fixed at its first position and does not drag on the surface. But then he examines the opponent's claim that if it drags, the dragging must be either natural or forced. The author asks why it cannot be forced, even if the specific cause of that forcing in this situation is unknown in detail. He appeals to the opponent's own method: they had argued that between upward and downward motion, there must be a state of rest, and that this rest causes the mountain to stand in the air, even if its specific cause is not known. So here too, the author argues, we can say that a body descending in air must have its lower tip drag on the surface, even if we do not know the specific cause. Thus the argument is turned back on the opponents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forced motion must always have a known specific cause.
Correction: The author shows that forced motion can be accepted even if its precise cause is unknown, as the opponents themselves accept unknown causes for the mountain's suspension.

Checkpoint

B) He agrees it is forced but the cause is known.

Four Counterexamples to the Spherical Shape Claim
Refutes necessity of spherical shape with four counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four counterexamples that refute the claim that a simple body must have a spherical shape, demonstrating that simplicity of nature does not necessitate uniformity of effect.

Core Idea
Simplicity does not force spherical shape; non-uniform effects are possible.

The third argument in favor of the spherical shape of celestial bodies is that the shape of a simple body must be the sphere. The author refutes this by listing four counterexamples from astronomy and logic. These examples show that simple natures can produce non-uniform effects, undermining the necessity of the spherical shape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author lists four distinct counterexamples.
2
Identify the four counterexamples
Find each of the four points: the homocentric/eccentric sphere, the planet location, the convex/concave surfaces, and the part-whole argument.
Each is introduced with 'أولها', 'وثانيها', etc.
3
Understand how each shows non-uniformity
For each counterexample, ask: how does a simple nature produce different effects in different directions or parts?
The first two involve thickness and location; the last two involve shape and size.
4
Reflect on the logical conclusion
Conclude that the claim that simple bodies must be spherical is not universally true, as non-uniform effects are observed.
This refutation relies on actual astronomical observations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : ( شكل البسيط يجب أن يكون هو الكرة ، فنقول : هذه الحجة منقوضة بأشياء أولها : إن الفلك الممثل ، إذا انفصل عنه فلك خارج المركز ، فإنه ينفصل من الفلك الممثل : متممان . أحدهما : من خارج الفلك الخارج المركز . والثاني : في داخله . ومعلوم أن طبيعة كل واحد من هذين المتممين : طبيعة بسيطة مع أنه في أحد الجانبين في غاية الثخن ، ومن الجانب الثاني في غاية الرقة . فههنا البسيطة اقتضت أثراً غير متشابه في كل الجوانب وثانيها : إن الفلك جرم بسيط . ثم إن الكوكب وقع في بعض جوانبه دون البعض . فالطبيعة بسيطة ، والأثر غير متشابه . أن تلك الطبيعة . وثالثها : إن الفلك طبيعة بسيطة مع اقتضت حصول الشكل المقيب في الخارج، وحصول الشكل المقعر في الداخل . وهما أثران مختلفان . وأيضاً : السطح الخارج يكون أعظم مقداراً من السطح الداخل . فالبسيطة واحدة ، والآثار مختلفة ورابعها : إن الأجسام إما أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة . أما البسائط فإن جزءها يكون مساوياً لكلها ، في تمام الماهية. فلو كانت تلك الطبيعة موجبة للشكل الكروي ، لزم أن يكون شكل كل واحد من الأجزاء المفترضة فيه ، هو الكرة . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لزم وقوع الخرق في جميع الأجسام المحسوسة .
And as for the third argument: it is their saying: 'The shape of the simple must be the sphere.' So we say: This argument is refuted by things. The first: That the homocentric sphere, when an eccentric sphere separates from it, two complements separate from the homocentric sphere: one from outside the eccentric sphere, and the second inside it. And it is known that the nature of each of these two complements is a simple nature, while on one side it is extremely thick, and on the second side extremely thin. So here the simple nature produced a non-uniform effect in all sides. The second: That the celestial sphere is a simple body. Then the planet occurs in some of its sides without others. So the nature is simple, and the effect is non-uniform. That nature... The third: That the celestial sphere is a simple nature while it necessitates the production of the convex shape in the exterior, and the production of the concave shape in the interior. And they are two different effects. Also: the exterior surface is greater in measure than the interior surface. So the simple nature is one, and the effects are different. The fourth: That bodies are either simple or composite. As for simples, their part is equal to the whole in complete essence. So if that nature necessitated the spherical shape, it would necessitate that the shape of each of the parts assumed in it be the sphere. And if the matter were so, the occurrence of gaps in all sensible bodies would be necessary.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage refutes the third argument, which claims that the shape of a simple body must be the sphere. The author lists four counterexamples. First: When an eccentric sphere separates from the homocentric sphere, two complements are formed, one outside and one inside the eccentric sphere. Each complement is simple in nature, but one side is extremely thick and the other extremely thin, so the simple nature produced non-uniform effects in different directions. Second: The celestial sphere is a simple body, but a planet is located in some parts and not others, so the simple nature yields non-uniform effects. Third: The sphere has both a convex outer surface and a concave inner surface, which are two different effects, and the outer surface is larger than the inner, all from one simple nature. Fourth: All bodies are either simple or composite. For simple bodies, each part is equal to the whole in complete essence. So if the nature required the spherical shape, every part of it would have to be spherical, and that would require gaps in all sensible bodies. Thus the claim that simple bodies must be spherical is refuted.

Key Terms
بسيط
فلك
كرة
متمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simplifying nature necessarily produces uniform effects (like uniform spherical shape).
Correction: Simple natures can produce non-uniform effects, as demonstrated by the four counterexamples.

Checkpoint
Which counterexample uses the idea that a part of a simple body is equal to the whole in essence?

B) The second counterexample

Key Takeaway
The spherical shape is not necessary for simple bodies; non-uniform effects are possible, as shown by astronomical and logical counterexamples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What assumption do you hold that you have not tested against real counterexamples? Commit to finding one such case this week.
?
The simplicity of the celestial sphere does not force uniformity of effect. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes expect that because your intentions are pure (simple), your actions should be uniformly good? How do you handle the non-uniformity of your own outcomes—do you accept it as part of Allah's design, or does it cause you despair? What would it mean to trust that Allah's wisdom allows for different effects from a single cause?
?
The author's fourth counterexample shows that if every part of a simple body were spherical, gaps would appear. Think about your own character: are there 'gaps'—inconsistencies or areas of neglect—that you have ignored because you assume your overall nature should produce uniform behavior? What specific gap will you work to fill starting today?

The Self-Contradiction of Sphere and Circle
Argues that affirming sphere/circle necessitates contradicting the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the affirmation of spheres and circles leads to a logical contradiction that forces the denial of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Affirming sphere/circle leads to both affirming and denying the indivisible atom.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a decisive argument that the concepts of sphere and circle cannot coexist with the indivisible atom (jawhar al-fard). The author demonstrates that if you accept spheres and circles, you are logically compelled to both affirm and deny the indivisible atom—an impossibility. This lesson unpacks that argument and explains why it concludes that spheres and circles must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first a claim of established proof, then the core argument, then an alternative formulation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the contradiction
Find where the author states that affirming sphere/circle necessitates both affirmation and denial of the indivisible atom. This is the key point.
3
Understand the supposition argument
The author presents a conditional: if sphere exists, rotating it on a surface produces a line of juxtaposed points, but that composition makes the sphere impossible. Trace this logic.
This is the alternative formulation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the statement of sphere/circle is false and impossible. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the nature of physical space?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقررنا هذا الدليل، ودفعنا الشكوك والأسئلة عنه، دفعاً لا يبقى للعاقل مرية في صحة هذه الحجة. وأما أن القول بالكرة والدائرة يوجب نفي الجوهر الفرد. فلأنا بينا البرهان القاطع أن مع القول بالجوهر الفرد لا يمكن إثبات الدائرة. ومع إثبات الدائرة، لا يمكن إثبات الجوهر الفرد. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن القول بالكرة والدائرة، يوجب القول بإثبات الجوهر الفرد، ويوجب القول بنفيه. وما كان كذلك، كان باطلاً. فكان القول بإثبات الكرة والدائرة باطلاً. ويمكن أيضاً أن يعبر من هذا الكلام بعبارة أخرى. فيقال: القول بالكرة والدائرة يقضي ثبوته إلى نفيه. وما كان كذلك، كان ثبوته باطلاً محالاً. وإنما قلنا: إنه يفضي ثبوته إلى نفيه. وذلك لأنه لو ثبت بالكرة، لكنا إذا فرضنا استدارتها على السطح، فإنه يلزم تركب ذلك الخط من النقط المتشافعة على ما بيناه في دليل الكرة - لكن لو ثبت كون الكرة مركبة من النقط المتشافعة، لوجب أن يمتنع وجود الكرة، لما بينا أن تأليف الكرة والدائرة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ: قول باطل محال. فثبت: أن القول بالكرة والدائرة، يفضي ثبوته إلى نفيه، فوجب أن يكون القول به باطلاً.
And we established this proof, and we repelled the doubts and questions about it, a repelling such that no rational person remains in doubt about the correctness of this argument. And as for that the statement of the sphere and circle necessitates the denial of the indivisible atom. For we have shown the decisive proof that with the statement of the indivisible atom, it is not possible to establish the circle. And with the establishment of the circle, it is not possible to establish the indivisible atom. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the statement of the sphere and circle necessitates the statement of affirming the indivisible atom, and necessitates the statement of denying it. And whatever is like that, is false. So the statement of affirming the sphere and circle is false. And it is also possible to express this speech in another expression. So it is said: The statement of the sphere and circle, its affirmation leads to its denial. And whatever is like that, its affirmation is false and impossible. And we only say: It leads its affirmation to its denial. And that is because if the sphere were affirmed, then if we suppose its rotation on the surface, then the composition of that line from juxtaposed points follows, as we have shown in the proof of the sphere. But if the sphere is composed of juxtaposed points, then the existence of the sphere must be impossible, because we have shown that the composition of the sphere and circle from indivisible parts is a false and impossible statement. So it is established: that the statement of the sphere and circle, its affirmation leads to its denial, so the statement of it must be false.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the affirmation of spheres and circles leads to a contradiction regarding the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). It begins by asserting that the proof has been firmly established, removing all doubt. The core argument: the claim of sphere/circle both requires and denies the indivisible atom. First, if one affirms the indivisible atom, a circle cannot be constructed because a circle requires smooth continuity, which atoms cannot provide. Conversely, if one affirms the circle, then the existence of indivisible atoms is impossible because a circle implies infinite divisibility. Thus the claim entails both affirmation and denial of the atom, making it self-contradictory and false. The passage then offers an alternative formulation: the affirmation of sphere/circle leads to its own denial. This is explained: if a sphere exists, rotating it on a surface produces a line of juxtaposed points (indivisible parts); but if the sphere is composed of such indivisible parts, the sphere itself cannot exist, as composition from indivisibles is impossible. Hence the claim collapses into absurdity.

الكرة
الدائرة
النقط المتشافعة
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is about the physical existence of spheres, not about the indivisible atom.
Correction: The argument actually uses the contradiction to prove that the indivisible atom cannot be reconciled with spheres/circles, thus undermining a key physical concept.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The affirmation of spheres and circles is self-contradictory because it both requires and rejects the indivisible atom, proving its impossibility.

?

?
The proof relies on pure reason to dismiss a seemingly intuitive concept (sphere/circle). When your own reasoning leads you to a conclusion that challenges common sense or popular opinion, do you trust it or hesitate? What does this teach you about the role of intellect in faith?
?
The argument forces a choice: accept the indivisible atom or reject spheres/circles. Are you willing to accept uncomfortable truths that upend your everyday assumptions? In what area of your life do you need to courageously abandon a false premise?

Finite Orbits Prove the Indivisible Atom
Shows that rotating sphere's orbits must be finite, leading to the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rotation of a sphere and the necessary properties of its orbits lead to the conclusion that the number of orbits is finite, which in turn proves the existence of the indivisible atom and refutes the reality of spheres and circles.

Core Idea
Each orbit's unique necessary property forces finitude, proving the indivisible atom.

This lesson presents the third argument from the Kalam tradition against the infinite divisibility of matter. By examining a rotating sphere, the argument shows that the orbits must be finite because each orbit has a unique necessary property. This finitude implies that matter is composed of indivisible atoms, not continuous spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument begins with a rotating sphere and its equator.
2
Identify the key components
Find the equator (منطقة), the parallel orbits (مدارات متوازية), and the observation that orbits closer to the equator are wider and faster.
This sets up the physical model for the argument.
3
Understand the property argument
Each orbit has a unique and necessary property (speed and position) that belongs only to it and cannot be transferred. This shows that each distinct orbit must actually exist.
This is a logical necessity, not an empirical observation.
4
Follow the conclusion
If orbits were infinite, the sphere would have infinite actual parts, which is impossible. Therefore orbits are finite, implying indivisible atoms and the falsehood of continuous spheres.
The argument is a proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إنا بينا: أن الكرة إذا استدارت، فإنه يفترض منطقتها. وهي أوسع الدوائر، وأسرعها حركة. ثم يرتسم هناك مدارات متوازية، وموازية للمنطقة. وكل ما كان منها أقرب إلى المنطقة، فهو أوسع وأسرع حركة مما كان أبعد منها. وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: كل واحد من تلك المدارات مختص بخاصية واجبة الحصول فيه وممتنعة الحصول في غيره، لأن قبوله تلك الحركة الموصوفة بذلك القدر المعين من السرعة والبطء: مختص بذلك المدار، وواجب الحصول فيه، وممتنع الحصول لغيره. وأيضاً: ذلك المدار المعين من التتابع: مختص بذلك المدار، بمعنى أنه واجب الحصول فيه، وممتنع الحصول في غيره. وإذا ثبت هذا، فقد ظهر أن كل واحد من المدارات التي يمكن فرضها فيه، فإنه مختص بخاصية معينة. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كانت المدارات الممكنة موجودة فيه بالفعل. فلو كانت تلك المدارات غير متناهية، لزم أن الكرة المستديرة مركبة من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل. وذلك محال. فثبت: أن المدارات الممكنة فيه: متناهية. وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بالجوهر الفرد: حقاً. وإذا ثبت هذا، كان القول بالكرة والدائرة: باطلاً قطعاً على ما ثبت تقريره.
The third proof: We have shown: that when the sphere rotates, its equator is assumed. And it is the widest of the circles and the fastest in motion. Then there are traced there parallel orbits, parallel to the equator. And every one of them that is closer to the equator is wider and faster in motion than that which is farther from it. And when this is established, we say: Each one of those orbits is characterized by a property necessarily obtaining in it and impossible to obtain in another, because its acceptance of that motion described by that specific amount of speed and slowness is specific to that orbit, and necessarily obtains in it, and is impossible to obtain in another. And also: that specific orbit from the sequence is specific to that orbit, meaning that it necessarily obtains in it and is impossible to obtain in another. And when this is established, it becomes apparent that every one of the orbits that can be assumed in it is characterized by a specific property. And when this is the case, the possible orbits exist in it actually. So if those orbits were infinite, it would necessitate that the spherical sphere is composed of actually infinite parts. And that is impossible. So it is established: that the possible orbits in it are finite. And when this is established, the statement of the indivisible atom is true. And when this is established, the statement of the sphere and circle is absolutely false, according to what has been established in its proof.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the reality of spheres/circles and in favor of the indivisible atom. It uses the rotation of a sphere: the equator (منطقة) is the widest and fastest circle, and parallel orbits (مدارات) trace around it. Orbits closer to the equator are wider and faster. Each orbit has unique properties (speed, position) that are necessary for it and impossible for others. Therefore all possible orbits must actually exist in the sphere. If there were infinitely many possible orbits, the sphere would contain infinitely many actual parts, which is impossible. Hence the number of orbits is finite. From this finitude, the existence of indivisible atoms (الجوهر الفرد) is derived, because finite parts require ultimate indivisible units. Consequently, the belief in spheres and circles—which imply infinite divisibility—is false.

سرعة
بطء
غير متناهية
متناهية
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on empirical astronomy or physical measurements.
Correction: The argument is a logical proof based on necessary properties, not empirical observation. It uses the concept of unique necessary properties to deduce finitude regardless of actual measurement.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?
If every orbit's property is necessary and fixed by its position, what does that teach you about Allah's precise decree in your life? Are you accepting His placement of you in your current circumstances, or are you constantly wishing for a different 'orbit'? What would it mean to fully embrace your current situation as divinely ordained?

The Hollow Sphere Argument for Finite Divisibility
Uses hollow sphere's thickness to argue for finite divisibility and the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinct surfaces of a hollow sphere demonstrate that physical bodies must be composed of finite, indivisible parts.

Core Idea
A hollow sphere's opposite curvatures force its thickness to divide finitely, proving the atom.

The fourth argument uses a clever geometrical example to show that infinite divisibility leads to an absurdity. By examining a hollow sphere—like the celestial spheres—we see that its two surfaces have opposite curvatures, making it impossible for the thickness to be infinitely divisible. This argument is central to the kalam defense of atomism.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the hollow sphere's properties
Note that a hollow sphere has an outer convex surface and an inner concave surface. These two surfaces have distinct attributes that cannot be interchanged.
The outer surface is larger than the inner surface.
2
Understand the division of thickness
Because the two surfaces are different, the thickness between them must separate into two halves. Continue dividing each half similarly; if infinite division were possible, the sphere would consist of actually infinite parts.
Infinite regress is avoided by halting at finite divisibility.
3
Conclude the existence of the atom
Since actually infinite parts are impossible, the thickness must be finitely divisible. This implies that there exist indivisible parts (al-jawhar al-fard), confirming atomism.
The argument also refutes the notion of infinitely divisible spheres and circles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إن كل كرة مجوفة مثل: الأفلاك. فإن سطحها الخارج أعظم من الداخل. وأيضاً: سطحها الخارج محدب، وسطحها الباطن مقعر. وإذا كان كذلك، فقد اختص كل واحد من هذين السطحين بصفات، يمتنع ثبوتها في الآخر. فوجب أن ينفصل ثخن ذلك الفلك إلى نصفين، ثم الحال في كل واحد من نصفيه كما سبق. فلو كان ثخنه قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية، لزم كونه مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل. وذلك باطل. فوجب أن يكون ثخنه قابلاً لانقسامات متناهية. فيكون القول بالجوهر الفرد حقاً. فيكون القول بإثبات الكرة والدائرة باطلاً على ما تقدم بيانه.
The fourth proof: Every hollow sphere such as the celestial spheres. Its outer surface is greater than the inner. Also: its outer surface is convex, and its inner surface is concave. And when this is so, each of these two surfaces is characterized by attributes whose existence in the other is impossible. So the thickness of that sphere must separate into two halves, then the situation in each of its halves is as previously. So if its thickness were divisible into infinite divisions, it would necessitate that it is composed of actually infinite parts. And that is false. So its thickness must be divisible into finite divisions. Thus the statement of the indivisible atom is true. Thus the statement of affirming the sphere and circle is false, according to what has been previously explained.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, using hollow spheres (such as celestial spheres) to prove the indivisible atom. A hollow sphere has two surfaces: an outer convex surface and an inner concave surface, each with distinct attributes that cannot belong to the other. The thickness (ثخن) between them must be divisible into two halves. If this thickness were infinitely divisible, the sphere would consist of infinitely many actual parts, which is absurd. Therefore, the thickness is finitely divisible, implying the existence of indivisible parts (الجوهر الفرد). Consequently, the belief in spheres and circles—which imply infinite divisibility—is false.

Key Terms
كرة مجوفة
سطح خارج
سطح باطن
محدب
مقعر
ثخن
انقسامات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only applies to celestial spheres.
Correction: The argument uses celestial spheres as an example, but the reasoning is general: any hollow sphere (or any body with two distinct surfaces) would exhibit the same property. The conclusion about finite divisibility applies to all physical bodies.

Checkpoint

B) A hollow sphere
C) A circle
D) A line

Key Takeaway
The hollow sphere argument shows that physical bodies are composed of finite indivisible parts because infinite divisibility would lead to actual infinite parts, which is impossible.

The continuous geometric sphere is a false abstraction when applied to physical reality. The Fourth Proof: Spherical Shells and the Indivisible Atom
The author presents the fourth proof from spherical shells for the necessity of the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from spherical shells leads to the necessity of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Infinite division of a sphere's thickness leads to absurdity, so atoms must exist.

In this passage, the author presents a fourth proof for the existence of indivisible atoms (jawhar al-fard). By analyzing the properties of a hollow sphere—such as a celestial sphere—he demonstrates that its thickness cannot be infinitely divisible, because that would require an actual infinity of parts. This argument serves as a key piece of evidence in the defense of atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author builds the argument step by step.
The proof uses the properties of a hollow sphere.
2
Identify the properties of the sphere
The outer surface is larger and convex; the inner surface is smaller and concave. These are opposite attributes.
3
Follow the reasoning to separation
Because the two surfaces have incompatible attributes, the thickness must separate into two halves. This leads to a regress.
Each half would face the same situation.
4
Understand the conclusion about division
If infinite division were possible, the sphere would contain actually infinite parts, which is impossible. Therefore division is finite.
This proves the need for indivisible atoms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن كل كرة مجوفة مثل : الأفلاك . فإن سطحها الخارج أعظم من الداخل . وأيضاً : سطحها الخارج محدب ، وسطحها الباطن مقعر . وإذا كان كذلك ، فقد اختص كل واحد من هذين السطحين بصفات ، يمتنع ثبوتها في الآخر. فوجب أن ينفصل ثخن ذلك الفلك إلى نصفين ، ثم الحال في كل واحد من نصفيه كما سبق . فلو كان ثخنه قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية ، لزم كونه مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل . وذلك باطل . فوجب أن يكون ثخنه قابلاً لانقسامات متناهية . فيكون القول بالجوهر الفرد . حقاً . فيكون القول بإثبات الكرة والدائرة : باطلا . على ما تقدم بيانه وأما الحجة المبنية على قولهم : شكل الجوهر الفرد إما أن يكون هو الكرة أو المضلع ، فنقول : الحق : أن الجوهر الفرد لا كرة ولا مضلع . لأن هذا إنما يعقل فيما يكون مؤلفاً من الجوانب والأبعاض . والجوهر الفرد ليس كذلك . فلا يمكن وصفه بشيء من هذه الصفات .
The fourth proof: Every hollow sphere, such as the celestial spheres, has its outer surface greater than its inner. Also, its outer surface is convex and its inner surface is concave. And if this is so, then each of these two surfaces has become distinguished by attributes whose existence in the other is impossible. So it is necessary that the thickness of that celestial sphere separates into two halves, and the situation with each of its halves is as before. So if its thickness were susceptible to infinite divisions, then it would necessarily be composed of actually infinite parts. And that is false. So it is necessary that its thickness be susceptible to finite divisions. Thus the assertion of the indivisible atom (jawhar al-fard) is true. Thus the assertion of affirming the sphere and the circle is false, according to what has preceded in its elaboration. As for the argument based on their saying: 'The shape of the indivisible atom is either a sphere or a polygon,' we say: The truth is that the indivisible atom is neither a sphere nor a polygon. Because this is only understood for something composed of sides and parts. And the indivisible atom is not like that. So it cannot be described by any of these attributes.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct parts: a proof from spherical shells for the indivisible atom, and a refutation of the claim that the atom must have a shape. The first part is a proof (al-ḥujja al-rābiʿa). The author begins by considering any hollow sphere, like the celestial spheres. He notes that the outer and inner surfaces differ—the outer is larger and convex, the inner is smaller and concave. Since they have opposite attributes (convex vs. concave, larger vs. smaller), the thickness between them must be separable into two halves. This reasoning leads to infinite regress: if the thickness could be divided infinitely, it would actually be composed of infinite parts, which is absurd. Therefore the thickness must be finitely divisible, meaning matter is composed of indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard). Consequently, the reality of a continuous sphere or circle is false. The second part addresses an objection: opponents claim the atom must be either a sphere or a polygon. The author denies this, arguing that shape is only conceivable for something composed of sides and parts. The indivisible atom has no parts, so it cannot have a shape. Thus the atom is neither sphere nor polygon.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الكرة
انقسامات غير متناهية
انقسامات متناهية
الأفلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that spheres exist as continuous entities.
Correction: The author actually uses spheres as a representative example to show that continuity leads to absurdity, thereby refuting their real existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that the thickness of a hollow sphere must be finitely divisible?
A) Because the outer and inner surfaces have the same attributes, requiring equal division.
B) Because infinite division would lead to an actually infinite number of parts, which is impossible.
C) Because the celestial spheres are composed of a single indivisible substance.
D) Because the atom must be a sphere or polygon.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the indivisible atom follows from the impossibility of actual infinite parts in a finite thickness.

The Shape of the Indivisible Atom: Neither Sphere nor Polygon
The author refutes the claim that the indivisible atom must be spherical or polygonal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the indivisible atom cannot be described as having a geometric shape like a sphere or polygon.

Core Idea
The atom has no parts, so it cannot have shape.

Having established the existence of the indivisible atom, the author now addresses a common objection: that the atom must have a shape, either spherical or polygonal. He refutes this by arguing that shape only applies to composite things with sides and parts. Since the atom is indivisible, it lacks such composition, and thus cannot be meaningfully described as sphere or polygon.

The fourth proof: Every hollow sphere, such as the celestial spheres, has its outer surface greater than its inner. Also, its outer surface is convex and its inner surface is concave. First Proof from Triangles Refuting the Indivisible Atom
The author cites Euclid's proof that a line of two parts can form an equilateral triangle requiring division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's triangle construction forces division and thereby refutes the concept of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Geometry forces division where atomism claims indivisibility.

This lesson presents the first geometric argument against the indivisible atom. It uses Euclid's classic construction of an equilateral triangle on a line segment to show that if the line were composed of a finite number of indivisible parts, constructing the triangle would necessarily require dividing those parts, contradicting their indivisibility. The proof is concise but powerful, relying on the placement of a new part on the connector of two existing parts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This proof is part of a broader refutation of atomism using geometry. The author explicitly cites Euclid. Recognize that the argument assumes the reader is familiar with geometric constructions.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical flow: statement of the type of proof, citation of Euclid's theorem, application to a line of two parts, and the crucial step about the connector.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the logical conclusion is clear.
3
Analyze the crucial step
Why does placing a part on the connector of two parts force division? Visualize the equilateral triangle: the apex must connect to the endpoints of the base. If the base is two indivisible parts, the apex cannot simply sit on a point; it requires connectors that span the parts, implying further division.
4
Connect to atomism
The proof shows that assuming indivisible parts leads to a contradiction in geometric construction. Therefore, the notion of the indivisible atom is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في ذكر الدلائل الدالة على نفي الجوهر الفرد المبنية على المثلثات والمربعات والكلام فيه مرتب على نوعين : النوع الأول : ما يتعلق بالمثلثات . وفيه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : بين أقليدس أن كل خط متناهي الطرفين فإنه يمكننا أن نعمل عليه مثلثاً متساوي الأضلاع فالخط المركب من الجزءين يمكن أن يعمل عليه مثلث متساوي الأضلاع ولا يتم ذلك إلا بأن نضع جزءاً على متصل الجزءين وذلك يوجب انقسام الأجزاء . واعلم : أنا متى وضعنا جزءاً على متصل جزءين كان كل واحد من الأجزاء الثلاثة واقعاً على متصل الجزءين .
Concerning the mention of proofs indicating the negation of the indivisible atom (jawhar al-fard) based on triangles and squares. And the discussion on it is arranged into two types: The first type: what pertains to triangles. And it contains aspects. The first proof: Euclid demonstrated that on every finite straight line we can construct an equilateral triangle. Commentary
This passage introduces a new section of proofs that aim to refute the indivisible atom using geometry (triangles and squares). The author states the discussion is divided into two types; the first concerns triangles. Under this, he presents the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā), citing Euclid. The proof: given a finite straight line composed of two indivisible parts (assuming atomism), an equilateral triangle can be constructed on that line. To construct the triangle, one must place a third part (the apex) such that it connects with the line's two parts via connectors (the sides). But placing one part on the connector of two parts forces each of the three parts to lie on a connector that spans two parts, implying that the parts must divide further to accommodate the geometry. Thus the assumption that the line is composed of two indivisible parts leads to contradiction, refuting atomism. The passage ends abruptly after describing this placement.

First geometrical proof: constructing an equilateral triangle
Introduces Euclid-based proof against atomism using triangle construction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's construction of an equilateral triangle on a line of two indivisible parts leads to a contradiction, refuting atomism.

Core Idea
A triangle cannot be built from indivisible parts without dividing them.

This lesson introduces the first geometrical argument used by Muslim theologians to disprove atomism. By applying a simple Euclidean construction to a line supposedly composed of two atoms, the argument reveals a logical impossibility. Let us follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recall Euclid's Theorem
Remember that Euclid proved that on any finite straight line, one can construct an equilateral triangle. This is a foundational geometric fact.
This theorem is the starting point of the proof.
2
Apply to the Atomist Line
Consider a line composed of two indivisible parts (atoms). According to atomism, such a line exists. Apply Euclid's construction to this line.
The line is assumed to have two parts meeting at a point.
3
Identify the Contradiction
To construct the triangle, we must place one part on the connector of the two parts. This act forces the indivisible parts to be divided, contradicting their indivisibility.
The 'connector' is the point where the two parts meet.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
Since the construction is impossible under atomism, atomism must be false. This is a powerful geometric refutation.
The proof does not assume atoms exist; it shows they cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر الدلائل الحالة على نفي الجوهر المقود المبنية على النمل بالمثلثات والمربعات والكلام فيه مرتب على نوعين : النوع الأول : ما يتعلق بالمثلثات . وفيه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : بين ( أقليدس ) أن كل خط متناهي الطرفين ، فإنه يمكننا أن نعمل عليه مثلثاً متساوي الأضلاع فالخط المركب من الجزءين يمكن أن يعمل عليه مثلث متساوي الأضلاع ولا يتم ذلك ، إلا بأن نضع جزءاً على متصل الجزءين وذلك يوجب انقسام الأجزاء . واعلم : أنا متى وضعنا جزءاً على متصل جزءين . كان كل واحد من الأجزاء الثلاثة واقعاً على متصل الجزءين .

Commentary
This passage introduces the geometrical proofs used to refute atomism (the view that bodies are composed of indivisible parts). The author states that the proofs are divided into two types: those involving triangles and those involving squares. The first type concerns triangles and contains several arguments. The first argument invokes Euclid's geometry: given any finite line, one can construct an equilateral triangle on it. Applying this to a line composed of two assumed indivisible parts leads to a contradiction because to form the triangle, one must place a part on the connecting point of the two parts, which forces the assumed indivisible parts to be divided. The author adds an explanatory note: when we place a part on the connector of two parts, each of the three parts (presumably the three sides?) falls on the connector of two parts, reinforcing the necessity of division.

Second proof: successive triangles and the objection
Explains the second proof using successive triangles and includes an unanswered objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against atomism using successive triangles and the initial objection that the argument assumes divisibility.

Core Idea
Triangles force division of assumed indivisible parts.

This passage presents the second argument against the theory that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms. It constructs a chain of triangles on lines of increasing length to show that triangles cannot exist without division of parts. The argument then records an objection that the key premise has been discussed before, leaving the answer for later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The passage ends with an objection, but the answer is not given here.

Understand that each side of a triangle is composed of two indivisible parts. When placed on a longer line, each part must fall on the connector of two parts, forcing division.
This directly challenges the assumption of indivisibility.

The objector challenges the premise that 'falling on a connector forces division' by stating it has been discussed before. Consider what that discussion might entail.
The answer is not provided in this lesson.

الحجة الثانية : إن جميع المثلثات لا تتم إلا عند وقوع كل واحد من الأجزاء المفترضة فيها ، على متصل الجزءين. وذلك لأن كل واحد من أضلاع المثلث المذكور ، يكون مركبا من جزءين . فإذا وضعنا ذلك المثلث على خط آخر مركب من ثلاثة أجزاء ، بحيث يقع كل واحد من الجزءين اللذين عنها حصل ضلع المثلث الأول ، على متصل جزءين من الأجزاء الثلاثة ، التي حصلت في هذا الخط . فهذا هو المثلث الثاني . ثم إذا وضعنا ذلك المثلث على خط آخر ، مركب من أربعة أجزاء بالطريق الأول ، فهو المثلث الثالث وقس عليه سائر المراتب التي لا نهاية لها. فثبت : بما ذكرنا : أنا إذا قلنا : الجسم مركب من أجزاء لا تتجزأ . فإن شيئاً من المثلثات لا يوجد إلا بسبب وقوع جزء متصل الجزءين . وذلك يوجب القسمة . ولقائل أن يقول : قولكم ( الجزء إذا وقع على متصل الجزءين ، لزم حصول القسمة ، وقد سبق الكلام عليه
The second argument: All triangles are not completed except when each of the assumed parts in them falls on the connector of two parts. This is because each side of the mentioned triangle is composed of two parts. So when we place that triangle upon another line composed of three parts, such that each of the two parts from which the first triangle's side resulted falls on the connector of two parts of the three parts that are in this line—this is the second triangle. Then when we place that triangle upon another line composed of four parts in the first way, it is the third triangle. And analogously for the remaining orders that have no end. So it is established by what we have mentioned: if we say that the body is composed of indivisible parts, then none of the triangles exists except because of the falling of a part upon the connector of two parts, and that necessitates division. And someone may object: Your saying 'when a part falls on the connector of two parts, division necessarily occurs'—the discussion has preceded concerning it.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against atomism, which uses a chain of triangles built on successively longer lines. The argument is that for a triangle to exist, each of its sides (composed of two assumed indivisible parts) must be placed on a line such that each part falls on the connector of two parts of the base line. This forces division of the parts. The author shows that if a body were composed of indivisible parts, no triangle could exist without this division, which contradicts the assumption of indivisibility. The passage then records an objection: someone may say that the claim 'when a part falls on the connector of two parts, division necessarily occurs' has been discussed before. Notably, the answer to this objection is not included in the window; the text moves directly to the third proof.

Third Proof: Numerical Sides and Triangle Types
Demonstrates through numerical examples that triangles become acute, forcing infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sequence of triangle types from obtuse to increasingly acute demonstrates the necessity of infinite divisibility, thereby refuting atomism.

Core Idea
Triangles can become arbitrarily acute, proving matter is infinitely divisible.

This lesson presents the third argument against atomism, which uses a simple numerical progression of side lengths to show that triangles can vary continuously from obtuse to right to acute angles. By observing that the angle becomes ever sharper with each successive set of integer sides, the argument demonstrates that the parts of a triangle must be divisible without limit. This challenges the concept of indivisible atoms (jawhar) that would not allow such continuous variation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the progression of side lengths and the resulting triangle types.
The passage uses specific numerical examples to illustrate the point.
2
Identify the sequence of triangles
List the side-length sets in order: (1,2,3) impossible, (2,3,4) obtuse, (3,4,5) right, (4,5,6) acute, (5,6,7) more acute. Recognize the pattern.
The squares are used to classify triangles by the Pythagorean relationship.
3
Understand the geometric principle
For a triangle, the largest angle is opposite the longest side. Compare the square of the longest side to the sum of squares of the other two to classify as obtuse, right, or acute.
This is a standard geometric test.
4
Connect to infinite divisibility
The continuous sharpening of the angle implies that the sides can be divided ever more finely, contradicting atomism which posits a finite smallest unit.
The proof argues that if atoms were indivisible, such continuous variation would be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : قالوا : إذا أردنا أن نعمل مثلثاً من الواحد والاثنين والثلاثة ، امتنع . وإلا لزم أن يكون مجموع الضلعين مساوياً للضلع الثالث . وهو محال : أما إذا أردنا أن نعمل مثلثاً من الاثنين والثلاثة والأربعة ، حصل منه مثلث منفرج الزواية . لأن مربع الأربعة، أزيد من مجموع مربع الاثنين الثلاثة . وبهذا البيان . يظهر أن المثلث الذي يكون منفرج الزواية ، أقدم أنواع المثلثات. أما إذا أردنا أن نعمل مثلثاً من الثلاثة والأربعة والخمسة . فإنه يحصل منه مثلث قائم الزاوية ، لأن مربع الخمسة يساوي مجموع مربع الأربعة . ومربع الثلاثة . أما إذا أردنا عمله من الأربعة والخمسة والستة ، حصل منه مثلث حاد الزوايا . لأن مربع الخمسة والستة أقل من مجموع مربع الأربعة . إلا أن هذا المثلث - وإن كان حاد الزوايا - إلا أنه مختلف الزوايا . وأعظمها هي الزاوية التي بوترها الضلع الأعظم - وهو الستة . وأما إذا أردنا عمل المثلث من الخمسة والستة والسبعة ، حصل أيضاً مثلث حاد الزوايا . إلا أن الزاوية التي كان بوترها الضلع الأعظم من المثلث السابق ، أعظم من الزاوية التي بوترها الضلع الأعظم من هذا المثلث . فإنا بينا : أن أول تلك الزوايا هي المنفرجة ثم القائمة ثم الحادة. وعلى هذا الطريق فلا يزال يزداد ضيق تلك الزوايا .
The third argument: They said: If we wish to construct a triangle from one, two, and three, it is impossible—otherwise the sum of the two sides would equal the third side, which is absurd. But if we wish to construct a triangle from two, three, and four, an obtuse-angled triangle results, because the square of four is greater than the sum of the squares of two and three. With this explanation, it appears that the obtuse-angled triangle is the first type of triangles. If we wish to construct a triangle from three, four, and five, a right-angled triangle results, because the square of five equals the sum of the squares of four and three. If we wish to construct it from four, five, and six, an acute-angled triangle results, because the square of five and six is less than the sum of the squares of four. However, this triangle—although acute-angled—is scalene, and its largest angle is the one subtended by the longest side, which is six. And if we wish to construct the triangle from five, six, and seven, an acute-angled triangle also results, except that the angle subtended by the longest side of the previous triangle is greater than the angle subtended by the longest side of this triangle. For we have shown that the first of those angles is obtuse, then right, then acute. And in this way the sharpness of those angles continues to increase.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against atomism, using numerical examples of side lengths and the resulting types of triangles. The argument shows that as side lengths increase in sequential integers, the triangles transition from impossible (1,2,3) to obtuse (2,3,4) to right (3,4,5) to acute (4,5,6) and then to more acute (5,6,7). The pattern demonstrates that triangles can become arbitrarily acute, implying that the parts of the triangle (sides) must be divisible without limit. The author emphasizes that the obtuse angle is the first type, then right, then acute, and with each successive triangle the angle subtended by the longest side becomes sharper. This infinite progression suggests that if bodies were composed of indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-maqūd), such continuous variation in angles would not be possible, as the smallest change would require further division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to equilateral triangles.
Correction: The proof uses scalene triangles with integer sides, showing that even with different side lengths, the angles vary continuously.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what is the sequence of triangle types as side lengths increase from (2,3,4) to (5,6,7)?
A) Obtuse, right, acute, more acute
B) Right, obtuse, acute, more acute
C) Acute, right, obtuse, more obtuse
D) Impossible, obtuse, right, acute

Key Takeaway
The continuous sharpness of the angle in successive integer triangles shows that matter must be infinitely divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that even simple integers can generate infinite variation in angles. In your own spiritual life, do you ever feel that your good deeds are too finite or insufficient? How does this truth about infinite potential change your reliance on Allah's boundless mercy? This week, try to perform one seemingly small act of kindness, trusting that its impact can be infinitely divided into blessings.
?
This argument requires you to trust the logical coherence of the universe as created by Allah. Are there aspects of your faith that you feel are at odds with reason? How does this proof strengthen your conviction that Islam is a religion of both reason and revelation? Take a moment to express gratitude for the precision of creation that points to its Creator.
?
If matter is infinitely divisible, then every particle, no matter how small, is a sign of Allah's infinite power. When you look at a single atom, do you see it as a finite unit, or as a testament to the infinite complexity that Allah has placed in creation? How does this realization change your awe of Him? Spend five minutes today contemplating one aspect of nature that displays this precision.
